Heredity: Teaching Companion

Classical and molecular genetics for Science Olympiad, grades 10 to 11. Technical, exam-oriented, and built to be walked one concept at a time.

Foundations: molecules and the cell Mendelian genetics Beyond Mendel Molecular genetics Mutation and applied Bridge (not taught here)

How the big ideas connect

Heredity is one long chain of cause and effect, and the map above is that chain drawn out. Everything starts with a molecule. DNA is packed into chromosomes, chromosomes are copied and sorted by mitosis and meiosis, and the two copies of each gene you inherit are its alleles. Those five ideas are the foundation: once you know that a gene comes in versions and that meiosis hands out one version per gamete, the rest of classical genetics is just bookkeeping.

That bookkeeping is the Mendelian core. Meiosis separating alleles is the physical reason behind Mendel's law of segregation, so segregation flows straight out of meiosis. Segregation gives you the Punnett square, the Punnett square is really just probability on a grid, and running two genes at once (thanks to independent assortment in meiosis) gives you the dihybrid cross. Notice that meiosis feeds this whole layer twice: once to explain segregation, once to explain independent assortment. That is the spine of the course.

The Beyond Mendel layer is what happens when the tidy 3 to 1 ratio stops matching reality. Alleles can blend or both show up (incomplete dominance and codominance), a gene can have more than two alleles (blood types), a gene can sit on a sex chromosome (sex-linked traits), many genes can add up (polygenic traits), or one gene can mask another (epistasis). Each of these still uses the Punnett square from the core; they just change what the boxes mean or how the ratios come out.

The molecular genetics layer answers a question the classical layer never asks: what is a gene actually made of, and how does it do anything? Here DNA copies itself in replication (during the S phase of the cell cycle you met in mitosis), and the central dogma runs DNA to RNA to protein through transcription and translation, with the genetic code as the dictionary that turns codons into amino acids. This layer connects back to the foundation: replication is why chromosomes can be copied before a cell divides.

The last layer, mutation and applied genetics, is where the molecular and classical halves finally meet. A copy error in replication or a change the genetic code cannot absorb is a mutation; errors during meiosis show up as whole-chromosome problems you can read off a karyotype; and the sex-linked and probability tools from earlier let you read a pedigree. All three roads lead to the genetic disorders casebook, where a real condition is traced from its mutation to its inheritance pattern to the family tree.

Where heredity connects outward

This course teaches heredity, but heredity is a doorway to several bigger subjects it does not cover. Evolution and population genetics pick up exactly where meiosis and alleles leave off: the variation meiosis creates is the raw material natural selection acts on, and counting allele frequencies across a whole population (the Hardy-Weinberg idea) is genetics scaled up from one family to millions. Biochemistry sits underneath the molecular layer: the bonds, sugars, and protein-folding rules that this course only names are its whole subject. And the Division C event Designer Genes includes everything here plus the frontier this course only points at: CRISPR and gene editing, cloning, and alternative splicing. If a student wants more after this, those three are the natural next steps, and the purple bridges on the map show exactly which chapters open each door.

How to use this

Walk the layers in order the first time, because each one leans on the one before it. After that, jump to whatever cross, ratio, molecule, or disorder you need. The chapter list below is the table of contents; the map above is the real lesson.

1. DNA: Nucleotides and Structure

DNA is a chain of building blocks called nucleotides. Its four bases pair by one strict rule, so the two strands are exact partners.

🧬 How to read this chapter: this is a molecular chapter, so the examples use real DNA sequences and chemical parts, not a family. Later chapters (from Chapter 5 on) follow the Rivera family for crosses. Everything those chapters predict is written in the molecule you meet here.
Prerequisites: none. This is the entry point of the whole map.
This chapter: what a nucleotide is, the four bases, the sugar-phosphate backbone, and the base-pairing rule.
Unlocks: Chapter 2: Chromosomes, Chromatin and the Genome · Chapter 15: DNA Replication

Start with real molecules

Before any rules, look closely at three real pieces of DNA. Read each picture carefully. The whole chapter is just the pattern hiding inside these three cases.

Case 1 — one building block

Here is the single repeating unit of DNA, drawn once. It has three parts joined together: a phosphate group (the purple circle on the left), a five-sided sugar in the middle, and a nitrogenous base box on the right (one of A, T, G, or C). The sugar's five carbons are numbered 1' through 5', read "one prime" through "five prime."

Notice exactly where things attach, because these positions run the rest of the chapter: the phosphate sits on the 5' carbon, the base sits on the 1' carbon, and a free hydroxyl group (an oxygen plus hydrogen, written OH) hangs off the 3' carbon. That 3' OH is the open hook where the next unit joins. Chain many of these units together and you have one strand of DNA.

Case 2 — a real sequence and its exact partner

Here is a short real stretch of DNA. The left strand reads 5'-A T G C G T-3'. That leading ATG is not random: it is the exact three-letter "start" signal that real protein-coding genes begin with, which you meet again in Chapter 18: Translation. Now look at what sits directly across from each base. Every A faces a T, and every G faces a C, and no other combination ever appears.

Two more things to read straight off the picture. Count the short bonds drawn in each rung: the A-T rungs have two, the G-C rungs have three. And read the end labels: where the left strand is marked 5', the right strand is marked 3'. The two strands point in opposite directions.

Case 3 — which way you read a strand

A strand of DNA has a direction, like a one-way street. One end is the 5' end (it carries a free phosphate), and the other is the 3' end (it carries a free OH). By convention we always read and write a strand from 5' to 3'. In Case 2 the partner strand ran the opposite way: reading top to bottom on the left you go 5' to 3', while the same downward reading on the right goes 3' to 5'. Figure 3 isolates just this fact.

The pattern in all three

Three facts repeat across the pictures:

  • DNA is built from one kind of repeating unit (Case 1): phosphate plus sugar plus base. Link the units through their 5' and 3' carbons and you get a strand.
  • The two strands are exact partners (Case 2): A always pairs with T, G always pairs with C. Know one strand and you can write the other, base by base.
  • Every strand has a direction (Case 3), and the two strands run in opposite directions.

Now name them. The repeating unit is a nucleotide. The strict partnership is base pairing, and it makes the strands complementary. The opposite-direction fact is called antiparallel. The rest of this chapter states each idea precisely and explains why it holds.

The structure, precisely

DNA (deoxyribonucleic acid) is a long chain. The single repeating unit of that chain, the one you met in Figure 1, is the nucleotide. Every nucleotide is built from exactly three parts:

  • A phosphate group. A small cluster of one phosphorus atom and oxygen atoms. It carries a negative charge and links one nucleotide to the next.
  • A deoxyribose sugar. A five-carbon sugar. "Deoxy" means it is missing one oxygen compared with the sugar ribose used in RNA.
  • A nitrogenous base. A ring-shaped molecule containing nitrogen. This is the part that carries the information. There are four kinds in DNA.

Nucleotide versus nucleoside. These two words differ by one part. A nucleoside is just the sugar plus the base (no phosphate). Add a phosphate group and you have a nucleotide. In short, nucleotide = nucleoside + phosphate. One familiar example is ATP (adenosine triphosphate): "adenosine" is the adenine nucleoside, and "triphosphate" means three phosphate groups are attached.

The four bases: purines and pyrimidines

The four DNA bases split into two families by the number of rings in the base:

  • Purines have two fused rings: adenine (A) and guanine (G).
  • Pyrimidines have one ring: cytosine (C) and thymine (T). In RNA, uracil (U) replaces thymine and is also a pyrimidine.

Two mnemonics keep them straight:

  • "Pure As Gold" for the purines: A and G.
  • "CUT the pie" for the pyrimidines: C, U, and T.

A memory hook: "purine" is the shorter word, yet a purine has more rings (two).

Numbering the carbons, and the backbone

The five carbons of the sugar are numbered with a mark called a prime, written 1' through 5' and read "one prime" through "five prime." As Figure 1 showed, three attachment points matter most:

  • The phosphate group attaches to the 5' carbon.
  • The nitrogenous base attaches to the 1' carbon.
  • A free hydroxyl group (an oxygen plus hydrogen, written OH) sits on the 3' carbon. This is where the next nucleotide joins.

Because each phosphate links the 5' carbon of one sugar to the 3' carbon of the next, the sugars and phosphates form a repeating chain called the sugar-phosphate backbone. Picture DNA as a twisted ladder: the two backbones are the two sides of the ladder, and the bases point inward as the rungs.

Two strands, antiparallel

DNA is usually double-stranded: two backbones wound around each other into the famous double helix (a shape like a twisted ladder). The two strands are antiparallel, which means they run in opposite directions, exactly as Figure 3 showed. Reading down one strand you go from its 5' end to its 3' end; its partner runs the other way, 3' to 5'. Marking the ends 5' and 3' is how we state a strand's direction.

The base-pairing rule

The rungs of the ladder are base pairs, and the bases do not pair at random. There is one strict rule, held together by hydrogen bonds (weak attractions between the bases):

A pairs only with T (2 hydrogen bonds). G pairs only with C (3 hydrogen bonds).

You never see A across from G or C, and you never see T across from G or C. Because G-C pairs share three hydrogen bonds while A-T pairs share only two, a stretch of DNA rich in G and C is harder to pull apart than a stretch rich in A and T. In RNA the rule shifts slightly: uracil replaces thymine, so A pairs with U.

This one-to-one rule has a powerful consequence. If you know one strand, you can write its partner exactly, base by base (this is what you did by eye in Case 2). The two strands are complementary. That built-in backup is what lets a cell copy its DNA faithfully, the story of Chapter 15: DNA Replication.

Aha: a helpful reminder is that the two "curvy" letters, G and C, go together (the pair with three bonds). A and T are the other pair (two bonds).

Chargaff's rule

The pairing rule leaves a fingerprint on the whole molecule. Since every A on one strand faces a T on the other, and every G faces a C, the amounts must balance across double-stranded DNA. This is Chargaff's rule:

\[ \%A = \%T \qquad \text{and} \qquad \%G = \%C. \]

All four percentages still add up to 100%. So a single measured percentage lets you work out the other three (you will practice this below). If a sample breaks Chargaff's rule, that is a clue the DNA may be single-stranded rather than double-stranded.

Who worked it out

James Watson and Francis Crick, together with Maurice Wilkins, are credited with describing the double-helix structure of DNA in 1953. Their model relied heavily on Rosalind Franklin's X-ray diffraction images. X-ray diffraction is a method that shines X-rays through a molecule and reads the scattered pattern to reveal its shape. Franklin's image (often called Photo 51) showed the helix clearly, and it was central evidence for the structure.

Worked examples

Example 1 (nucleotide anatomy). Look at the monomer in Figure 1. Name its three parts. Is it a nucleotide or a nucleoside? Which carbon carries the phosphate, and which carries the base?

The three parts are a phosphate group, a deoxyribose sugar, and a nitrogenous base. Because a phosphate group is present, this is a nucleotide (a nucleoside would be the sugar plus base only, with no phosphate). The phosphate attaches to the 5' carbon, and the base attaches to the 1' carbon. The free OH on the 3' carbon is where the next nucleotide would join.

Aha: the word "nucleotide" always includes the phosphate. That phosphate is the link that joins one sugar to the next, which is how the sugar-phosphate backbone gets built.

Example 2 (complementary antiparallel strand, and count the bonds). One strand of DNA reads 5'-A T G C G T-3'. Write the partner strand with its correct direction, then count the total number of hydrogen bonds holding the two strands together.

Swap each base for its one partner, using A-T and G-C. Then remember the partner runs in the opposite direction, so its 3' end lines up under the first strand's 5' end.

5'- A  T  G  C  G  T -3'
3'- T  A  C  G  C  A -5'

Now count bonds by pair type. The six pairs are A-T, T-A, G-C, C-G, G-C, T-A. That is three A-T pairs (2 bonds each) and three G-C pairs (3 bonds each): \[ (3 \times 2) + (3 \times 3) = 6 + 9 = 15 \text{ hydrogen bonds.} \]

Aha: the complement is more than "the opposite letters." It is also written in the opposite 5' to 3' direction. That is exactly what antiparallel means.

Example 3 (classify by ring count, and read the bond strength). Sort A, G, C, T into purines and pyrimidines. Then, of the segments 5'-G G C C-3' and 5'-A T A T-3', which is harder to pull apart, and why?

Ring count sorts the bases: purines (two rings) are A and G; pyrimidines (one ring) are C and T. For the two segments, the G-C-rich one is harder to separate. Each G-C pair has 3 hydrogen bonds while each A-T pair has 2, so 5'-G G C C-3' is held by \(4 \times 3 = 12\) bonds against just \(4 \times 2 = 8\) bonds for 5'-A T A T-3'.

Aha: ring count and bond count are two different facts about the same four letters. Ring count (one versus two) classifies a base as pyrimidine or purine. Bond count (2 versus 3) sets how tightly a base pair holds.
Common pitfalls:
  • Pairing the wrong bases. The only pairs are A with T and G with C. There is no A-G, A-C, T-G, or T-C rung. In RNA it is A with U, never A with T.
  • Reversing the bond counts. A-T has 2 hydrogen bonds; G-C has 3. The "curvy" letters G and C get the extra bond. Do not swap them.
  • Mixing up nucleotide and nucleoside. The only difference is the phosphate. Nucleoside = sugar + base; nucleotide = nucleoside + phosphate.
  • Ignoring direction. A complement written 5' to 3' looks reversed next to its template because the strands are antiparallel. Always mark the 5' and 3' ends so the partner is unambiguous.

Connections

  • → Chapter 2: Chromosomes, Chromatin and the Genome: this DNA molecule winds around proteins and coils up into the chromosomes that store it in the nucleus.
  • → Chapter 15: DNA Replication: because one strand fully determines the other, a cell can split the two strands and build a fresh partner for each. Complementarity is what makes copying possible.
  • → Chapter 16: RNA: Types and Structure: RNA uses the sugar ribose instead of deoxyribose and the base uracil instead of thymine, so its pairing rule is A with U.
  • → Chapter 20: Mutations: a change to even a single base can change a trait. The sickle cell allele the Riveras could carry is one such single-base change.
  • ↔ Chemistry: the pairing rule is not arbitrary. It comes from the shapes of the bases and where their hydrogen bonds can form. This is the bridge from biology to chemistry.

★ Landmark it points toward

DNA sequencing: reading the exact order of A, T, G, and C along a strand. It is the tool behind modern genomics, from tracing disease genes to comparing whole genomes. (Just meet the name for now.)

Practice

P1. Classify each of these bases as a purine or a pyrimidine: A, G, C, T, U.

AnswerPurines (two rings): A and G. Pyrimidines (one ring): C, T, and U. Mnemonics: "Pure As Gold" for purines A and G; "CUT the pie" for pyrimidines C, U, T.

P2. One strand of DNA reads 5'-G G G C A T-3'. Write its complementary partner strand with the correct direction, then count the total number of hydrogen bonds between the strands.

Answer

Swap each base for its partner and flip the direction, so the partner reads 3'-C C C G T A-5':

5'- G  G  G  C  A  T -3'
3'- C  C  C  G  T  A -5'

The pairs are G-C, G-C, G-C, C-G, A-T, T-A: four G-C pairs (3 bonds each) and two A-T pairs (2 bonds each). Total \((4 \times 3) + (2 \times 2) = 12 + 4 = 16\) hydrogen bonds.

P3. A sample of double-stranded DNA is 30% adenine. What percentage of the sample is guanine?

Answer

The calculation: by Chargaff's rule, %T = %A = 30%, so adenine and thymine together are \(30\% + 30\% = 60\%\). The remaining \(100\% - 60\% = 40\%\) is guanine plus cytosine. Since %G = %C, split that 40% evenly: %G = %C = 20%. So guanine is 20%.

Why it works: the pairing rule forces every A to face a T and every G to face a C, so the two members of each pair must be present in equal amounts across the whole molecule. That is why %A = %T and %G = %C. The four percentages describe every base in the sample, so they must total 100%. Knowing one value, plus these two equalities, pins down all four.

Pattern (conservation plus symmetry): whenever a rule forces two quantities to be equal and a fixed total to be shared, use both facts together. Match the equal pair, subtract the known pair from the total, then split the remainder between the two equal partners. This only holds for double-stranded DNA. Single-stranded DNA or RNA need not obey Chargaff's rule.

Watch / read preview before showing students

↗ Khan Academy: DNA and RNA structure ↗ Learn.Genetics (University of Utah): Tour of the Basics (What is DNA?) ↗ National Human Genome Research Institute: Talking Glossary of Genomic and Genetic Terms ↗ PDB-101 (RCSB): DNA molecule of the month

Teaching notes

Opener: write "if one strand is 5'-A T G C G T-3', what is the other?" on the board cold. Let students attempt it, then reveal two facts they need: the pairing rule (A-T, G-C) and the antiparallel twist (the partner runs the other way). That single question threads the whole chapter, and it is exactly the Case 2 sequence.

Board moves: draw one nucleotide first (phosphate, sugar, base) and label the 5', 3', and 1' carbons so "5' phosphate, 1' base" becomes visual. Then draw the ladder and write the pairing rule big: "A-T (2 bonds), G-C (3 bonds)." Point at a base and have the class call out its partner.

What to name: nucleotide, nucleoside, phosphate, deoxyribose, nitrogenous base, purine, pyrimidine, antiparallel, complementary, hydrogen bond, and Chargaff's rule. Do not bring in dominant, recessive, or alleles yet; those start in Chapter 5.

Check for understanding: give a strand like 5'-T A C G-3' and ask for the antiparallel partner (answer 3'-A T G C-5'). Ask "which is stronger, an A-T pair or a G-C pair, and why?" to catch the 2 versus 3 bond fact. Ask "nucleotide or nucleoside?" while pointing at Figure 1 with and without the phosphate.

Pacing: spend about 20 minutes on the structure and the three figures, 15 minutes on base pairing and Chargaff, and 10 minutes on practice. Save the Chargaff calculation (P3) until after the pairing rule is solid, since it is the one step that needs the rule as a tool.

2. Chromosomes, Chromatin and the Genome

How about two meters of DNA folds into a nucleus you cannot see, and how that folded DNA is organized into the 46 chromosomes of a human cell.

Prerequisites: Chapter 1: DNA Structure
This node: Chromosomes, Chromatin and the Genome
Unlocks: Chapter 3: Mitosis · Chapter 21: Karyotypes

Three pictures before the vocabulary

Before we define any terms, look at the DNA of one human cell in three ways: the whole set, a single chromosome up close, and one matched pair getting ready to divide. Every term later in this chapter is just a name for something in these three pictures.

Picture 1: the whole set. Stain a human body cell as it divides, spread its chromosomes on a slide, and sort them by size. What you get is a karyotype, the complete ordered set of that cell's chromosomes. There are 46 of them, and they fall into 23 matched pairs. Pairs 1 through 22 look the same in both sexes; these are the autosomes. The 23rd pair is the sex chromosomes: two X chromosomes in a typical female, one X and one Y in a typical male. Count them: 22 pairs plus 1 pair is 23 pairs, and \(23 \times 2 = 46\) chromosomes.

Picture 2: one chromosome, unpacked. Pull one chromosome out of that set and unravel it. Inside is a single, enormously long DNA molecule. All the DNA in one human cell is about 2 meters long, yet it has to fit inside a nucleus only about 6 micrometers across (6 millionths of a meter). It fits because it is wound and folded in stages, from the bare double helix of Chapter 1 up to the compact chromosome you can see under a microscope.

Picture 3: one pair, copied and ready to divide. Zoom in on a single pair from Picture 1, in a cell that has just copied all its DNA to prepare for division. The two members of the pair are homologous chromosomes: one came from the mother, one from the father, and they carry the same genes in the same order. Because the cell has copied its DNA, each member is now two identical sister chromatids joined at a pinch point called the centromere. So this one pair is 2 chromosomes but 4 chromatids.

The pattern. All three pictures describe the same object at different zoom levels. A human body cell holds one fixed set of 46 chromosomes in 23 pairs, so we call it diploid. Each chromosome is a single very long DNA molecule, packaged in stages so it fits. The set is paired because you inherit one member of each pair from each parent (homologous chromosomes), and when the cell copies its DNA each chromosome briefly becomes two identical sister chromatids. The rest of this chapter names each of these ideas and explains how the packaging works and why the counting is done by centromeres.

The core idea

The problem of scale. Chapter 1 showed that DNA is a long double helix, a ladder about 2 nanometers wide (a nanometer is one billionth of a meter). If you laid out all the DNA in a single human cell end to end, it would be about 2 meters long. That DNA has to fit inside a nucleus (the membrane-bound compartment that holds a cell's DNA) that is only about 6 micrometers across, which is 6 millionths of a meter. The cell solves this by winding and folding the DNA in stages.

Chromatin. Inside the nucleus, DNA does not float freely. It is wound around proteins to form chromatin, which is the complex of DNA plus its packaging proteins (and some RNA). The main packaging proteins are called histones. A short length of DNA wraps a little under two turns around a core of eight histone proteins. That DNA-plus-histone spool is a nucleosome, the basic repeating unit of chromatin. A string of nucleosomes looks like beads on a string, and this coils up further into a thicker fiber. Chromatin has four jobs: it packs loose DNA into a compact shape, it reinforces the DNA so the cell can divide, it protects the DNA from damage, and it helps control which genes are switched on.

Two states of chromatin. Not all chromatin is packed equally tightly, and how tightly it is packed controls whether its genes can be read.

  • Euchromatin is loosely packed. Because it is open, the cell's machinery can reach it, so euchromatin is the actively transcribed form (transcription is copying a gene into RNA, covered in Chapter 17). About 92% of the human genome is euchromatin.
  • Heterochromatin is tightly packed and mostly silent. It usually does structural work rather than coding for active genes. The centromeres and telomeres of chromosomes are heterochromatin. A centromere is the pinched region where a chromosome's copies stay joined; telomeres are the protective caps on the ends.

From chromatin to chromosome. For most of a cell's life the DNA stays as diffuse chromatin so its genes can be used. When a cell is about to divide, the chromatin coils and folds much more tightly. It condenses into chromosomes, the compact, individually visible packages of DNA that appear at the start of mitosis (Chapter 3). Condensing the DNA this way keeps the long strands from tangling and breaking while the cell splits them between two daughter cells.

Sister chromatids. Before a cell divides it first copies all of its DNA (this happens in S phase, covered in Chapter 3). So each chromosome that condenses is already a duplicated chromosome. It consists of two identical copies, called sister chromatids, joined together at the centromere (this is the state drawn in Panel D of the packaging figure above, and in Picture 3). Sister chromatids are exact copies of each other because one was made from the other during replication. They are held together until the cell pulls them apart, sending one copy to each daughter cell.

The genome and the chromosome set. The genome is the complete set of genetic material in a cell or organism: all of its DNA, both the genes and the non-coding regions between them. The human genome is about 3 billion base pairs. That genome is not one long strand. It is divided into separate chromosomes. A human body cell (a somatic cell, meaning any cell that is not a sperm or egg) contains 46 chromosomes, organized as 23 pairs: \[ 46 = 22 \text{ autosome pairs} + 1 \text{ sex-chromosome pair} \] An autosome is any chromosome that is not a sex chromosome. The 23rd pair is the sex chromosomes: two X chromosomes in a typical female, and one X plus one Y in a typical male. A cell with two of each chromosome is diploid, written \(2n = 46\), where \(n = 23\) is the number of chromosomes in one set.

Homologous chromosomes. The two chromosomes in a pair are called homologous chromosomes, or homologs. One came from the person's mother and one from the father. Homologous chromosomes carry the same genes in the same order along their length. At any given location, called a locus (plural loci), both homologs carry a gene for the same trait. What can differ is the allele, which is a specific version of that gene (Chapter 5). So the two homologs might carry the same allele or two different alleles at a locus. This is different from sister chromatids, which are identical.

Worked examples

Example 1. Follow the packaging ladder. Name each level of packaging in order, from the bare double helix up to the condensed chromosome, and give the one job that packaging accomplishes.

Reading the question. This asks you to put the packaging stages in the right order and to say why the cell bothers. Use the packaging ladder figure (Picture 2 above) as your guide.

  1. DNA double helix. The bare ladder from Chapter 1, about 2 nm wide.
  2. Nucleosome. A stretch of DNA wraps a little under two turns around a core of eight histone proteins. A row of these looks like beads on a string.
  3. Chromatin fiber. The string of nucleosomes coils into a thicker fiber, about 30 nm across.
  4. Condensed chromosome. The fiber loops and folds until, at the start of mitosis, it becomes a compact chromosome that you can see under a microscope.

The job. Each level shortens the DNA. Together they compress about 2 meters of DNA into a nucleus about 6 micrometers across, and they keep the strands from tangling or breaking when the cell divides.

Aha: packing is not one squeeze but a ladder of coils. Winding on histones, then coiling the beads, then folding the fiber. Each step multiplies the compaction of the step before it.

Example 2. A human skin cell has just finished copying its DNA and its chromosomes have condensed for mitosis. (a) How many chromosomes does it have? (b) How many chromatids does it have? (c) How many chromosomes will each daughter cell have after the cell divides?

Reading the question. The trap here is that copying the DNA does NOT change the chromosome count. We count chromosomes by counting centromeres, not by counting strands of DNA.

  • (a) Chromosomes: 46. A human somatic cell has 46 chromosomes. After replication each chromosome is now two sister chromatids joined at one centromere, but it is still one chromosome, because there is still one centromere holding the copies together.
  • (b) Chromatids: 92. Each of the 46 chromosomes now has 2 sister chromatids, so \(46 \times 2 = 92\) chromatids total.
  • (c) Daughter cells: 46 each. During division the sister chromatids are pulled apart. Once separated, each former chromatid becomes a chromosome in its own right. So each daughter cell receives 46 chromosomes, the same count the parent started with.
Aha: a chromosome's identity is its centromere, not its amount of DNA. Two sister chromatids sharing one centromere are still ONE chromosome, right up until the centromere splits and they become two.

Example 3. On one chromosome-11 homolog a person carries the normal allele for the beta-globin gene, and on the other homolog they carry the sickle cell allele. (This is the kind of pairing the Rivera family could carry, since the sickle cell allele is a single-base change in this one gene, covered in Chapter 20.) Are these two DNA molecules sister chromatids or homologous chromosomes? What does that tell you about the person?

Reading the question. Two versions of the same gene sit at the same locus but hold different alleles. The key word is "different."

Answer. They are homologous chromosomes, not sister chromatids. Sister chromatids are identical copies, so they could not carry two different alleles. Homologous chromosomes carry the same gene at the same locus but may carry different alleles, which is exactly what we see here: normal on one homolog, sickle cell on the other.

What it tells you. Carrying one normal and one sickle cell allele makes the person a heterozygote (Chapter 5), which for sickle cell means a healthy carrier. The two alleles sit on homologs, one inherited from each parent.

Common pitfalls

Pitfall 1: counting a replicated chromosome as two. After DNA replication, each chromosome is two sister chromatids joined at one centromere. It is still ONE chromosome, because it has one centromere. A human cell in this state has 46 chromosomes and 92 chromatids, not 92 chromosomes. The count only doubles when the sister chromatids actually separate during division.
Pitfall 2: confusing sister chromatids with homologous chromosomes. Sister chromatids are identical copies of ONE chromosome, joined at a centromere. Homologous chromosomes are TWO separate chromosomes (one from each parent) that carry the same genes but can carry different alleles. Identical versus possibly-different is the whole distinction.
Pitfall 3: thinking heterochromatin means more DNA or that tight packing is the normal state. Euchromatin is the loosely packed, actively read form, and it is about 92% of the genome. Heterochromatin is the tightly packed minority doing structural work such as centromeres and telomeres. Tighter is not the default and does not mean more.

Connections

  • ← Chapter 1: DNA Structure: the double helix and its base pairs are the raw material that gets wound onto histones. The packaging ladder starts where Chapter 1 ends.
  • → Chapter 3: Mitosis: chromatin condenses into chromosomes at the start of mitosis, and the sister chromatids you meet here are pulled apart in anaphase to give each daughter cell a full set.
  • → Chapter 21: Karyotypes: a karyotype is a picture of all 46 condensed chromosomes lined up as 23 pairs. Counting autosomes and sex chromosomes here is the foundation for reading one.
  • → Chapter 5: Alleles, Genotype and Phenotype: the "same gene, possibly different allele" idea on homologous chromosomes is what makes genotypes like Aa possible.

Practice

P1. Put the four levels of DNA packaging in order, from least to most compact: chromatin fiber, condensed chromosome, double helix, nucleosome.

AnswerDouble helix, then nucleosome, then chromatin fiber, then condensed chromosome.

P2. A cell has replicated its DNA. One of its chromosomes is made of two sister chromatids joined at a centromere. A homologous chromosome (also replicated) sits nearby. In total, how many chromosomes and how many chromatids do these two homologs account for? Explain how you counted.

Answer2 chromosomes and 4 chromatids.

Why it works: count chromosomes by counting centromeres. Each replicated homolog has exactly one centromere holding its two sister chromatids together, so two homologs are two chromosomes, not four. Each of those chromosomes carries two chromatids, so the chromatid count is \(2 \times 2 = 4\). The chromatids only become separate chromosomes later, when the centromeres split during division.

Pattern, count by centromeres for chromosomes and by arms for chromatids: whenever a question mixes replicated and unreplicated chromosomes, always ask "how many centromeres?" for the chromosome count and "how many chromatids per centromere?" (one if unreplicated, two if replicated) for the chromatid count. This decision rule keeps you from doubling the chromosome number just because the DNA was copied. Contrast the two states: before replication one chromosome equals one chromatid; after replication one chromosome equals two chromatids.

P3. A researcher stains a nucleus and finds one region that is loosely packed and busy making RNA, and another region that is tightly packed, near a centromere, and making almost no RNA. Name each region (euchromatin or heterochromatin), and say which one most of the genome is.

AnswerThe loosely packed, RNA-making region is euchromatin. The tightly packed, centromere region making almost no RNA is heterochromatin. Most of the genome (about 92%) is euchromatin.

Why it works: packing density controls access. Loosely packed euchromatin is open, so the transcription machinery can reach the genes and copy them into RNA, which is why "busy making RNA" points to euchromatin. Tightly packed heterochromatin is closed off, so its genes are mostly silent, and it tends to sit in structural spots such as centromeres and telomeres. The 92% figure tells you the readable, open form is the majority, not the exception.

Pattern, map the clue to the packing state: "loosely packed / actively transcribed / making RNA" always maps to euchromatin, and "tightly packed / silent / structural / centromere or telomere" always maps to heterochromatin. Use the activity clue and the location clue together, because they point the same way. The common trap is assuming tighter packing is the default state of the genome; it is not, since the open euchromatin form dominates.

Watch / read preview before showing learners

↗ DNA packaging and chromosomes, Learn.Genetics (University of Utah) ↗ Khan Academy: chromosomes, chromatids, and chromatin ↗ Scioly.org Heredity wiki, DNA and chromatin section

Teaching notes

Opener: start with the scale problem before any vocabulary. Ask "about 2 meters of DNA has to fit in a nucleus 6 micrometers wide. How?" Let them guess. That tension motivates the whole packaging ladder and makes histones feel necessary rather than arbitrary.

Board moves: draw the packaging ladder left to right, one level at a time, and have students name the level after each arrow. Then write the two headline counts side by side: 46 chromosomes and 92 chromatids in a replicated cell. That pairing is the single most tested fact from this chapter.

What to hammer: "a chromosome is defined by its centromere, not its amount of DNA." Repeat it until Example 2 part (a) feels obvious. Also hammer the identical (sister chromatids) versus possibly-different (homologs) contrast, because the next several chapters depend on it.

Common stumble: students double the chromosome count to 92 after replication. Catch it early with P2, and make them count centromeres out loud every time.

Pacing: spend most of the time on the two distinctions (sister chromatids vs homologs, euchromatin vs heterochromatin), since those are the transferable ideas. The packaging ladder is mostly naming, so keep it brisk.

When to save Example 3: use it once alleles have been previewed, or use it to seed Chapter 5. It quietly connects the molecular picture to the classical genetics of the Rivera family, so it is a good bridge to the Mendelian chapters.

3. The Cell Cycle and Mitosis

A body cell copies its DNA once, then splits once, to make two genetically identical diploid daughter cells. That is the whole point of mitosis: growth and repair with no change to the genome.

Prerequisites: Chapter 1: DNA, Nucleotides and Structure · Chapter 2: Chromosomes, Chromatin and the Genome
This chapter: The Cell Cycle and Mitosis
Unlocks: Chapter 4: Meiosis (the contrast: division that halves the genome and makes variety)

First, watch one cell divide

A single fertilized egg becomes a body of trillions of cells, and a scraped knee heals in a week. Both happen because body cells keep dividing, and each copy must be exact. Before we name the stages, follow one cell all the way through a single division and just watch the numbers.

Take a made-up animal cell with only four chromosomes, written \(2n = 4\). That means two matched pairs: two long chromosomes (drawn red) and two short ones (drawn blue), one of each pair inherited from each parent. We will track three numbers at every step: the number of chromosomes, the number of chromatids (each chromatid is one double-helix DNA molecule), and the total amount of DNA. For the DNA amount we use the letter \(C\), where one \(C\) is the DNA in a single unreplicated set of chromosomes. A normal diploid cell that has not yet copied its DNA holds two sets, so \(2C\).

Stage Chromosomes Chromatids (DNA molecules) DNA amount
End of G1 (before copying)44\(2C\)
End of S / G2 (after copying)48\(4C\)
Metaphase (lined up)48\(4C\)
Anaphase (sisters split, one cell, brief)88\(4C\)
Each daughter (after cytokinesis)44\(2C\)

Read the table one row at a time. In G1 the cell is just growing, so each of the four chromosomes is a single chromatid: 4 chromosomes, 4 chromatids, \(2C\). In S phase the cell copies every chromosome, so each becomes two identical sister chromatids joined at a pinch point called the centromere, drawn as an X. The chromosome count does not change (still 4, because the two sisters share one centromere), but the DNA doubles to \(4C\) and the chromatid count doubles to 8. The cell finishes growing in G2 with the same counts. Then in mitosis the four doubled chromosomes line up at the middle (metaphase) and the sisters are pulled to opposite ends (anaphase). For one instant the separated sisters count as 8 chromosomes, then the cell splits in two (cytokinesis). Each daughter ends with 4 chromosomes, 4 chromatids, and \(2C\), exactly the cell we started with.

The pattern: copy once, divide once, stay diploid. The DNA doubles exactly once, in S phase, and the doubled material is split evenly into two cells exactly once, in M phase. Because the copy is exact and the split is even, the two daughters are genetically identical to the parent and to each other, and each is diploid (\(2n\)) again. That single rule is the whole point of mitosis: growth and repair with no change to the genome. Everything below explains how the cell carries the rule out, and how it checks that the copy is perfect.

The core idea

The cell cycle is the ordered set of stages a cell passes through to grow and divide. It has two big parts:

  • Interphase (the cell is not dividing yet; it grows and copies its DNA). This is where a cell spends most of its life, roughly 90% of the time. Interphase has three stages, in order:
    • G1 phase (Gap 1): the main growth phase. The cell increases in size, makes proteins, and duplicates its organelles (for example mitochondria). Usually the longest stage.
    • S phase (Synthesis): DNA replication happens here. Every chromosome is copied, so each chromosome now consists of two identical sister chromatids joined at the centromere (the constricted region where the copies are held together). A sister chromatid is one of the two identical DNA copies of a duplicated chromosome. Important: replication doubles the amount of DNA, but it does NOT change the chromosome number.
    • G2 phase (Gap 2): a shorter second growth phase. The cell finishes growing and builds the proteins it needs to divide.
  • The mitotic phase (M phase): the cell actually divides. It is two events. Mitosis is the division of the nucleus, which splits the copied chromosomes into two identical nuclei. Cytokinesis is the division of the rest of the cell (the cytoplasm) into two separate cells.

Somatic cells only. Mitosis happens in somatic cells, which means all body cells except the germ cells that make eggs and sperm. Somatic cells are diploid (written \(2n\)): they carry two copies of each chromosome, one inherited from each parent. In humans \(2n = 46\). Mitosis takes one diploid cell and makes two diploid cells that are genetically identical to the parent and to each other.

Checkpoints: quality control gates. The cell cycle is policed at checkpoints, points where the cell verifies that everything is correct before it is allowed to continue. Three matter for this event:

  • G1 checkpoint (end of G1): checks for DNA damage and for enough nutrients and size to divide. A cell that fails can pause, repair, or exit the cycle.
  • G2 checkpoint (end of G2): checks that the DNA was completely and correctly replicated in S phase, and makes repairs if needed, before mitosis begins.
  • Spindle checkpoint (during metaphase of mitosis): checks that every chromosome is properly attached to the spindle before the sister chromatids are allowed to separate.

These checkpoints protect the genome. When checkpoint control fails, a cell with damaged DNA can keep dividing without control. That unregulated division is the basis of cancer.

The four phases of mitosis. Once a cell passes into M phase, mitosis proceeds through four phases in a fixed order. A common memory aid for the whole sequence is IPMAT: Interphase, then Prophase, Metaphase, Anaphase, Telophase.

  • Prophase. The loose chromatin (the relaxed form of DNA wrapped on proteins, from Chapter 2) condenses into compact, visible chromosomes. Condensing protects the DNA and lets it be moved in one piece. The mitotic spindle begins to form. The spindle is a framework of microtubules (protein fibers) that grows out from the centrosomes and organizes and moves the chromosomes. The nuclear envelope (the membrane around the nucleus) breaks down and the nucleolus disappears. In late prophase (sometimes called prometaphase) spindle microtubules attach to each chromosome at the kinetochore, a protein patch on the centromere.
  • Metaphase. The spindle lines all the chromosomes up single file along the middle of the cell, on an imaginary plane called the metaphase plate. The two sister chromatids of each chromosome are attached to microtubules coming from opposite poles. The spindle checkpoint now verifies every chromosome is correctly attached before anything separates.
  • Anaphase. The protein glue holding the sisters together is cut, and the sister chromatids separate. Each is pulled to an opposite pole of the cell, and each separated chromatid is now a full chromosome in its own right. If a pair fails to separate, that error is called nondisjunction, and it leaves a daughter cell with the wrong number of chromosomes.
  • Telophase. The chromosomes reach the poles and begin to decondense back into chromatin. A new nuclear envelope reforms around each set, the nucleoli reappear, and the spindle breaks down. Telophase is essentially prophase run in reverse.

Cytokinesis then splits the cytoplasm into two cells. It differs by cell type because plant cells have a rigid cell wall and animal cells do not. In animal cells, a ring of protein filaments pinches the cell in two along a cleavage furrow. In plant cells, a cell plate forms across the middle and becomes a new cell wall between the two daughters.

Worked examples

Example 1 (name the phase). A microscope shows a dividing cell. The nuclear envelope is gone, the chromosomes are fully condensed and lined up single file across the exact center of the cell, and spindle fibers reach each chromosome from both poles. Which phase is this, and what happens immediately next?

The defining clue is the single-file line-up across the center. That center line is the metaphase plate, so this is metaphase. What happens next is anaphase: the spindle checkpoint confirms every chromosome is attached, then the sister chromatids separate and move to opposite poles.

aha: each phase of mitosis is named by where the chromosomes are and what they are doing, not by a clock. Condensing and spindle forming = prophase. Lined up in the middle = metaphase. Pulling apart = anaphase. Reforming nuclei = telophase.

Example 2 (count chromosomes and chromatids). A human somatic cell has \(2n = 46\) chromosomes. Track two numbers, the number of chromosomes and the number of chromatids, at four points: the end of G1, the end of G2, during metaphase, and in each daughter cell after cytokinesis.

Start by separating the two ideas. A chromosome is counted by its centromeres: while two sister chromatids stay joined at one centromere, they still count as one chromosome. A chromatid is a single DNA copy. So replication in S phase changes the chromatid count but not the chromosome count.

  • End of G1 (before replication): 46 chromosomes, each a single chromatid, so 46 chromatids.
  • End of G2 (after S phase copied everything): still 46 chromosomes, but each now has 2 sister chromatids, so \(46 \times 2 = 92\) chromatids.
  • Metaphase (lined up, not yet split): unchanged from G2. 46 chromosomes, 92 chromatids.
  • Each daughter after cytokinesis: anaphase separated the sisters and split them evenly, so each daughter gets 46 chromosomes, each a single chromatid, so 46 chromatids. Back to \(2n = 46\).
aha: replication doubles the chromatids, never the chromosomes. The chromosome number appears to jump to 92 only for the instant of anaphase, when the sisters split and are briefly separate chromosomes, and then cytokinesis hands 46 to each cell. The starting number \(2n = 46\) is restored. Mitosis keeps ploidy constant.

Example 3 (checkpoints and cancer). Ultraviolet light from the sun damages the DNA of a skin cell late in G1, just before S phase. Which checkpoint should catch this, and what goes wrong if the checkpoint machinery (for example the protein p53) is not working?

The damage occurs at the end of G1, so the G1 checkpoint is the gate that should catch it. Working correctly, the checkpoint halts the cycle so repair enzymes can fix the DNA before S phase copies it. If the damage is too severe, the checkpoint can force the cell to self-destruct so the fault is not passed on. Now suppose the checkpoint protein is broken. The damaged cell is waved through into S phase. It replicates the damaged DNA, passes the mistakes to both daughters in mitosis, and those daughters divide again. Errors accumulate, division is no longer controlled, and the result is the uncontrolled growth we call cancer.

aha: checkpoints are the cell's quality control gates, and healthy division depends on them saying "stop" when something is wrong. Losing checkpoint control is a defining feature of cancer, which is why genes like p53 are called tumor suppressors.

Common pitfalls

Interphase is not part of mitosis. Mitosis is only the four phases prophase, metaphase, anaphase, and telophase. Interphase (G1, S, G2) comes before it. In particular, DNA replication happens in S phase, during interphase, not during mitosis. Mitosis moves already-copied chromosomes; it does not copy them.
Mitosis makes identical diploid cells, not varied or haploid ones. The two daughters are genetically identical to the parent and each is diploid (\(2n\)). Do not confuse this with meiosis. Meiosis makes four genetically varied haploid cells. Genetic variety comes from meiosis and fertilization, never from mitosis.
Sister chromatids separate in anaphase; homologous chromosomes do not separate at all in mitosis. In mitosis, anaphase pulls apart the two identical sister chromatids of each chromosome. Homologous chromosomes (the maternal and paternal copies of a chromosome) are not paired or separated in mitosis. Their separation is a meiosis event (anaphase I). Failure to separate at anaphase is nondisjunction, which produces cells with an abnormal chromosome number.

Connections

The DNA copied in S phase is the double helix from Chapter 1: DNA, Nucleotides and Structure, and the actual copying machinery of S phase is Chapter 15: DNA Replication. The chromatin that condenses in prophase, and the diploid chromosome set, come from Chapter 2: Chromosomes, Chromatin and the Genome. The sharpest contrast is Chapter 4: Meiosis: mitosis copies once and divides once to make two identical diploid cells, while meiosis copies once and divides twice to make four varied haploid gametes. The nondisjunction that can happen at anaphase is the cause of the chromosome-number disorders in Chapter 21: Chromosomal Disorders and Karyotypes. The checkpoint failure that leads to uncontrolled division connects to Chapter 23: Genetic Disorders and the Frontier.

★ Landmarks it points toward

  • ★ The cell cycle and cancer (checkpoint control). How tumor suppressor genes (such as p53) and oncogenes govern the checkpoints, and how their failure drives unregulated cell division. Named here, studied in depth later.

Practice

P1. Put these stages in the correct order for a dividing body cell: metaphase, G1, telophase, S, prophase, anaphase, G2.

Answer

Order: G1, S, G2, prophase, metaphase, anaphase, telophase.

Why: the cell first goes through interphase, whose three stages run G1, then S, then G2, in that order (grow, copy DNA, grow again). Only after interphase does mitosis begin, and its four phases always run prophase, metaphase, anaphase, telophase. Reading the events tells you the order without memorizing: DNA must be copied (S) before it can be lined up and split, chromosomes must condense (prophase) before they can line up (metaphase), and they must line up before they can be pulled apart (anaphase) and repackaged into nuclei (telophase).

Pattern (order by what has to happen first): the memory aid IPMAT covers the whole sequence, Interphase then Prophase, Metaphase, Anaphase, Telophase. When you forget the order, do not recite letters blindly; ask what each phase needs to already be true. Each phase depends on the one before it, so the biology fixes the order. This same "order by dependency" habit carries straight into Chapter 4: Meiosis, which adds a second division on the end.

P2. What happens during S phase, and how does it change each chromosome?

AnswerDNA replication. Each chromosome is copied, so it goes from one chromatid to two identical sister chromatids joined at the centromere. The chromosome number does not change.

P3. A human cell with \(2n = 46\) completes mitosis and cytokinesis. How many cells result, how many chromosomes does each have, and how do they compare genetically? During mitosis, when do the sister chromatids separate, and what is it called if they fail to separate?

Answer

Result: 2 daughter cells, each with 46 chromosomes, and both are genetically identical to each other and to the parent cell. The sister chromatids separate during anaphase. A failure to separate is called nondisjunction, which leaves one daughter with an extra chromosome and the other missing one.

Why: the cell copied all its DNA exactly once, in S phase, so each chromosome became two identical sister chromatids. Mitosis then divided that material exactly once, sending one chromatid from each pair to each pole in anaphase. One round of copying and one round of splitting means the count returns to the starting \(2n = 46\), and because the two chromatids were identical copies, the two cells carry the same genome. Nondisjunction breaks the even split, so a daughter ends up with the wrong number of chromosomes (aneuploidy).

Pattern (copy once, divide once, stay diploid): mitosis is defined by one replication followed by one division, which is exactly why the product is two identical diploid cells for growth and repair. Contrast this with meiosis, which copies once but divides twice, halving the number to make four varied haploid gametes. Whenever a question asks "how many cells, what ploidy, how similar," count the replications and the divisions first. That is the deciding difference between the two processes, developed next in Chapter 4: Meiosis.

Watch / read preview before showing students

↗ Khan Academy: The cell cycle and mitosis ▶ Amoeba Sisters: Mitosis, the amazing cell process (YouTube) ▶ Amoeba Sisters: The cell cycle and cancer (YouTube) ↗ Science Olympiad wiki: Heredity (Mitosis and Cell Cycle section)

Teaching notes

Opener: ask "you started as one cell and you have trillions now, and a cut heals in a week, so what must your cells be doing constantly?" Land on "dividing, and copying perfectly." That frames mitosis as exact copying, which sets up the whole chapter.

Board moves: draw the cell cycle wheel first and shade interphase big, M small, so the "90% interphase" fact is visual. Then draw the four-panel phase strip left to right and narrate the chromosomes moving. Do the chromosome-and-chromatid count table from Example 2 live; it is the single best check that they understand what S phase does.

What to hammer: three things. Interphase is not mitosis. S phase doubles chromatids, not chromosome number. The product is two identical diploid cells. Make them say the last one out loud.

Common stumble: students say S phase "makes more chromosomes." Correct it every time to "copies each chromosome into two sister chromatids; the number stays the same." The count table fixes this.

Pacing: about 15 minutes on the cycle and checkpoints, 20 minutes on the four phases with the strip, 10 minutes on the count table and practice. Save the deep cancer or p53 discussion for the landmark; name it here, do not teach it.

Save for later: resist teaching meiosis now. Keep the contrast as a one-line teaser ("next chapter: the division that halves and shuffles") so Chapter 4: Meiosis has a clean hook.

4. Meiosis

One division makes copies. Two divisions, with a shuffle in between, make variety: meiosis turns one diploid cell into four genetically different haploid gametes.

Prerequisites: Chapter 3: The Cell Cycle and Mitosis (interphase, DNA replication in S phase, how sister chromatids separate).
This chapter: Meiosis (how gametes are made, and why they vary).
Unlocks: Chapter 6: Mendel and the Law of Segregation (segregation is meiosis seen from the outside) · Chapter 21: Chromosomal Disorders and Karyotypes (what happens when meiosis fails to separate chromosomes).

Start with three cases

Meiosis is the special cell division that makes gametes (the sex cells: sperm and eggs). Before the step-by-step machinery, work through three concrete cases and watch what actually happens to the chromosomes. Two facts fall out of them, and the rest of the chapter just explains why. A few terms are defined as they come up; the mechanism section afterward makes them precise.

Case 1 (track a cell through both divisions). Start with a germ cell (a cell in the reproductive line, as opposed to an ordinary body or somatic cell) that has \(2n = 4\). That means it is diploid: it carries two copies of every chromosome, one from each parent, so here two pairs, one long pair and one short pair. We draw the two parents' copies in two colors. The cell has already copied its DNA in S phase, so each chromosome is now two identical sister chromatids joined at the centromere. Follow the two divisions in the figure. How many chromosomes and how many chromatids are present after replication, after meiosis I, and after meiosis II?

After replication (start of meiosis I): the cell still has 4 chromosomes (2 homolog pairs), but each is now 2 sister chromatids, so there are \(4 \times 2 = 8\) chromatids.

After meiosis I: the two members of each pair, the homologous chromosomes, are pulled apart. Each of the 2 daughter cells gets 2 chromosomes. Each chromosome is still 2 sister chromatids, so each cell has 4 chromatids. The cells are now haploid (\(n = 2\), one copy of each chromosome) even though the chromosomes are still doubled.

After meiosis II: the sister chromatids separate, so each of the 4 gametes has 2 single-chromatid chromosomes (\(n = 2\)).

Aha: chromosome number is counted by centromeres, not by chromatids. The number halves at meiosis I (homologs separate), not at meiosis II. A cell can be haploid and still have doubled chromosomes.

Case 2 (why the gametes are not identical copies). Take one homolog pair. On one homolog, a gene near the top carries allele \(A\) and a gene near the bottom carries allele \(B\). On the other homolog the same two genes carry \(a\) and \(b\) (a different allele, or version, of each gene). Before any crossing over, what allele combinations can this pair send into gametes? What does one crossover, an exchange of matching segments between the homologs, add?

Before crossing over: the two homologs give only the two parental combinations, \(A\,B\) and \(a\,b\). These are the combinations the cell inherited.

After one crossover between the genes: the two inner chromatids swap their lower segments. Now the four chromatids read \(A\,B\), \(A\,b\), \(a\,B\), and \(a\,b\). The two new ones, \(A\,b\) and \(a\,B\), are recombinant: allele pairings that neither parent chromosome had.

Aha: crossing over builds allele combinations that did not exist in the parent cell. It only matters where the homologs carry different alleles (where the cell is heterozygous), because swapping identical segments changes nothing.

Case 3 (how much variation, before crossing over even starts). A human has \(n = 23\) chromosome pairs. Ignoring crossing over for the moment, how many chromosomally different gametes can the random sorting of pairs alone produce?

At metaphase I each of the 23 pairs lines up independently of the others, and each pair can send either its maternal or its paternal homolog into a given gamete. That is 2 choices per pair, made independently, so by the multiplication principle:

\[ 2^{n} = 2^{23} = 8{,}388{,}608 \text{ possible gametes.} \]

This is independent assortment: the pairs sort on their own. Add crossing over, and the number of genuinely distinct gametes is far larger still. Add random fertilization (any of one person's gametes meeting any of another's) and the number of possible offspring genotypes runs into the trillions.

Aha: variation here is combinatorial, so it grows as a power of 2, not by simple addition. This is why siblings (except identical twins) are never genetically the same.

The pattern: halving and shuffling

The three cases share the same two moves, and together they are the entire point of meiosis.

  • Halving. Case 1 showed the chromosome number drop from \(2n\) to \(n\). If two diploid cells simply fused at fertilization, the count would double every generation. Meiosis halves it first, so fertilization can restore \(2n\) in the offspring.
  • Shuffling. Cases 2 and 3 showed the alleles rearranged. Crossing over builds allele combinations that were on neither parent chromosome, and independent assortment mixes whole chromosomes, giving \(2^n\) combinations for \(n\) pairs. The four products are genetically varied, never identical copies.

Halving plus shuffling in one process is exactly what sexual reproduction needs: gametes with half the chromosomes, each one genetically unique. The rest of the chapter explains the machinery that produces both.

The mechanism: two divisions, one replication

The precise statement. Meiosis takes one diploid germ cell and produces four haploid cells in two divisions, called meiosis I and meiosis II, following a single round of DNA replication. Body cells are diploid (\(2n\), two copies of each chromosome); gametes are haploid (\(n\), one copy of each). The four products are genetically varied, not identical copies.

Two vocabulary words you must not confuse. Nearly every meiosis question turns on telling these two apart:

  • Homologous chromosomes (homologs): the two members of a chromosome pair. They carry the same genes in the same order, but they may carry different alleles (different versions of a gene), because one came from the mother and one from the father. So homologs are a matched pair from two parents, and they can differ.
  • Sister chromatids: the two identical copies of a single chromosome, produced when DNA replicates in S phase, joined at a point called the centromere. Sister chromatids are copies of the same chromosome, so they carry the same alleles (until crossing over changes one of them). They start out identical.

Meiosis I (the reductional division)

Meiosis I is called reductional because it is the division that reduces the chromosome number from \(2n\) to \(n\).

  • Prophase I: chromosomes condense, and each pair of homologs comes together and lines up side by side, a pairing called synapsis. The paired homologs form a four-chromatid structure called a tetrad (also called a bivalent). While they are paired, the homologs undergo crossing over: they exchange matching (homologous) segments at crossover points called chiasmata. Crossing over creates new combinations of alleles on a chromosome, an outcome called recombination (this is Case 2).
  • Metaphase I: the tetrads line up across the middle of the cell. Each pair orients randomly, independent of how the other pairs orient. This random orientation is independent assortment (this is Case 3).
  • Anaphase I: the two homologous chromosomes are pulled to opposite ends of the cell. The sister chromatids stay joined. This is the exact step that halves the chromosome number.
  • Telophase I and cytokinesis: two haploid cells form. Each holds one chromosome from every original pair, and each chromosome still consists of two sister chromatids.

Meiosis II (the equational division, like mitosis)

Meiosis II is called equational because the chromosome number does not change during it. There is no DNA replication between meiosis I and meiosis II.

  • Prophase II and metaphase II: the chromosomes (each still two sister chromatids) line up in each of the two cells.
  • Anaphase II: now the sister chromatids separate and move to opposite poles, exactly as they do in mitosis.
  • Result: four haploid cells, each with single-chromatid chromosomes.

Three sources of genetic variation all trace to meiosis and fertilization, never to mitosis:

  1. Crossing over (prophase I): reshuffles alleles along a single chromosome.
  2. Independent assortment (metaphase I): each homolog pair sorts on its own, giving \(2^n\) chromosome combinations for \(n\) pairs.
  3. Random fertilization: any one sperm can meet any one egg.

Meiosis versus mitosis

The two divisions look similar under a microscope but do opposite jobs. Keep this table straight and most meiosis questions answer themselves.

Feature Mitosis Meiosis
Number of divisions12 (meiosis I and II)
Daughter cells24
Ploidy of productsdiploid (\(2n\))haploid (\(n\))
Genetics of productsidentical to the parent cellgenetically varied
What separates, and whensister chromatids, in anaphasehomologs in anaphase I, then sister chromatids in anaphase II
Crossing overnoyes, in prophase I
Purposegrowth, repair, asexual reproductionmaking gametes for sexual reproduction

After meiosis: sperm versus egg

Meiosis produces four haploid cells, but the two sexes package them differently.

  • Spermatogenesis (sperm formation): all four haploid products mature into four functional sperm.
  • Oogenesis (egg formation): the cytoplasm divides unequally. One product keeps almost all of the cytoplasm and organelles and becomes the single functional egg. The other products become small polar bodies that carry a nucleus but little cytoplasm, and they degenerate. So one meiosis yields one egg, not four.

A side effect: because the egg supplies nearly all the cytoplasm (including the mitochondria) and the sperm's mitochondria are destroyed after fertilization, mitochondrial DNA is inherited from the mother. This is why maternal lineage can be traced through mitochondrial DNA.

Common pitfalls:
  • Confusing what separates when. Homologous chromosomes separate in anaphase I; sister chromatids separate in anaphase II (and in mitosis). Reversing these is the single most common mistake on this topic.
  • Thinking meiosis makes identical cells. That is mitosis. Meiosis makes four genetically varied cells; if a question wants identical copies, it is asking about mitosis.
  • Adding a second DNA replication. DNA is copied once, before meiosis I. There is no replication between meiosis I and meiosis II.
  • Miscounting ploidy. Ploidy counts whole chromosomes (centromeres), not chromatids. After meiosis I the cells are already haploid even though each chromosome still has two chromatids.

Connections

  • ← Chapter 3: The Cell Cycle and Mitosis: meiosis II is essentially mitosis run on a haploid cell (sister chromatids separate). The difference lives entirely in meiosis I.
  • → Chapter 6: Mendel and the Law of Segregation: Mendel's law of segregation (a parent passes only one allele of each gene to a gamete) is exactly what anaphase I does when homologs separate. Segregation is meiosis viewed from the outside.
  • → Chapter 9: Independent Assortment and Dihybrid Crosses: the \(2^n\) shuffling of chromosome pairs is the physical basis of independent assortment and the \(9{:}3{:}3{:}1\) dihybrid ratio.
  • → Chapter 21: Chromosomal Disorders and Karyotypes: if homologs (anaphase I) or sister chromatids (anaphase II) fail to separate, a condition called nondisjunction, a gamete ends up with the wrong chromosome number, causing conditions such as trisomy 21.
  • ↔ The family thread: the alleles the Rivera parents (Elena and David) pass to Mateo, Lucia, and Sofia were separated and reshuffled by exactly this process, which is why the three children are not genetic copies of each other.

Practice

P1. In a cell undergoing meiosis, in which anaphase do homologous chromosomes separate, and in which do sister chromatids separate?

AnswerHomologous chromosomes separate in anaphase I; sister chromatids separate in anaphase II. (This is also why chromosome number halves at meiosis I, not meiosis II.)

P2. A grasshopper species has \(n = 12\). Ignoring crossing over, how many chromosomally different gametes can independent assortment alone produce? What is the same number for a human (\(n = 23\))?

AnswerGrasshopper: \(2^{12} = 4{,}096\). Human: \(2^{23} = 8{,}388{,}608\).

Why it is a power of 2: at metaphase I each homolog pair lines up independently, and for each pair a given gamete gets either the maternal homolog or the paternal one. That is 2 independent choices per pair. Because the pairs sort independently, you multiply the choices together (the multiplication principle), giving \(2 \times 2 \times \cdots\) with one factor per pair, which is \(2^n\).

Pattern (independent binary choices multiply to a power of 2): whenever a count is built from \(n\) independent yes-or-no (or this-or-that) choices, the total is \(2^n\). Reach for it when each item contributes exactly 2 options and the items are independent. Contrast it with crossing over, which adds even more variety on top of this figure, and with random fertilization, which multiplies one parent's \(2^n\) by the other parent's \(2^n\).

P3. A diploid cell has \(2n = 8\). Compare the products of mitosis and of meiosis for this cell: how many cells result, and how many chromosomes does each contain?

AnswerMitosis: 2 cells, each diploid with \(2n = 8\) chromosomes, genetically identical to the parent. Meiosis: 4 cells, each haploid with \(n = 4\) chromosomes, genetically varied.

Watch / read preview before showing students

▶ Amoeba Sisters: Meiosis (YouTube) ▶ Amoeba Sisters: Comparing Mitosis and Meiosis (YouTube) ↗ Khan Academy: Phases of Meiosis ▶ CrashCourse Biology: Mitosis vs Meiosis (YouTube)

Teaching notes

Opener: ask "if each parent's cells have 46 chromosomes, why doesn't the child have 92?" Let them wrestle with the doubling problem, then reveal that meiosis halves the number so fertilization can restore it. That framing makes haploid gametes feel necessary rather than arbitrary, and it sets up the three opening cases.

Board moves: draw the meiosis strip live with \(2n = 4\), two colors for the two parents (this is Case 1). Stop the class at anaphase I and ask "what is moving apart, homologs or chromatids?" then again at anaphase II. The two-color drawing makes independent assortment visible: point out that the top and bottom cells got different color mixes. Draw the crossing-over figure separately (Case 2) so recombination does not get tangled with the strip.

What to hammer: the homolog versus sister-chromatid distinction, and the rule "homologs separate in anaphase I, chromatids in anaphase II." Say it out loud several times. Also hammer that ploidy counts centromeres, so a cell is haploid the moment meiosis I ends.

Common stumble: students say a cell is still diploid after meiosis I because "the chromosomes still look doubled." Correct it immediately: doubled chromatids do not make a cell diploid; the count of chromosomes (one per pair now) does.

Pacing: open with the three cases, then name the halving-and-shuffling pattern before formalizing the phases. Spend the most time on meiosis I and the two anaphases (that is where all the concepts live), then treat meiosis II quickly as "just mitosis on a haploid cell." Use the \(2^n\) counting (Case 3) as the bridge into independent assortment.

Chapter 5: Alleles, Genotype and Phenotype

A gene sits at a fixed address on a chromosome. You carry two copies, one per homologous chromosome. The pair you carry is your genotype; the trait you show is your phenotype.

🧬 Running example, the Rivera family: we track PTC tasting through three generations. PTC (phenylthiocarbamide) is a harmless bitter chemical. Some people taste it strongly; others taste nothing. Tasting is controlled by one gene with two alleles: T = taster (dominant) and t = non-taster (recessive). The parents Elena and David are both tasters. Their three children are Sofia, Lucia, and Mateo. We will use this same family and these same alleles for every classical-genetics chapter, so the genotypes stay consistent.
Prerequisites: Chapter 1: DNA, Nucleotides and Structure (a gene is a stretch of DNA) · Chapter 4: Meiosis (homologous chromosomes separate into gametes).
This chapter: Alleles, Genotype and Phenotype (the vocabulary that turns molecules into inheritance).
Unlocks: all of Mendelian genetics: Chapter 6: Mendel and the Law of Segregation · Chapter 7: Punnett Squares and Monohybrid Crosses · Chapter 8: Probability in Genetics · Chapter 9: Independent Assortment and Dihybrid Crosses.

Start with one family and one taste test

Elena and David are both PTC tasters. One afternoon they gave a PTC test strip to each of their three children. Sofia and Lucia both tasted a sharp bitterness right away. Mateo tasted nothing at all. Same strip, same parents, three children, and one of them cannot taste what the other two can. Why?

The answer is written in their genes. Each child carries two copies of the PTC gene, one inherited from Elena and one from David. We label each copy with a letter: T for the taster version and t for the non-taster version. Here is the pair of copies each child carries, and the result on the strip.

Read the three cases one at a time:

  • Sofia carries T and T. She has the taster version twice, so she is a taster.
  • Lucia carries T and t. She has one taster copy and one non-taster copy, yet she is still a full taster. The single T is enough.
  • Mateo carries t and t. He has no taster copy at all, so he is a non-taster.

Name the pattern

Look across the three children and two rules jump out.

One T is enough. A child tastes PTC whenever at least one T is present. Sofia has two T copies and Lucia has one, and both are tasters. Only Mateo, who has no T at all, cannot taste. So a single T copy hides the t sitting next to it. It takes two t copies, with zero T, to show the non-taster result.

The copies are one thing; the trait is another. Sofia (T and T) and Lucia (T and t) carry different pairs of copies, yet they show the same result. The pair of letters a child carries and the trait that pair produces are two separate levels, and they do not always line up one to one.

This also solves the puzzle we opened with. Both parents taste PTC, yet Mateo does not. Both Elena and David must each carry a hidden t copy alongside a T (each parent is T and t), and Mateo happened to receive the t copy from each of them. A non-taster copy can ride along unseen in a tasting parent and then surface in a child. Chapter 6: Mendel and the Law of Segregation makes that idea precise.

The rest of this chapter gives these ideas their real names: the copies are alleles, the pair a child carries is the genotype, the trait it produces is the phenotype, and the "one T is enough" rule is dominance.

The core idea

Recall from Chapter 1 that a gene is a segment of DNA that carries the instructions for one product, often a protein. Every gene has a fixed physical address on a chromosome. That address is called a locus (plural loci). The PTC gene, for example, lives at one specific locus on human chromosome 7. Wherever that gene is discussed, it is at the same locus in every person.

From Chapter 2, your body cells are diploid: they carry chromosomes in matching pairs. The two members of a pair are called homologous chromosomes. Homologous means they carry the same genes in the same order, at the same loci. One homolog came from your mother, the other from your father. So at the PTC locus you have two copies of the gene, one on each homolog.

The two copies do not have to be identical. A gene can exist in more than one version, and each version is an allele. For PTC there are two common alleles: T (taster) and t (non-taster). Your mother's homolog might carry T while your father's carries t. That is why we can write your makeup for this gene as a pair of letters.

Genotype versus phenotype

Two terms name the two levels, and exam questions constantly test whether you can tell them apart.

  • Genotype is the pair of alleles you carry for a gene, written as two letters, for example TT, Tt, or tt. It is the genetic makeup, the instructions.
  • Phenotype is the observable trait that results, for example "taster" or "non-taster". It is what you can measure or see.

A genotype describes the alleles; a phenotype describes the outcome. Different genotypes can give the same phenotype, which is exactly what happens with dominance below.

Homozygous versus heterozygous

A genotype's two alleles are either the same or different.

  • Homozygous means the two alleles are identical: TT (homozygous dominant) or tt (homozygous recessive). "Homo" means same.
  • Heterozygous means the two alleles are different: Tt. "Hetero" means different.

Dominant versus recessive

When the two alleles differ, the cell still has to produce a single trait. The rule is dominance.

  • A dominant allele shows its effect even when only one copy is present. It masks the recessive allele in a heterozygote. We write it with a capital letter, T.
  • A recessive allele shows its effect only when two copies are present, that is, only in the homozygous recessive genotype tt. We write it lowercase, t.

So T is dominant to t. A person is a non-taster (the recessive phenotype) only if the genotype is tt. Both TT and Tt are tasters, because a single T is enough to make the taster phenotype.

Dominant does not mean common, stronger, or better. "Dominant" says only one thing: this allele's effect shows up in a heterozygote and hides the other allele's effect. It says nothing about how frequent the allele is in a population. A recessive allele can be far more common than the dominant one. Polydactyly (extra fingers) is caused by a dominant allele, yet it is rare, because that allele is rare. Frequency is set by how many copies exist in the population, not by dominance.

Why the two alleles separate: a preview of segregation

Here is the bridge from the cell chapters to Mendel. In Chapter 4 you saw that homologous chromosomes separate from each other during anaphase I of meiosis. Each gamete (egg or sperm) receives one homolog from each pair, so a gamete is haploid: one copy of each chromosome, and therefore one allele of each gene.

Apply that to a heterozygous parent, genotype Tt. The T allele sits on one homolog and the t allele sits on the other. When those homologs separate in meiosis, T and t end up in different gametes. Half the gametes carry T and half carry t. In symbols, each gamete has probability \(\tfrac{1}{2}\) of carrying T and \(\tfrac{1}{2}\) of carrying t.

That is the physical reason behind Mendel's Law of Segregation, which you will meet formally in Chapter 6: the two alleles of a gene separate cleanly into different gametes because the two homologous chromosomes that carry them separate in meiosis. The vocabulary in this chapter is what makes that law readable.

Worked examples

Example 1 (read a genotype, name the phenotype). Elena and David are both tasters. Their three children have the genotypes: Sofia is TT, Lucia is Tt, and Mateo is tt. For each child, name the zygosity and the phenotype.

Sofia, TT. Two identical alleles, so homozygous (specifically homozygous dominant). At least one T is present, so the phenotype is taster.

Lucia, Tt. Two different alleles, so heterozygous. One T is present and it masks the t, so the phenotype is taster.

Mateo, tt. Two identical alleles, so homozygous (homozygous recessive). No T is present, so the recessive phenotype shows: non-taster.

Aha: go from genotype to phenotype by asking one question: is there at least one dominant allele? If yes, the dominant trait shows. Only the all-recessive genotype (tt) shows the recessive trait.

Example 2 (is a heterozygote a taster?). Lucia is Tt. She is a taster, yet she carries the non-taster allele t. How can she be a taster while still carrying t, and what will she pass on?

Lucia's phenotype is set by dominance. She has one T, and T is dominant, so her cells make the taster phenotype. The t allele is still physically present on one homolog; it is simply masked, not erased. A heterozygous carrier like Lucia is often called a carrier of the recessive allele: she does not show the recessive trait, but she can pass the recessive allele to a child.

What she passes on comes from meiosis. Her T and t sit on homologous chromosomes that separate, so half her gametes carry T and half carry t. Probability \(\tfrac{1}{2}\) T and \(\tfrac{1}{2}\) t.
