Aha: phenotype and gametes are different questions. A heterozygote shows the dominant trait but transmits either allele with equal chance. This is why a recessive trait can reappear in a later generation even after seeming to vanish.

Example 3 (genotype to gametes, the segregation link). David is a taster with genotype Tt. List the alleles his sperm can carry, and connect the answer to what happens in meiosis.

David's two alleles, T and t, are on two homologous chromosomes. During anaphase I of meiosis those homologs are pulled to opposite poles, so they end up in different cells. The result is two kinds of gamete: one carrying T, one carrying t, in equal proportion. So David's sperm are \(\tfrac{1}{2}\) T and \(\tfrac{1}{2}\) t.

Aha: a heterozygote makes two gamete types, one per allele, because it has two homologs that separate. A homozygote (TT or tt) makes only one gamete type for that gene, because both homologs carry the same allele. This single fact is what a Punnett square in Chapter 7 is built on.

Common pitfalls

  • Confusing genotype with phenotype. "Taster" is a phenotype, not a genotype; "Tt" is a genotype, not a phenotype. A question that asks for the genotype wants letters (TT, Tt, or tt); a question that asks for the phenotype wants the trait (taster or non-taster). Read the wording and match it.
  • Thinking dominant means common or stronger. Dominant only means the allele's effect appears in a heterozygote. It says nothing about frequency in a population or about the "strength" of the molecule. See the boxed warning above.
  • Reading a heterozygote as a blend. In simple dominance, Tt is a full taster, identical in phenotype to TT. It is not "half a taster". Blending of the two alleles is a different pattern, incomplete dominance, covered in Chapter 10.
  • Forgetting that tt is the only recessive phenotype. A single dominant allele is enough to mask the recessive, so only the genotype with zero dominant alleles shows the recessive trait.

Connections

  • ← Chapter 1: DNA: a gene is a stretch of DNA, and an allele is a version of that sequence. Two alleles differ by their DNA sequence at the same locus.
  • ← Chapter 2: Chromosomes: diploid cells carry homologous pairs, which is why you have two alleles of every gene.
  • ← Chapter 4: Meiosis: homologous chromosomes separate in anaphase I, which physically separates the two alleles into different gametes.
  • → Chapter 6: Mendel and the Law of Segregation: the separation of alleles into gametes, named and formalized.
  • → Chapter 7: Punnett Squares: gamete alleles are the row and column labels; the grid combines them into offspring genotypes.
  • → Chapter 10: Incomplete Dominance and Codominance: patterns where the heterozygote is not identical to a homozygote, so genotype and phenotype line up one to one.
  • → Chapter 22: Pedigrees: a carrier (heterozygote) is unaffected but transmits the recessive allele, which is how recessive traits skip generations.

Practice

P1. Sofia has genotype TT and Mateo has genotype tt. For each, state the zygosity (homozygous or heterozygous) and the PTC phenotype.

AnswerSofia, TT: homozygous (homozygous dominant), phenotype taster. Mateo, tt: homozygous (homozygous recessive), phenotype non-taster.

P2. Lucia is heterozygous, Tt, for PTC tasting. Is she a taster or a non-taster? Explain in one sentence.

AnswerTaster. She has one T allele, and because T is dominant, a single copy is enough to produce the taster phenotype and mask the t.

P3. A classmate says, "T is the dominant allele for PTC tasting, so most people in the world must be tasters, and the t allele must be dying out." Is this reasoning correct? Explain what is wrong and what "dominant" actually tells you.

Answer

The reasoning is wrong. Being dominant does not make an allele common, and being recessive does not make an allele rare or "dying out". Dominance describes only what happens inside a single heterozygote: the dominant allele's effect shows and the recessive allele's effect is masked. How frequent each allele is in a population is a separate fact, set by how many copies exist across all people, not by which allele is dominant. (In real populations the non-taster allele t is quite common, and non-tasters are a large fraction of people, even though T is dominant.)

Why: dominance and frequency answer two different questions. Dominance answers "in a Tt individual, which trait shows?" Frequency answers "out of all the allele copies in the population, how many are T versus t?" Nothing in the definition of dominant links the two. An allele stays at whatever frequency the population's history gave it, whether it is dominant or recessive. A dominant allele does not spread just by being dominant, and a recessive allele does not shrink just by being recessive. Allele frequencies change only through processes like selection, migration, and chance, not through dominance.

Pattern (separate the mechanism from the count): whenever a claim mixes "dominant" with "common" (or "recessive" with "rare"), split it into two questions and answer each on its own. Dominance is a rule about masking within one genotype; frequency is a count across a whole population. Real counterexamples fix the idea: the dominant polydactyly allele is rare, and many common recessive alleles produce common traits. The tool that actually predicts allele and genotype frequencies in a population is Hardy-Weinberg equilibrium, which you will see named later.

Watch / read preview before showing students

↗ Khan Academy: Alleles and genes ▶ Alleles and Genes (Amoeba Sisters, YouTube) ↗ NHGRI genetics glossary: Allele

Teaching notes

Opener: hand out (or describe) PTC test strips. Some students taste bitterness, some taste nothing, from the same strip. Ask "why does the same chemical taste different?" That difference is a phenotype, and the point of the chapter is to trace it down to two alleles at one locus.

Board moves: draw the homologous pair (Figure 2) first. Put the locus at the same height on both chromosomes and write T on one, t on the other. Say out loud, "one gene, one address, two copies, one from each parent." Then build the genotype-to-phenotype table (Figure 3) column by column, filling the phenotype last so students predict it.

What to name and hammer: the four-way vocabulary (allele, locus, genotype, phenotype) plus homozygous versus heterozygous and dominant versus recessive. Drill the single decision rule: "at least one dominant allele means the dominant trait shows." Repeat the warning "dominant is not the same as common" at least twice; it is the misconception students carry in and must carry out corrected.

The common stumble: students swap genotype and phenotype on tests. Every time you say a trait, ask "genotype or phenotype?" until it is automatic.

Bridge to save for the end: the segregation preview (Example 3). Show that a Tt parent makes T and t gametes because the homologs separate in meiosis. This is the hook into Chapter 6 and the Punnett square in Chapter 7, so end here and let them see the map ahead.

Pacing: this is a vocabulary chapter, so keep it tight. Spend most of the time on the genotype-to-phenotype table and the "dominant is not common" correction, which are the two ideas that pay off all through Mendelian genetics.

Chapter 6: Mendel & the Law of Segregation

The rule that starts classical genetics: the two alleles of a gene separate into different gametes, so a hidden recessive can reappear in the next generation.

Prerequisites: Chapter 4: Meiosis (where the two alleles physically separate) · Chapter 5: Alleles, Genotype & Phenotype (dominant, recessive, homozygous, heterozygous).
This node: Mendel's pea experiments and the Law of Segregation, plus the common monohybrid cross ratios.
Unlocks: Chapter 7: Punnett Squares & Monohybrid Crosses (the tool that computes these ratios) · Chapter 9: Independent Assortment & Dihybrid Crosses (the same rule applied to two genes at once).

Start with what Mendel saw

In the 1860s Gregor Mendel ran a series of breeding experiments on pea plants that started the whole science of genetics. We will follow one of his experiments exactly as he did it, across three generations, and watch what the numbers do before we explain them.

Mendel began with plants he already knew bred consistently. A plant is true-breeding (also called pure-breeding) for a trait when self-pollinating it gives offspring that all show that same trait, generation after generation. One of his lines was true-breeding for purple flowers; another was true-breeding for white flowers. Geneticists label the generations of a cross with standard names, so learn them now:

  • P generation (parental): the two true-breeding parents you start with. This P means "parental." It is a different P from the allele letter P we use later for the purple allele.
  • F1 generation (first filial): the direct offspring of the P cross. "Filial" is Latin for son or daughter.
  • F2 generation (second filial): the offspring made when F1 individuals are crossed with each other. For a self-pollinating pea, this simply means letting each F1 plant self-pollinate.

Here is the flower-color experiment and, more importantly, its result:

  1. P generation: true-breeding purple crossed with true-breeding white.
  2. F1 generation: every single offspring was purple. The white trait vanished completely. Not one F1 plant was white, and none were an in-between pale shade.
  3. F2 generation: Mendel let the all-purple F1 self-pollinate. White came back. He counted 705 purple and 224 white plants, a ratio of about 3.15 to 1, very close to 3 purple : 1 white.

That 3 : 1 in the F2 is the observation this whole chapter exists to explain. Mendel did not stop at flower color. He ran the same kind of cross for seven different pea traits (seed shape, seed color, pod shape, and more), and every one gave the same pattern: one trait disappears entirely in the F1, then returns in about one quarter of the F2. That same 3 : 1 turning up across seven independent traits is what told him a real, repeatable mechanism was at work, not luck.

The figure below lays the same cross out with genotypes attached, so we can point back to it as we explain the numbers. We write the purple allele P and the white allele p (the convention is the first letter of the dominant trait: uppercase for dominant, lowercase for recessive). A true-breeding plant is homozygous (both alleles identical, from Chapter 5), so true-breeding purple is PP and true-breeding white is pp. Each F1 plant, having one allele from each parent, is Pp, a heterozygote (two different alleles).

Reading the pattern

Two features of that result need explaining, and together they point straight at the mechanism.

The white trait did not blend away. Before Mendel, most people believed in blending inheritance: the idea that a purple parent and a white parent should produce a medium, pale-purple child, with the two contributions mixed like paint and lost for good. If blending were true, a pure white flower could never reappear. But it did reappear in the F2, exactly as white as before. So the white factor (what we now call an allele) was not destroyed in the F1. It was carried along, hidden by purple, and shown again whenever it was on its own.

The reappearance was a clean fraction. White returned in close to exactly one quarter of the F2, giving the 3 : 1 ratio. A simple fraction like that is the fingerprint of a plain counting process. It suggests each parent passes its trait-factors in fixed, equal proportions, not in a blended blur.

Put the two together and a single rule accounts for both: each organism carries two factors (alleles) for the trait, and passes just one of them, chosen at random with equal chance, to each offspring. A hidden recessive is never lost, so it can return; and because the choice is a fair one-of-two, the offspring fall into clean fractions. That rule is the Law of Segregation.

The Law of Segregation (the formal statement)

Law of Segregation: every organism carries two alleles for each gene, and these two alleles separate (segregate) from each other during gamete formation, so that each gamete receives exactly one allele. When two gametes join at fertilization, the offspring again has two alleles, one from each parent.

This is not just a rule Mendel invented to fit his data. It is a direct consequence of meiosis, which you met in Chapter 4. The two alleles of a gene sit on homologous chromosomes (the matching pair, one inherited from each parent). In anaphase I of meiosis, the homologous chromosomes are pulled to opposite poles. That physical separation is exactly what sends the two alleles into different gametes. So a heterozygous Pp plant makes two kinds of gametes in equal numbers:

\[ \tfrac{1}{2}\,P \quad \text{and} \quad \tfrac{1}{2}\,p. \]

Now we can explain the observed 3 : 1 directly. The F1 are all Pp. When you self-cross them (Pp × Pp), each gamete from one parent meets each gamete from the other with equal chance. Combining \(\tfrac12 P\) and \(\tfrac12 p\) from each side gives the offspring probabilities

\[ P(\text{PP}) = \tfrac14, \qquad P(\text{Pp}) = \tfrac12, \qquad P(\text{pp}) = \tfrac14, \]

which is the genotype ratio 1 : 2 : 1. Because PP and Pp both look purple, the phenotype ratio is 3 : 1, matching Mendel's 705 : 224 count. The one-quarter white group is exactly the pp corner, the only way for two recessive alleles to meet.

Genotype ratio versus phenotype ratio

These two ratios describe the same offspring but count different things, and exams test the difference on purpose. Define both precisely:

  • A genotype is the exact allele combination an individual carries (PP, Pp, or pp). The genotype ratio lists how many offspring fall into each genotype. For Pp × Pp it is 1 PP : 2 Pp : 1 pp.
  • A phenotype is the observable trait (purple or white). The phenotype ratio lists how many offspring show each trait. For Pp × Pp it is 3 purple : 1 white, because the one PP and the two Pp all look the same.

The phenotype ratio is coarser: it merges genotypes that look alike. Read the question wording and answer the exact ratio it asks for.

The common monohybrid crosses (memorize this table)

For a simple dominant and recessive gene, only a few monohybrid crosses are possible, and each has a fixed pair of ratios. Memorizing them saves time on tests, because you can write the answer without drawing a Punnett square every time. Here A is the dominant allele and a is the recessive allele.

Worked examples

Example 1. Predict the whole cross from true-breeding parents. Mendel crossed a true-breeding purple pea (PP) with a true-breeding white pea (pp). Give the F1 genotype and phenotype, then predict the F2 genotype and phenotype ratios.

Solution. Each P-generation parent is homozygous, so each makes only one kind of gamete: the PP parent makes all \(P\) gametes, the pp parent makes all \(p\) gametes. Every F1 offspring is therefore Pp, and since purple is dominant, all F1 are purple. Now self-cross the F1 (Pp × Pp). By segregation each F1 makes \(\tfrac12 P\) and \(\tfrac12 p\) gametes, so the F2 genotypes are \(\tfrac14\) PP, \(\tfrac12\) Pp, \(\tfrac14\) pp. That is a genotype ratio of 1 PP : 2 Pp : 1 pp and a phenotype ratio of 3 purple : 1 white. Mendel's real F2 count of 705 : 224 matches this closely.

Aha: the recessive white phenotype skips the F1 entirely and returns in exactly one quarter of the F2. It reappears only when two recessive p alleles land in the same plant (pp). The allele was never destroyed in the F1, only masked.

Example 2. Read a ratio straight off the table. In the Rivera family, PTC tasting follows a simple dominant and recessive gene: T is the taster allele (dominant) and t is the non-taster allele (recessive). Rosa is a heterozygous taster (Tt). Suppose she is crossed with a homozygous taster (TT). Without drawing a Punnett square, give the genotype and phenotype ratios of the children.

Solution. This is the AA × Aa row of the table, with A standing for T. The genotype ratio is 2 TT : 2 Tt, which simplifies to 1 TT : 1 Tt. Every child carries at least one T allele, so the phenotype ratio is all tasters.

Aha: a genotype ratio of 1 : 1 here produces a phenotype ratio of "all one trait." Genotype variety does not always show up as phenotype variety, because a dominant allele hides whatever is paired with it.

Example 3. A cross where genotype and phenotype ratios happen to match. Elena is a heterozygous taster (Tt) and her husband David is a non-taster (tt). Give the genotype and phenotype ratios of their children, and connect the answer to segregation.

Solution. This is the Aa × aa row. Elena (Tt) makes \(\tfrac12 T\) and \(\tfrac12 t\) gametes by segregation. David (tt) makes only \(t\) gametes. Combining them, half the children are Tt and half are tt. The genotype ratio is 1 Tt : 1 tt and the phenotype ratio is 1 taster : 1 non-taster, so both ratios are 1 : 1. Here the two ratios agree, because each genotype gives a different phenotype (Tt is a taster, tt is not).

Aha: crossing a heterozygote with a homozygous recessive is called a test cross, and it turns each parental allele into a visible offspring trait. That is why it is the standard way to reveal whether a dominant-looking individual is AA or Aa, a technique developed fully in Chapter 7.

Common pitfalls

Pitfall 1: mixing up the genotype ratio and the phenotype ratio. For a heterozygous monohybrid cross (Aa × Aa) the genotype ratio is 1 : 2 : 1 and the phenotype ratio is 3 : 1. These are two different answers to two different questions. If a test asks for the genotypic ratio and you write 3 : 1, it is wrong even though the cross is right. Always read which ratio is being asked. (Warning for later chapters: in incomplete dominance and codominance, the heterozygote looks different from both homozygotes, so the phenotype ratio becomes 1 : 2 : 1, the same as the genotype ratio, not 3 : 1.)
Pitfall 2: thinking "dominant" means common, stronger, or better. Dominant only means that the allele masks the recessive allele in a heterozygote. It says nothing about how frequent the allele is in a population, and nothing about which trait is healthier. A recessive allele can be far more common than a dominant one. In Mendel's own data white flowers were fully able to breed; they were just recessive, not rare in any absolute sense.
Pitfall 3: expecting exactly 3 : 1 in a real family. The 3 : 1 ratio is a probability, not a guarantee. It shows up cleanly only over large numbers, which is why Mendel counted hundreds of plants. A single family of four children can easily be all tasters or all non-tasters. Each offspring is an independent event, so earlier children do not change the odds for the next one.

Connections

  • Chapter 4: Meiosis is the physical cause of segregation: homologous chromosomes separate in anaphase I, which sends the two alleles into different gametes.
  • Chapter 5: Alleles, Genotype & Phenotype supplies the vocabulary (dominant, recessive, homozygous, heterozygous) that this chapter puts to work.
  • Chapter 7: Punnett Squares & Monohybrid Crosses is the grid tool that computes these ratios and formalizes the test cross.
  • Chapter 8: Probability in Genetics turns segregation into the product and sum rules, letting you predict a single child's odds without a full square.
  • Chapter 9: Independent Assortment & Dihybrid Crosses extends segregation to two genes at once, giving the 9 : 3 : 3 : 1 ratio.
  • Chapter 10: Incomplete Dominance & Codominance is where the clean 3 : 1 phenotype ratio breaks and becomes 1 : 2 : 1.

★ Landmark it points toward

The chi-square goodness-of-fit test. When your real F2 count (say 705 : 224) is close to but not exactly 3 : 1, how do you decide whether the difference is just chance or evidence that segregation does not apply here? Statisticians answer this with the chi-square goodness-of-fit test, which compares observed counts to expected counts. You will meet it by name in later work; for now, just know it is the tool that decides whether data "fits" a predicted ratio.

Practice

P1. Mendel also studied seed shape, where round (R) is dominant and wrinkled (r) is recessive. He crossed a true-breeding round plant with a true-breeding wrinkled plant. Give the F1 phenotype, then predict the F2 genotype and phenotype ratios.

Answer

Calculation. P generation: RR × rr. The RR parent makes only \(R\) gametes and the rr parent makes only \(r\) gametes, so every F1 is Rr and all F1 are round. Self-crossing the F1 (Rr × Rr): each makes \(\tfrac12 R\) and \(\tfrac12 r\), giving F2 offspring of \(\tfrac14\) RR, \(\tfrac12\) Rr, \(\tfrac14\) rr. Genotype ratio 1 RR : 2 Rr : 1 rr; phenotype ratio 3 round : 1 wrinkled. (Mendel's real count was 5474 round : 1850 wrinkled, about 2.96 : 1.)

Why it works. The F1 are all heterozygous, so by the Law of Segregation each one produces equal numbers of \(R\) and \(r\) gametes. When two such plants cross, the two gametes combine independently, and the only way to get the recessive wrinkled phenotype is to pair \(r\) with \(r\), which happens \(\tfrac12 \times \tfrac12 = \tfrac14\) of the time. The other three quarters carry at least one \(R\) and look round.

Pattern. True-breeding dominant × true-breeding recessive always gives an all-dominant F1 and a 3 : 1 phenotype F2. Reach for this whenever a problem starts from two pure-breeding parents that differ in one trait. The genotype under that 3 : 1 is always 1 : 2 : 1. This is the signature of a single gene with simple dominance; if the F2 comes out 1 : 2 : 1 in phenotype instead, the gene is not simple dominant (it is incomplete dominance or codominance, Chapter 10).

P2. Use the common-crosses table. For the Rivera PTC gene (T taster dominant, t non-taster recessive), two heterozygous taster parents (Tt × Tt) have children. Give the genotype ratio and the phenotype ratio, and state what fraction of children are non-tasters.

Answer

Calculation. This is the Aa × Aa row of the table, with A standing for T. Genotype ratio 1 TT : 2 Tt : 1 tt; phenotype ratio 3 tasters : 1 non-taster. The fraction of non-tasters is the tt fraction, which is \(\tfrac14\).

Why it works. Each Tt parent makes \(\tfrac12 T\) and \(\tfrac12 t\) gametes by segregation. A child is a non-taster only if it receives a \(t\) from each parent, and \(\tfrac12 \times \tfrac12 = \tfrac14\). The taster group of three quarters is really one part TT plus two parts Tt, which is why the genotype ratio (1 : 2 : 1) carries more detail than the phenotype ratio (3 : 1).

Pattern. Heterozygote × heterozygote is the one cross that gives both a 1 : 2 : 1 genotype and a 3 : 1 phenotype. Recognize it whenever both parents show the dominant trait but a recessive child appears, because a recessive child forces both parents to carry a hidden recessive allele. Contrast it with the other table rows: only this cross produces the 3 : 1 phenotype, and only this cross can produce all three genotypes at once.

P3. In Mendel's flower-color cross, why did the white trait completely disappear in the F1 and then return in the F2? Explain in terms of alleles and dominance.

Answer

The F1 plants are all heterozygous (Pp): each received one purple allele (P) from the true-breeding purple parent and one white allele (p) from the true-breeding white parent. Purple is dominant, so in a heterozygote the P allele masks the p allele and the plant looks purple. The white allele is still present in every F1 plant. It was hidden, not lost. When the F1 self-cross, segregation puts the p allele into half of their gametes, so one quarter of the F2 inherit two p alleles (pp) and finally show the white phenotype. This reappearance is the direct evidence against blending inheritance: a blended trait could never come back unchanged, but a masked recessive allele can.

Watch / read preview before showing learners

↗ Khan Academy, The law of segregation ↗ Wikipedia, Mendelian inheritance ↗ Science Olympiad wiki, Heredity (the event scope)

Teaching notes

Opener: ask the class to predict what happens when a purple-flowered plant is crossed with a white-flowered one. Most will guess "light purple" (blending). Write that prediction on the board, then reveal Mendel's F1 (all purple) and let the contradiction sit. The whole chapter exists to explain why blending is wrong.

Board moves: draw the P, F1, F2 ladder from Figure 1 once, slowly, naming each generation. Then write the two ratios for Pp × Pp side by side, labeled GENOTYPE 1 : 2 : 1 and PHENOTYPE 3 : 1, and box the difference. That single board image is what most students misremember on the test.

What to hammer: segregation is meiosis, not a separate rule. Point back to anaphase I from Chapter 4 every time you say "the alleles separate." Students who see segregation as a physical event (chromosomes pulled apart) never forget it; students who memorize it as a sentence do.

The common stumble: answering the wrong ratio. Drill "which ratio did they ask for?" until it is automatic. A quick check for understanding: give them "Aa × aa" and ask for both ratios (1 : 1 genotype, 1 : 1 phenotype), then "Aa × Aa" (1 : 2 : 1 genotype, 3 : 1 phenotype) and make them notice the genotype and phenotype numbers are different in the second case.

Pacing: spend most of the time on Figure 1 and the genotype-versus-phenotype distinction. The table can be assigned to memorize as homework; it is a lookup, not a concept. Save the test-cross idea in Example 3 as the hook into Chapter 7.

Punnett Squares and Monohybrid Crosses

A grid that predicts the offspring of a cross: put each parent's gametes on the two axes, combine them in the cells, then read off the genotype and phenotype ratios.

Prerequisites: Chapter 6: Mendel and the Law of Segregation (each parent passes one of its two alleles to each gamete).
This chapter: Punnett squares and the monohybrid cross (one gene at a time).
Unlocks: Chapter 8: Probability in Genetics · Chapter 9: Independent Assortment and Dihybrid Crosses · Chapter 10: Incomplete Dominance and Codominance · Chapter 11: Multiple Alleles and Blood Types · Chapter 12: Sex-Linked Traits
🧬 Running example: the Rivera family. The parents Elena and David are both PTC tasters. PTC is a bitter chemical that some people taste and others cannot. Tasting is controlled by one gene with two alleles: T (taster) is dominant, t (non-taster) is recessive. Both Elena and David are Tt, so they taste PTC but each carries a hidden non-taster allele. Their children are Mateo, Lucia, and Sofia. We use this one cross, \(Tt \times Tt\), as the anchor for the whole chapter.

Three crosses, worked before any rules

Before stating a single rule, watch three real crosses get solved by drawing a grid. Each grid puts one parent's gametes (sex cells, eggs or sperm, each carrying just one of that parent's two alleles) along the top, and the other parent's gametes down the side, then combines them cell by cell. An allele is one version of a gene, here \(T\) (taster) or \(t\) (non-taster). A parent carrying two different alleles (\(Tt\)) is heterozygous; one carrying two of the same (\(TT\) or \(tt\)) is homozygous. Solve the crosses first. The section right after them names why the grid gives the ratios it does.

Example 1 (the anchor cross, \(Tt \times Tt\)). Elena (\(Tt\)) and David (\(Tt\)) are both PTC tasters. What genotypes and phenotypes can their children have, and in what ratios?

Each parent is heterozygous, so each makes two kinds of gamete in equal amounts: half carry \(T\) and half carry \(t\). Put David's gametes across the top and Elena's gametes down the side. Fill each cell by combining the column gamete with the row gamete.

Reading the four cells: one \(TT\), two \(Tt\), one \(tt\). Genotypic ratio \(1:2:1\). Since \(TT\) and \(Tt\) both taste, the phenotypic ratio is \(3\) taster \(: 1\) non-taster. So each child has probability \(\tfrac{3}{4}\) of being a taster and \(\tfrac{1}{4}\) of being a non-taster.

Aha: the two middle cells are both \(Tt\), but they come from opposite gamete combinations, \(T\) from Elena with \(t\) from David, and \(t\) from Elena with \(T\) from David. That is why the heterozygote count is \(2\) and not \(1\). The gametes are what fill the axes; the genotypes appear only inside the grid.

Example 2 (heterozygote crossed with recessive, \(Tt \times tt\)). Imagine one of the Rivera children grows up as a heterozygous taster (\(Tt\)) and marries a non-taster (\(tt\)). What do their children look like?

The \(tt\) parent is homozygous recessive, so every one of that parent's gametes carries \(t\). Both columns are labeled \(t\). The \(Tt\) parent still makes \(T\) and \(t\) gametes, so the rows are \(T\) and \(t\).

The cells are two \(Tt\) and two \(tt\). Genotypic ratio \(2\,Tt : 2\,tt\), which simplifies to \(1:1\). Phenotypic ratio \(1\) taster \(: 1\) non-taster. Notice the genotypic and phenotypic ratios are the same here, because each genotype is a different phenotype.

Aha: crossing with a homozygous recessive parent turns the recessive allele into a "reporter." Half the children are non-tasters, which can only happen if the taster parent handed down a \(t\). The recessive parent lets a hidden allele show up in the next generation.

Example 3 (a test cross to reveal a hidden genotype). A pea plant has yellow seeds. Yellow (\(Y\)) is dominant to green (\(y\)), so a yellow plant could be \(YY\) or \(Yy\). You cannot tell by looking. How do you find out which it is?

Cross the unknown yellow plant with a green plant. Green is recessive, so a green plant must be \(yy\). This is called a test cross: you cross an individual showing the dominant trait with a homozygous recessive individual, then read the offspring to deduce the unknown genotype. There are two possible outcomes.

If the yellow parent is \(YY\): every gamete carries \(Y\), so every child is \(Yy\) and yellow.

If the yellow parent is \(Yy\): the cross is \(Yy \times yy\), which is exactly the pattern from Example 2. Half the offspring are \(Yy\) (yellow) and half are \(yy\) (green), a \(1:1\) ratio.

So the offspring settle the question. If any green plant appears, the yellow parent must have contributed a \(y\) allele, which means it is \(Yy\). If every offspring is yellow (over a good number of them), the yellow parent is almost certainly \(YY\).

Aha: a homozygous recessive partner is the perfect test partner because it hides nothing. It always passes a recessive allele, so the offspring phenotypes report the unknown parent's second allele directly. Test crosses need an organism you can breed on purpose, like peas or fruit flies. In humans you cannot arrange crosses, so geneticists read genotypes from family trees instead (see Chapter 22: Pedigrees).

The pattern

All three grids share one structure. Each parent contributes exactly one allele to each gamete, and the grid lays out every equally likely pairing of one gamete from the top parent with one from the side parent. You then read the filled grid two ways: group the cells by their allele pair for the genotypic ratio, and group them by the visible trait for the phenotypic ratio. That grid is a Punnett square (named after the geneticist Reginald Punnett), and a one-gene cross like these is a monohybrid cross (mono means one gene, hybrid means a cross between two parents).

Watch the numbers that keep returning. Heterozygote by heterozygote (\(Tt \times Tt\)) gave \(3:1\) by phenotype and \(1:2:1\) by genotype. A cross against a homozygous recessive (\(Tt \times tt\)) gave a flat \(1:1\). A homozygous dominant against a homozygous recessive (\(YY \times yy\)) gave a single phenotype for every child. The next section explains why exactly those ratios fall out every time.

The method, and why it gives those ratios

A monohybrid cross follows a single gene through one mating. To predict the offspring we fill a Punnett square. First, the vocabulary it needs, each defined plainly on first use:

  • An allele is one version of a gene. Here the two alleles are \(T\) and \(t\).
  • A gamete is a sex cell (an egg or a sperm). It is haploid, meaning it carries only one copy of each gene, so a gamete holds a single allele, either \(T\) or \(t\), never both.
  • A genotype is the pair of alleles an individual carries, for example \(TT\), \(Tt\), or \(tt\). It is diploid, meaning two copies. Homozygous means the two alleles are the same (\(TT\) or \(tt\)); heterozygous means they differ (\(Tt\)).
  • A phenotype is the observable trait, here "taster" or "non-taster."
  • Dominant means an allele shows its trait even when only one copy is present, so it masks the recessive allele in a heterozygote. Recessive means the trait appears only when both alleles are recessive.

The single most important rule for reading a square: the labels on the rows and columns are gametes (single alleles), and the contents of the cells are genotypes (allele pairs). Each cell combines one gamete from the top parent with one gamete from the side parent. This works because of the law of segregation: a \(Tt\) parent's two alleles separate into different gametes, so that parent makes two kinds of gamete, \(T\) and \(t\), in equal numbers. That is why heterozygote by heterozygote fills the four cells \(TT, Tt, Tt, tt\). Two independent equal choices give four equally likely combinations, and the middle genotype \(Tt\) appears twice because it forms two ways (\(T\) from one parent with \(t\) from the other, or the reverse). Count those cells and the \(1:2:1\) genotype ratio, and the \(3:1\) phenotype ratio, drop out on their own.

After you fill the grid, you read two different ratios:

  • The genotypic ratio counts the genotypes among the cells. For \(Tt \times Tt\) it is \(1\,TT : 2\,Tt : 1\,tt\), written \(1:2:1\).
  • The phenotypic ratio counts the visible traits. Because \(TT\) and \(Tt\) both taste, the ratio is \(3\text{ taster} : 1\text{ non-taster}\), written \(3:1\).

These are two answers to two different questions. A test often asks for one specifically, so always check which one is wanted.

Common monohybrid crosses (simple dominant and recessive gene)
CrossGenotypic ratioPhenotypic ratio
\(Aa \times Aa\) (het × het)\(1\,AA : 2\,Aa : 1\,aa\)\(3\) dominant : \(1\) recessive
\(AA \times aa\) (hom dom × hom rec)all \(Aa\)all dominant
\(AA \times Aa\) (hom dom × het)\(2\,AA : 2\,Aa\)all dominant
\(Aa \times aa\) (het × hom rec)\(2\,Aa : 2\,aa\) (that is \(1:1\))\(1\) dominant : \(1\) recessive
Common pitfalls:
  • Putting genotypes on the axes. The rows and columns are gametes, each a single allele. A \(Tt\) parent contributes \(T\) or \(t\), so its axis reads "T" and "t," not "Tt." If you write \(Tt\) on an axis you are giving a gamete two alleles, which breaks the law of segregation. This same rule scales up: a dihybrid parent makes four gamete types, not two (see Chapter 9).
  • Confusing the genotypic and phenotypic ratios. For \(Tt \times Tt\) the genotype ratio is \(1:2:1\) but the phenotype ratio is \(3:1\). They are different numbers for the same cross. Read the question and answer the one asked. (For incomplete dominance and codominance the heterozygote looks different, so the two ratios become equal at \(1:2:1\). That is Chapter 10.)
  • Thinking "dominant" means "common" or "stronger." Dominant only means the allele masks the recessive in a heterozygote. It says nothing about how frequent the allele is in a population. A recessive allele can be far more common than a dominant one.
  • Reading the ratio as a guarantee. The \(3:1\) ratio is the expected proportion over many offspring. Each child is an independent event. Elena and David could have three tasters in a row and no non-taster. Past children do not change the odds for the next one.
  • Calling a carrier "affected." Elena and David are heterozygous tasters. They carry the recessive \(t\) allele but show the dominant taster phenotype, so they are unaffected carriers. A carrier never shows the recessive trait.

Connections

  • ← Chapter 6: Mendel and the Law of Segregation: segregation is why the axes are single alleles. Each parent's two alleles separate into different gametes.
  • → Chapter 8: Probability in Genetics: a Punnett square is a picture of probability. The \(3:1\) ratio means \(P(\text{taster}) = \tfrac{3}{4}\). The product and sum rules let you skip the grid for questions like "probability that all three children are tasters."
  • → Chapter 9: Independent Assortment and Dihybrid Crosses: two genes at once. Each parent now makes four gamete types, the grid is \(4 \times 4\), and \(Aa\,Bb \times Aa\,Bb\) gives the \(9:3:3:1\) phenotype ratio.
  • → Chapter 10: Incomplete Dominance and Codominance: the same square, but the heterozygote is its own visible phenotype, so the phenotype ratio equals the genotype ratio \(1:2:1\).
  • → Chapter 11: Multiple Alleles and Blood Types: the square still works with more than two alleles per gene, as in the ABO system.
  • → Chapter 12: Sex-Linked Traits: the axes include the X and Y chromosomes, which explains why some traits show up mostly in males.
  • ↔ Combinatorics and probability: filling the grid is the multiplication principle. Each cell is one choice of gamete from each parent.

★ Landmark it points toward

This chapter has no separate name-only landmark. The natural next step is simply more genes at once: the dihybrid cross in Chapter 9, and beyond that the trihybrid cross (three genes, an \(8 \times 8\) grid), which is a Nationals-level topic you rarely need to draw by hand.

Practice

P1 (predict a ratio). Miguel is a homozygous taster (\(TT\)) and Rosa is a heterozygous taster (\(Tt\)). Give the genotypic and phenotypic ratios of their children.

AnswerMiguel's gametes are all \(T\); Rosa's are \(T\) and \(t\). The cells are \(TT, TT, Tt, Tt\). Genotypic ratio \(2\,TT : 2\,Tt\) (that is \(1:1\)); phenotypic ratio all tasters, \(4:0\). This matches the "hom dom × het" row of the common-cross table.

P2 (set up a test cross). A pea plant has purple flowers. Purple (\(P\)) is dominant to white (\(p\)), so the plant is \(PP\) or \(Pp\). Design a single cross that reveals which, and state what each possible result tells you.

AnswerCross the purple plant with a white plant. White is recessive, so it must be \(pp\), and it can only give \(p\) gametes.

Why it works: the white partner contributes nothing but a recessive allele, so whatever the offspring show comes straight from the unknown parent's second allele. If the purple plant is \(PP\), every gamete is \(P\), so every offspring is \(Pp\) and purple. If the purple plant is \(Pp\), the cross is \(Pp \times pp\), which gives a \(1\) purple \(: 1\) white ratio. A single white offspring proves the purple parent carried a \(p\), so it is \(Pp\). If all offspring are purple across many seeds, the plant is almost certainly \(PP\).

Pattern (the test cross): to expose a hidden genotype behind a dominant phenotype, cross with a homozygous recessive. The recessive partner acts as a clean reporter. Reach for this whenever a dominant-looking individual could be either homozygous or heterozygous and you can breed it. When you cannot breed the organism, such as in humans, you read the genotype from a pedigree instead (Chapter 22).

P3 (which genotype). In the anchor cross Elena (\(Tt\)) × David (\(Tt\)), one of the children is a taster. What genotypes could that taster have, and in what ratio?

AnswerThe three taster cells of the square are \(TT, Tt, Tt\). So a taster child is either \(TT\) or \(Tt\), in the ratio \(1\,TT : 2\,Tt\). A taster child is twice as likely to be \(Tt\) as \(TT\). You cannot tell the two genotypes apart by looking, since both taste PTC; a test cross or a pedigree would be needed to distinguish them.

Watch / read preview before showing learners

Teaching notes

Opener: put the blank \(Tt \times Tt\) grid on the board and ask the class to label the axes before filling anything. Most will write "Tt" on the axes. Use that mistake to teach the one rule that matters: axes are gametes, cells are genotypes.

Board moves: draw Example 1 slowly, saying "one gamete from David, one from Elena" as you fill each cell. Circle the two \(Tt\) cells and ask why there are two of them (opposite parent contributions). Then do Example 2 next to it so students see how a homozygous recessive parent collapses one axis to a single allele.

What to hammer: the difference between the genotypic ratio (\(1:2:1\)) and the phenotypic ratio (\(3:1\)) for the same cross. Ask for both every time until it is automatic. Also hammer that dominant does not mean common.

The common stumble: students read the \(3:1\) ratio as a promise about a real family. Stress that each birth is independent and the ratio is an expectation over many offspring.

Pacing: spend most of the time on Examples 1 and 3. Example 1 builds the core skill; Example 3 (the test cross) is the reasoning students find hardest and the one that appears most on tests. Save the \(1 TT : 2 Tt\) point in P3 as a bridge into Chapter 8, where conditional questions get formal treatment.

Chapter 8: Probability in Genetics

A Punnett square is a probability table. Once you see that, you can answer questions about many children, and about several genes at once, without drawing a giant square.

Prerequisites: Chapter 7: Punnett Squares and Monohybrid Crosses
This chapter: Probability in Genetics
Unlocks: Chapter 9: Independent Assortment and Dihybrid Crosses (fast dihybrid math) · Chapter 22: Pedigrees (pedigree probability)

Start with three questions

Before any rule, work through three genetics questions and watch what the answers have in common. Elena and David Rivera are both heterozygous PTC tasters, genotype \(Tt\), where \(T\) = taster is dominant and \(t\) = non-taster is recessive. A gamete is a sex cell (egg or sperm) that carries one allele per gene. Every question below is about their children.

Question 1 (chance a child is affected by the recessive trait). What is the probability that a child of Elena and David is a non-taster, genotype \(tt\)?

Draw the \(Tt \times Tt\) Punnett square. Each parent makes two kinds of gamete, \(T\) and \(t\), so the square has four boxes, and each box is one equally likely combination of a gamete from each parent. The four boxes are \(TT\), \(Tt\), \(Tt\), \(tt\). A non-taster must be genotype \(tt\), and only 1 of the 4 boxes is \(tt\): \[ P(\text{non-taster}) = \frac{\text{non-taster boxes}}{\text{total boxes}} = \frac{1}{4}. \] This is exactly the shape of the question "what is the chance a child is affected by a recessive condition?" that runs through pedigrees later.

Question 2 (chance of a specific genotype from \(Tt \times Tt\)). For the same cross, what is the probability a child is the heterozygote \(Tt\)? What about \(TT\), and \(tt\)?

Read the same four boxes, but now count genotypes, not phenotypes. Two of the four boxes are \(Tt\), one is \(TT\), one is \(tt\): \[ P(Tt) = \frac{2}{4} = \frac{1}{2}, \qquad P(TT) = \frac{1}{4}, \qquad P(tt) = \frac{1}{4}. \] So the genotype outcomes come in the ratio \(1\,TT : 2\,Tt : 1\,tt\). A taster can be \(TT\) or \(Tt\), which is \(\frac{1}{4} + \frac{2}{4} = \frac{3}{4}\) of the boxes. That is where the 3:1 phenotype ratio comes from: it is the genotype ratio \(1:2:1\) with the two taster genotypes counted together.

Question 3 (combining two independent traits). Elena and David are also each heterozygous for the Rh blood factor, genotype \(Rr\), where \(R\) = Rh-positive is dominant and \(r\) = Rh-negative is recessive. The two genes are on different chromosomes, so they are independent. What is the probability a child is a non-taster and Rh-negative?

The slow way. The full cross is \(TtRr \times TtRr\). Each parent now makes four gamete types (\(TR\), \(Tr\), \(tR\), \(tr\)), so the honest Punnett square is 4 by 4, sixteen boxes, just to read off one probability.

The fast way. Handle each gene as its own small cross. From \(Tt \times Tt\), non-taster is \(\frac{1}{4}\). From \(Rr \times Rr\), Rh-negative is \(\frac{1}{4}\). Because the genes are independent, combine them by multiplying: \[ P(\text{non-taster and Rh-negative}) = \frac{1}{4} \times \frac{1}{4} = \frac{1}{16}. \] One box out of sixteen, without drawing sixteen boxes.

The pattern behind the three

Two moves did all the work, and every genetics probability question is built from them.

  • Add mutually exclusive outcomes. In Question 2, a taster is genotype \(TT\) or \(Tt\). One child cannot be both, so those outcomes are mutually exclusive, and you add their box counts: \(\frac{1}{4} + \frac{2}{4} = \frac{3}{4}\). This is the sum rule.
  • Multiply independent events. In Question 3, being a non-taster and being Rh-negative come from two independent genes, so you multiply their separate probabilities: \(\frac{1}{4} \times \frac{1}{4} = \frac{1}{16}\). This is the product rule.

The payoff is Question 3: the product and sum rules replace a big Punnett square. A two-gene cross would need a 16-box square, and a three-gene cross a 64-box square. Solving each gene alone and then multiplying gives the same answer with almost no drawing, and it keeps working when the square becomes impossible to draw.

The core idea: two rules and why they hold

Probability is a number from 0 to 1 that measures how likely an outcome is. When outcomes are equally likely (as Punnett boxes are), it is a box count: \[ P(\text{outcome}) = \frac{\text{number of favorable boxes}}{\text{total number of equally likely boxes}}. \] That is why a Punnett square is really a probability table: each box is one equally likely gamete-pair outcome, so the fraction of boxes showing a phenotype is that phenotype's probability.

The two rules

  • The product rule (AND, multiply): if two events are independent (one happening does not change the chance of the other), the probability that both happen is the product of their separate probabilities. \[ P(A \text{ and } B) = P(A) \times P(B). \] Signal words: "and", "both", "in a row", "all of".
  • The sum rule (OR, add): if two outcomes are mutually exclusive (they cannot both happen at the same time), the probability that one or the other happens is the sum of their separate probabilities. \[ P(A \text{ or } B) = P(A) + P(B). \] Signal words: "or", "either", "at least".

Why the rules hold (tie it to segregation)

Both rules come straight from the law of segregation. Segregation says each parent puts one randomly chosen allele into each gamete, like a fair coin flip. So the sum rule is already inside a single child: the coin flip lands on a taster genotype \(TT\) or \(Tt\), never both, and you add those mutually exclusive outcomes to get \(P(\text{taster}) = \frac{1}{4} + \frac{2}{4} = \frac{3}{4}\). The product rule comes from independent assortment (Chapter 9): genes on different chromosomes segregate independently, so the PTC coin flip and the Rh coin flip do not affect each other, and the chance both land a certain way is the product of the two chances.

Each child is an independent event

This is the rule people break most. Each fertilization is a fresh, independent draw of one gamete from each parent. A run of one phenotype does not change the odds for the next child. If a couple has had three non-taster children, the fourth child is still a taster with probability \(\frac{3}{4}\). The gametes have no memory of past children. Believing otherwise is the gambler's fallacy, and it is wrong in genetics for the same reason it is wrong for coin flips.

Worked examples

Example 1 (two children, the product rule across children). Elena and David plan two children. What is the probability that both are non-tasters?

Each child is an independent event, and each has \(P(\text{non-taster}) = \frac{1}{4}\). "Both" means the first child is a non-taster AND the second child is a non-taster, so multiply (the product rule): \[ P(\text{two non-tasters in a row}) = \frac{1}{4} \times \frac{1}{4} = \frac{1}{16}. \]

Aha: "and" plus "independent" means multiply. Two separate 1-in-4 events combine to 1-in-16, not 1-in-8. This is the same multiply move as combining two genes in Question 3, applied instead to two children.

Example 2 (at least one, two methods). For those same two planned children, what is the probability that at least one is a taster?

Method 1, the complement. "At least one taster" is the opposite of "no tasters at all". No tasters means both children are non-tasters, which we just found is \(\frac{1}{16}\). Subtract from 1: \[ P(\text{at least one taster}) = 1 - P(\text{both non-tasters}) = 1 - \frac{1}{16} = \frac{15}{16}. \] Method 2, the sum rule over cases. "At least one taster" splits into three mutually exclusive outcomes: both tasters, or first taster and second non-taster, or first non-taster and second taster. Multiply within each case (independent children), then add across the cases: \[ \underbrace{\tfrac{3}{4}\times\tfrac{3}{4}}_{\text{both tasters}} + \underbrace{\tfrac{3}{4}\times\tfrac{1}{4}}_{\text{taster, non}} + \underbrace{\tfrac{1}{4}\times\tfrac{3}{4}}_{\text{non, taster}} = \frac{9}{16} + \frac{3}{16} + \frac{3}{16} = \frac{15}{16}. \] Both methods give \(\frac{15}{16}\), which is a good sign they are correct.

Aha: "at least one" is almost always easier as \(1 - P(\text{none})\). Counting "none" is one product; counting "at least one" directly is several cases to add.

Example 3 (the forked-line method in full). Return to Question 3's cross \(TtRr \times TtRr\). Find the probability of every combined phenotype, and confirm it matches the 16-box square.

The forked-line method is the product rule drawn as a tree (see the forked-line figure above). Get each gene's probability from its own tiny cross, then multiply across the two genes for each combination. From \(Tt \times Tt\): taster \(\frac{3}{4}\), non-taster \(\frac{1}{4}\). From \(Rr \times Rr\): Rh-positive \(\frac{3}{4}\), Rh-negative \(\frac{1}{4}\).

  • Taster and Rh-positive: \(\frac{3}{4} \times \frac{3}{4} = \frac{9}{16}\).
  • Taster and Rh-negative: \(\frac{3}{4} \times \frac{1}{4} = \frac{3}{16}\).
  • Non-taster and Rh-positive: \(\frac{1}{4} \times \frac{3}{4} = \frac{3}{16}\).
  • Non-taster and Rh-negative: \(\frac{1}{4} \times \frac{1}{4} = \frac{1}{16}\).

The four combined phenotypes come out in the ratio \(9 : 3 : 3 : 1\), and the four fractions add to \(\frac{9}{16} + \frac{3}{16} + \frac{3}{16} + \frac{1}{16} = 1\). That is exactly what a 16-box square would give, with far less work.

Aha: get each gene's probability from its own tiny cross, then multiply across genes. This is the engine behind the dihybrid ratio in Chapter 9, and it scales to any number of independent genes.

Common pitfalls

Gambler's fallacy (the big one). After several children of one phenotype, students think the next child "must" swing the other way. It does not. Each child is an independent event, so the probability resets to the cross value every time. Three non-taster children in a row do not make a taster "due"; the fourth child is still a taster with probability \(\frac{3}{4}\).
Multiplying when you should add, or adding when you should multiply. Use the product rule (multiply) for "and" with independent events. Use the sum rule (add) for "or" with mutually exclusive outcomes. Ask two questions: are these joined by "and" or by "or"? Are the pieces independent, or mutually exclusive? A quick check: probabilities you multiply give a smaller number, while probabilities you add give a larger one.
Adding events that can both happen. The sum rule only works when the outcomes are mutually exclusive. "At least one taster in two children" is not simply \(\frac{3}{4} + \frac{3}{4}\) (that exceeds 1 and double counts the both-tasters case). Either split into mutually exclusive cases, or use the complement \(1 - P(\text{none})\).

Connections

  • ← Chapter 7: Punnett Squares and Monohybrid Crosses: the square is a table of equally likely outcomes. This chapter reads it as probability instead of drawing it.
  • ← Chapter 6: Mendel and the Law of Segregation: segregation is why each parent contributes one random allele, which is the coin flip behind every branch.
  • → Chapter 9: Independent Assortment and Dihybrid Crosses: the forked-line method turns the 9:3:3:1 dihybrid ratio into simple multiplication, no 16-box square needed.
  • → Chapter 22: Pedigrees: "what is the chance this couple's next child is affected?" is exactly a product-and-sum rule question run on a family tree.
  • ↔ Test crosses (Chapter 7): a test cross uses these odds in reverse. You breed an unknown dominant individual to a homozygous recessive and read the offspring ratio to deduce the hidden genotype.

★ Landmark it points toward

The binomial distribution. When you ask "in a family of \(n\) children, what is the probability exactly \(r\) are affected?", the answer is a binomial probability: it combines the product rule for one arrangement with a count of how many arrangements give \(r\) affected. This chapter is the two-child warm-up for that general formula.

Practice

P1 (an AND/OR combination). Elena and David (\(Tt \times Tt\)) plan two children. What is the probability the two children have the same PTC phenotype, that is, both tasters or both non-tasters?

Answer Both tasters: \(\frac{3}{4} \times \frac{3}{4} = \frac{9}{16}\). Both non-tasters: \(\frac{1}{4} \times \frac{1}{4} = \frac{1}{16}\). These two cases are mutually exclusive, so add: \(\frac{9}{16} + \frac{1}{16} = \frac{10}{16} = \frac{5}{8}\).

Why it works: "same phenotype" is really two separate matched outcomes joined by "or". Inside each outcome the two children are independent, so you multiply to get that outcome's probability. The two outcomes (both taster, both non) cannot happen at once, so they are mutually exclusive and you add them. Multiply first to build each block, then add the blocks.

Pattern, multiply within an AND, add across a mutually exclusive OR: when a problem describes several complete scenarios that each require multiple independent things to line up, compute each scenario with the product rule, then combine the scenarios with the sum rule. Reach for it whenever the wording is "either this whole situation or that whole situation". Contrast the two rules: the product rule joins pieces of one scenario, the sum rule joins separate scenarios.

P2 (the "next child" trap). Elena and David (\(Tt \times Tt\)) already have three children, and all three are non-tasters. What is the probability their fourth child is a taster?

Answer \(\frac{3}{4}\).

Why it works: each child is an independent event. Fertilization draws one fresh gamete from each parent, and the gametes carry no record of the earlier children. So the probability of a taster resets to the cross value, \(\frac{3}{4}\), for every child no matter what came before. The \(\frac{3}{4}\) comes from the parents' genotypes, not from the family's history. Do not confuse this question with "what is the probability of four non-tasters in a row from the start", which is \(\left(\frac{1}{4}\right)^4 = \frac{1}{256}\). That planning-ahead question multiplies four independent children. This question asks only about the single fourth child, given the first three already happened.

Pattern, independence and the gambler's fallacy: a run of past outcomes never changes the probability of the next independent trial. When events are independent, condition on nothing; the next event has its original probability. This is the opposite of a draw without replacement (for example, cards from a deck), where past outcomes really do change the pool. Genetics is with replacement: the parents' alleles are still there for the next child.

P3 (a forked-line two-gene probability). Elena and David are each \(Tt\) for PTC tasting and \(Rr\) for the Rh factor (\(R\) = Rh-positive dominant, \(r\) = Rh-negative recessive), the two genes independent. Using the forked-line method, find the probability a child is (a) a non-taster and Rh-negative, and (b) a taster and Rh-positive.

Answer (a) Non-taster and Rh-negative: \(\frac{1}{4} \times \frac{1}{4} = \frac{1}{16}\). (b) Taster and Rh-positive: \(\frac{3}{4} \times \frac{3}{4} = \frac{9}{16}\).

Why it works: the two genes assort independently, so the PTC outcome and the Rh outcome are independent events. That lets you treat each gene as its own small \(Tt \times Tt\) or \(Rr \times Rr\) cross, read off its single-gene probability, and multiply across the genes with the product rule. The forked-line method just draws this multiplication as a tree, so you never build the 4 by 4 square with its 16 boxes. Follow every branch and the four combined phenotypes come out in the ratio \(9 : 3 : 3 : 1\), which sums to 16 as a check.

Pattern, forked-line across independent genes: for any number of independent genes, solve each gene on its own, then multiply the per-gene probabilities for the combination you want. This scales to three or more genes where a Punnett square becomes impossible to draw (a trihybrid cross would need 64 boxes). It is the fast way to the dihybrid math in Chapter 9. It works only while the genes are independent; linked genes on the same chromosome break the simple product.

Watch / read preview before showing learners

↗ Khan Academy: Probabilities in genetics (article) ▶ Khan Academy: The sum rule and product rule (video)

Teaching notes

Opener: put the \(Tt \times Tt\) Punnett square on the board and ask the three opening questions in order before defining anything: "chance a child is a non-taster?" (box-count 1/4), "chance a child is specifically \(Tt\)?" (2/4), and "chance a child is a non-taster AND Rh-negative?" Let them try the third with a 16-box square, then reveal the \(\frac{1}{4}\times\frac{1}{4}\) shortcut. Only after those three do you name the two rules. Build the whole chapter from that one reframing: a Punnett square is a probability table.

Board moves: write the two rules in giant letters, "AND means multiply (independent), OR means add (mutually exclusive)." Point back to Question 2 to show the sum rule hiding inside one child (\(TT\) or \(Tt\)). Point back to Question 3 for the product rule across genes. Then do Example 1 for the product rule across children (\(\frac{1}{4} \times \frac{1}{4}\)), draw the two-children tree big for Example 2 and show both methods landing on \(\frac{15}{16}\), and close on the full forked-line in Example 3 so they feel the relief of not drawing a 16-box square.

What to hammer: the gambler's fallacy. Ask "the couple has three non-taster kids, what is the chance the next is a taster?" and let the wrong answer surface before correcting it. Say the phrase "each child is an independent event" until it is automatic. Contrast with drawing cards, where the pool really does change.

Common stumble: multiplying when they should add and vice versa. Make them label every step "AND (multiply)" or "OR (add)" and state whether the pieces are independent or mutually exclusive. The habit prevents most errors.

Pacing: spend 15 minutes on the three opening questions and naming the pattern, 15 minutes on the two rules and why they follow from segregation, 10 minutes on the gambler's fallacy, and 10 minutes on the multi-child and forked-line examples as the bridge to Chapter 9.

Independent Assortment and Dihybrid Crosses

Follow two genes at once. Because genes on different chromosomes sort into gametes independently, a doubly heterozygous parent makes four kinds of gamete, and the cross of two such parents gives the classic \(9:3:3:1\) phenotype ratio.

Prerequisites: Chapter 6: Mendel and the Law of Segregation (a parent passes one allele of a gene to each gamete) · Chapter 7: Punnett Squares and Monohybrid Crosses (axes are gametes, cells are genotypes) · Chapter 8: Probability in Genetics (the product rule for independent events).
This chapter: the \(4 \times 4\) dihybrid Punnett square and the observed \(9:3:3:1\) ratio, the forked-line shortcut, the Law of Independent Assortment (and why meiosis produces it), the four gametes of a dihybrid heterozygote, and the full genotype ratio.
Unlocks: Chapter 14: Epistasis and Lethal Alleles (when one gene masks another and distorts the \(9:3:3:1\) ratio). A second exception, gene linkage, is named at the end of this chapter.
🧬 Running example: the Rivera family. The parents Elena and David are followed for two independent traits at once. Trait one is PTC tasting: allele \(T\) (taster) is dominant, \(t\) (non-taster) is recessive. Trait two is the Rh blood factor: allele \(R\) (Rh-positive) is dominant, \(r\) (Rh-negative) is recessive. Both Elena and David are \(TtRr\), meaning heterozygous for both genes. So each is a taster and Rh-positive, but each carries a hidden \(t\) and a hidden \(r\). The two genes sit on different chromosomes, so they pass to a child independently. We use the cross \(TtRr \times TtRr\) as the anchor for the whole chapter.

Start with a real cross

Before any law, work one two-gene cross all the way through and read the ratio straight off the grid. A dihybrid cross follows two genes through one mating (di means two, hybrid means a cross between two parents). A dihybrid heterozygote is an individual that is heterozygous for both genes, like \(TtRr\). Elena and David Rivera are both \(TtRr\): tasters who are Rh-positive, each carrying a hidden \(t\) and a hidden \(r\). What phenotypes appear among their children, and in what proportions?

Example 1 (the anchor cross, \(TtRr \times TtRr\)). Elena (\(TtRr\)) and David (\(TtRr\)) are both PTC tasters and both Rh-positive. What are the phenotype proportions among their possible children?

Each parent makes four kinds of gamete, \(TR\), \(Tr\), \(tR\), and \(tr\), because each gamete carries one allele of each gene (we prove there are exactly four, and why, once the law is stated below). Put David's four gametes across the top and Elena's four down the side, then fill each of the \(16\) cells by combining the column gamete with the row gamete. Sort the alleles of each gene inside a cell (capital first), so a cell holding \(t,T\) for the PTC gene and \(R,r\) for the Rh gene is written \(TtRr\).

Count the shaded classes: \(9\) taster and Rh-positive, \(3\) taster and Rh-negative, \(3\) non-taster and Rh-positive, \(1\) non-taster and Rh-negative. So each child has probability \(\tfrac{9}{16}\) of showing both dominant traits, \(\tfrac{3}{16}\) and \(\tfrac{3}{16}\) for the two mixed classes, and \(\tfrac{1}{16}\) of showing both recessive traits. The observed ratio is

\[ 9 \;:\; 3 \;:\; 3 \;:\; 1 \]
Aha: the double-recessive corner (\(ttrr\), pink) is the rarest outcome at \(\tfrac{1}{16}\), and it appears only when both parents happen to pass their hidden \(t\) and hidden \(r\) in the same gamete. Two heterozygous, fully unaffected parents can still produce a child showing both recessive traits. That is why recessive conditions can appear "out of nowhere."

This \(9:3:3:1\) is a real, measured ratio, not just a classroom prediction. Gregor Mendel crossed pea plants that were heterozygous for two traits at once, seed shape (\(R\) round dominant, \(r\) wrinkled recessive) and seed color (\(Y\) yellow dominant, \(y\) green recessive), a cross \(RrYy \times RrYy\). Among the \(556\) offspring he counted \(315\) round-yellow, \(108\) round-green, \(101\) wrinkled-yellow, and \(32\) wrinkled-green. A perfect \(9:3:3:1\) out of \(556\) would be about \(313 : 104 : 104 : 35\), so his real counts land right on the pattern. The same ratio shows up whenever two simple dominant/recessive genes are followed together, whatever the traits are.

The pattern: two \(3:1\) crosses multiplied

Read the grid one gene at a time and the \(9:3:3:1\) stops looking mysterious. Look only at PTC: \(12\) of the \(16\) cells carry at least one \(T\) (tasters) and \(4\) are \(tt\) (non-tasters), a \(3:1\) split, exactly the monohybrid \(Tt \times Tt\) result from Chapter 7. Look only at Rh: again \(12\) Rh-positive to \(4\) Rh-negative, another \(3:1\). The four two-trait classes are just these two \(3:1\) splits multiplied together. Because the genes behave independently, a child's PTC outcome and Rh outcome are independent events, so you combine them with the product rule (multiply the probabilities of independent events):

\[ \tfrac{3}{4}\cdot\tfrac{3}{4}=\tfrac{9}{16},\quad \tfrac{3}{4}\cdot\tfrac{1}{4}=\tfrac{3}{16},\quad \tfrac{1}{4}\cdot\tfrac{3}{4}=\tfrac{3}{16},\quad \tfrac{1}{4}\cdot\tfrac{1}{4}=\tfrac{1}{16} \]

Those four fractions are exactly \(9:3:3:1\) out of \(16\). This is the forked-line method: fork on the first gene's \(3:1\), fork again on the second gene's \(3:1\), then multiply along each path. It replaces the whole \(16\)-box grid with a small tree, and it scales, because a trihybrid is just three \(3:1\) splits multiplied.

The Law of Independent Assortment

The pattern above rests on one law. The Law of Independent Assortment (Mendel's second law): the alleles of two genes that sit on different chromosomes separate into gametes independently of one another. In plain terms, which PTC allele a gamete receives (\(T\) or \(t\)) does not change which Rh allele it receives (\(R\) or \(r\)). The two coin flips are unlinked, which is exactly what lets you treat each gene as its own \(3:1\) cross and multiply.

Why it holds: meiosis. During meiosis, in metaphase I the homologous chromosome pairs line up at the cell's middle and each pair orients at random relative to the others. The pair carrying the PTC gene can point either way independently of the pair carrying the Rh gene, so a \(T\) is equally likely to travel with \(R\) or with \(r\). Independent orientation of chromosome pairs at metaphase I is the physical cause of independent assortment.

A dihybrid heterozygote makes four kinds of gamete, in equal amounts. The PTC gene gives each gamete either \(T\) or \(t\); the Rh gene independently gives either \(R\) or \(r\). Combine the two choices and you get four gamete types. The clean way to list them is to multiply the two allele pairs the way you multiply two binomials, a step often called FOIL (First, Outer, Inner, Last, the order you multiply the terms of two two-term sums):

\[ (T + t)(R + r) \;=\; TR \;+\; Tr \;+\; tR \;+\; tr \]

So the four gametes are \(TR\), \(Tr\), \(tR\), \(tr\), each with probability \(\tfrac{1}{4}\), which is why the grid above had four labels on each axis. A monohybrid heterozygote makes only two gametes; a dihybrid makes \(2 \times 2 = 4\); a trihybrid would make \(2^3 = 8\). The count of gamete types is \(2^{(\text{number of heterozygous genes})}\).

The full genotype ratio. The phenotype ratio hides more detail than the genotype ratio. The sixteen cells contain nine distinct genotypes in the ratio

\[ 1 : 2 : 1 : 2 : 4 : 2 : 1 : 2 : 1 \]

for \(TTRR : TTRr : TTrr : TtRR : TtRr : Ttrr : ttRR : ttRr : ttrr\). The single most common genotype is the double heterozygote \(TtRr\), which fills \(4\) of the \(16\) cells, because several gamete combinations rebuild it. You get this same \(1:2:1:2:4:2:1:2:1\) genotype ratio by multiplying the two single-gene genotype ratios \((1:2:1)\times(1:2:1)\), the same "multiply two monohybrids" move applied to genotypes instead of phenotypes.

Worked examples

Example 2 (the shortcut for one phenotype class). Using the same cross \(TtRr \times TtRr\), find the probability that a child is a taster but Rh-negative, without drawing the \(16\)-cell grid.

Split the problem into two independent single-gene questions and multiply, because the genes assort independently. For the PTC gene, \(Tt \times Tt\) gives \(\tfrac{3}{4}\) tasters. For the Rh gene, \(Rr \times Rr\) gives \(\tfrac{1}{4}\) Rh-negative (only the \(rr\) class is Rh-negative). The child must be a taster AND Rh-negative, so use the product rule:

\[ P(\text{taster and Rh-negative}) = \tfrac{3}{4}\cdot\tfrac{1}{4} = \tfrac{3}{16} \]

This matches the three yellow cells in the Example 1 grid, confirming the shortcut. You can read off every class the same way: taster and Rh-positive is \(\tfrac34\cdot\tfrac34=\tfrac{9}{16}\); non-taster and Rh-positive is \(\tfrac14\cdot\tfrac34=\tfrac{3}{16}\); non-taster and Rh-negative is \(\tfrac14\cdot\tfrac14=\tfrac{1}{16}\).

Aha: you never need a \(4 \times 4\) square on a timed test. Solve each gene as its own \(3:1\) monohybrid cross, then multiply the two answers. A big dihybrid grid is really just two small crosses laid over each other.

Example 3 (a dihybrid test cross). Suppose one Rivera child is a taster and Rh-positive but their genotype is unknown (it could be \(TtRr\), \(TTRr\), \(TtRR\), or \(TTRR\), among others). To reveal it, cross this person with a non-taster who is Rh-negative (\(ttrr\)). What offspring appear if the unknown parent is \(TtRr\)?

A test cross mates the individual of interest with a fully homozygous recessive partner. The \(ttrr\) partner makes only one gamete type, \(tr\), so it hides nothing and simply reports the other parent's gametes directly. The \(TtRr\) parent makes the four gametes \(TR\), \(Tr\), \(tR\), \(tr\) in equal amounts, so the offspring are:

The four offspring phenotypes appear in a \(1:1:1:1\) ratio: taster Rh-positive, taster Rh-negative, non-taster Rh-positive, non-taster Rh-negative. This even \(1:1:1:1\) split is the signature of a dihybrid heterozygote. If instead the unknown parent had been \(TTRR\), every offspring would be a taster and Rh-positive (all \(TtRr\)), because that parent has only \(TR\) to give.

Aha: a test cross turns a parent's gamete list into visible offspring. The homozygous recessive partner contributes only recessive alleles, so the offspring phenotypes are a direct picture of the tested parent's gametes. A \(1:1:1:1\) result proves the parent made four gamete types in equal amounts, so it was a dihybrid heterozygote.
Common pitfalls:
  • Giving a dihybrid parent only two gametes. A \(TtRr\) parent makes four gamete types (\(TR, Tr, tR, tr\)), not two. Every axis of the dihybrid square has four labels. Forgetting the cross combinations \(Tr\) and \(tR\) is the most common dihybrid error. Use FOIL: \((T+t)(R+r)\) has four terms.
  • Writing a gamete with two alleles of the same gene. A gamete carries exactly one allele per gene, so a valid gamete is \(TR\) or \(tr\), never \(TtR\) or \(TTRr\). The law of segregation forbids a gamete from holding both alleles of one gene.
  • Confusing the genotype ratio with the phenotype ratio. The phenotype ratio is \(9:3:3:1\) (four visible classes), but the genotype ratio is \(1:2:1:2:4:2:1:2:1\) (nine genotypes). Read which one the question asks for.
  • Assuming \(9:3:3:1\) always holds. The \(9:3:3:1\) ratio requires two things: two simple dominant/recessive genes, AND independent assortment. If the two genes sit close together on the same chromosome they are linked and do not assort independently, so the ratio shifts (linkage is named below). If one gene masks the other (epistasis), the four classes collapse into modified ratios like \(9:3:4\) or \(12:3:1\); see Chapter 14.
  • Reading a ratio as a promise. \(9:3:3:1\) is the expected proportion over many offspring, not a guarantee for one family. Each child is an independent event, so the Riveras could have three children who are all taster and Rh-positive.

Connections

  • ← Chapter 6: Mendel and the Law of Segregation: segregation (the first law) explains why each gene contributes one allele; independent assortment (the second law) explains why the two genes do so separately.
  • ← Chapter 7: Punnett Squares and Monohybrid Crosses: the same grid method, scaled from \(2 \times 2\) to \(4 \times 4\). The dihybrid square is literally four monohybrid squares tiled together.
  • ← Chapter 8: Probability in Genetics: the multiply-two-monohybrids shortcut IS the product rule for independent events. \(P(\text{both}) = P(\text{gene 1}) \times P(\text{gene 2})\).
  • → Chapter 10: Incomplete Dominance and Codominance: when a heterozygote is its own visible phenotype, the single-gene split becomes \(1:2:1\) instead of \(3:1\), which changes the dihybrid ratio too.
  • → Chapter 14: Epistasis and Lethal Alleles: when one gene masks another, the four \(9:3:3:1\) classes merge into distorted ratios like \(9:3:4\), \(12:3:1\), \(15:1\), and \(9:7\).
  • ↔ Probability and combinatorics: filling a Punnett square is the multiplication principle, and the \(9:3:3:1\) numbers are the four products in \((3+1)(3+1) = 9+3+3+1\) when you keep the two cross terms separate.

★ Landmarks it points toward

Gene linkage (name only). Everything here assumes the two genes assort independently, which is true only when they are on different chromosomes (or far apart on the same one). Two genes that sit close together on the same chromosome are linked: they tend to travel into a gamete together, so the four gamete types are no longer equally likely and the offspring ratio drifts away from \(9:3:3:1\). Crossing over during meiosis breaks linked pairs apart at a rate that depends on the distance between the genes, which is the basis of gene mapping. This is a Science Olympiad topic named here but not worked in this course.

The trihybrid cross (name only). The natural extension is more genes at once: \(AaBbCc \times AaBbCc\), where each parent makes \(2^3 = 8\) gametes, the grid is \(8 \times 8 = 64\) cells, and the phenotype ratio is \(27:9:9:9:3:3:3:1\). Under Science Olympiad rules the trihybrid cross is a Nationals-only topic and is rarely tested, and crosses above three genes are not allowed on tests. You almost never draw a trihybrid by hand; the multiply-the-monohybrids shortcut handles it in a few lines.

Practice

P1 (predict a dihybrid ratio). In Mendel's peas, seed shape has alleles \(R\) (round, dominant) and \(r\) (wrinkled, recessive), and seed color has alleles \(Y\) (yellow, dominant) and \(y\) (green, recessive). Cross two plants that are both \(RrYy\). Give the phenotype ratio of the offspring.

AnswerThe offspring appear in the ratio \(9\) round yellow \(: 3\) round green \(: 3\) wrinkled yellow \(: 1\) wrinkled green, that is \(9:3:3:1\).

Why it works: both parents are dihybrid heterozygotes, so each makes four gametes (\(RY, Ry, rY, ry\)) in equal amounts, giving a \(4 \times 4\) grid of \(16\) cells. Both genes are simple dominant/recessive and they assort independently, so the sixteen cells sort into the four phenotype classes as \(9:3:3:1\). This is the same structure as the Rivera \(TtRr \times TtRr\) cross; only the trait names differ. This is the exact cross whose real counts (315 : 108 : 101 : 32 in the opening) revealed the second law.

Pattern (the dihybrid heterozygote cross): any cross of two dihybrid heterozygotes with two simple dominant/recessive genes gives \(9:3:3:1\). Reach for it the moment you see a doubly heterozygous parent crossed with an identical partner. It fails when the genes are linked or when one gene masks the other (epistasis), so check those before assuming \(9:3:3:1\).

P2 (shortcut for a specific probability). In the Rivera cross \(TtRr \times TtRr\), use the multiply-two-monohybrids shortcut to find the probability that a child is a non-taster who is Rh-positive.

Answer\(P(\text{non-taster}) = \tfrac{1}{4}\) (the \(tt\) class from \(Tt \times Tt\)); \(P(\text{Rh-positive}) = \tfrac{3}{4}\) (the \(R\_\) class from \(Rr \times Rr\)). Multiply: \(\tfrac{1}{4}\cdot\tfrac{3}{4} = \tfrac{3}{16}\).

Why it works: the two genes assort independently, so being a non-taster and being Rh-positive are independent events. The product rule says the probability of two independent events both happening is the product of their separate probabilities. Each gene is solved as its own \(Tt \times Tt\) or \(Rr \times Rr\) monohybrid cross, so you only ever handle a \(3:1\) split, never the full \(16\)-cell grid. This answer matches the three blue cells in the Example 1 square.

Pattern (per-gene product rule): to find any one phenotype class of a multi-gene cross, compute each gene's single-gene probability (\(\tfrac34\) for a dominant phenotype, \(\tfrac14\) for a recessive one, from a heterozygous cross) and multiply. This is the same move for dihybrid, trihybrid, or any number of independent genes; the only change is multiplying more factors together. It works only when the genes are independent, which is exactly what fails under linkage.

P3 (a dihybrid test cross, working backward). A person who is a taster and Rh-positive is test-crossed to a non-taster who is Rh-negative (\(ttrr\)). The offspring come out about half taster Rh-positive and half non-taster Rh-positive, with no Rh-negative children at all. What was the tested parent's genotype?

Answer\(TtRR\). Solve one gene at a time. For PTC, half the offspring are tasters and half are non-tasters, a \(1:1\) split, so the parent passed both \(T\) and \(t\), meaning it is \(Tt\). For Rh, every offspring is Rh-positive and none is Rh-negative, so the parent could only pass \(R\), meaning it is homozygous \(RR\). Combining the two genes gives \(TtRR\).

Watch / read preview before showing learners

↗ Khan Academy: The law of independent assortment ▶ Amoeba Sisters: Dihybrid Crosses and the 9:3:3:1 Ratio (YouTube) ↗ Nature Scitable: Law of Independent Assortment (definition)

Teaching notes

Opener (examples-first): do not state the law first. Build the \(TtRr \times TtRr\) grid, count \(9, 3, 3, 1\) out loud, then show Mendel's real pea counts (315 : 108 : 101 : 32) so students see the ratio is measured in nature, not invented. Only after the pattern is visible do you name the Law of Independent Assortment and tie it to random orientation of chromosome pairs at metaphase I of meiosis.

Board moves: build the \(4 \times 4\) square once, slowly, writing the four gametes on each axis first. Then draw a box around each \(2 \times 2\) quadrant and show it is a monohybrid \(Rr \times Rr\) square, so the whole grid is four monohybrid crosses tiled together. Shade the four phenotype classes in four colors and count \(9, 3, 3, 1\).

What to hammer: the forked-line shortcut. After the grid is done once, insist students solve every later dihybrid by multiplying two \(3:1\) crosses, because a \(4 \times 4\) grid is slow and error-prone on a timed test. Also hammer that four gametes come from two heterozygous genes, and that \(2^n\) gametes come from \(n\) heterozygous genes.

The common stumble: students drop the mixed gametes \(Tr\) and \(tR\), then get a wrong ratio. Circle those two gametes every time. The second stumble is assuming \(9:3:3:1\) always holds; flag now that linkage and epistasis break it, previewing Chapter 14.

Pacing: spend most of the time on the opening grid and Example 2 (the shortcut), since the shortcut is what they will actually use. Save Example 3 (the test cross) for when the \(1:1:1:1\) signature can be contrasted with the \(9:3:3:1\) of the heterozygous cross.

Incomplete Dominance and Codominance

Cross a red flower with a white flower and Mendel's rules predict red offspring. They come out pink. Cross a red bull with a white cow and the calf is neither: it wears both colors at once. Two ways a single gene breaks the clean dominant and recessive pattern.

Prerequisites: Chapter 5: Alleles, Genotype and Phenotype (genotype is the allele pair, phenotype is the visible trait) · Chapter 7: Punnett Squares and Monohybrid Crosses (the \(2\times2\) grid and the \(1:2:1\) genotype ratio).
This chapter: genes where neither allele is fully dominant, so the heterozygote looks different from both homozygotes.
Unlocks: Chapter 11: Multiple Alleles and Blood Types (ABO blood type is codominance with three alleles).
