🧬 Real documented cases: unlike PTC tasting, these patterns need traits where you can actually see the heterozygote. We use the standard textbook cases: 4 o'clock flowers and snapdragons (red \(\times\) white gives pink) for incomplete dominance, and roan cattle and human ABO blood type AB for codominance. We tie back to the Rivera family through ABO: if Elena is type A and David is type B, a child can be type AB, showing both antigens at once.

Start with two crosses

Before any definitions, work through two real crosses whose results are surprising. In Chapter 7 a heterozygote \(\times\) heterozygote cross of one gene always gave a \(3:1\) phenotype ratio, because the heterozygote hid inside the dominant type. Watch what these two crosses do instead. Do not reach for a rule yet, just read the outcomes.

Cross 1 (4 o'clock flowers, red \(\times\) white). A true-breeding red 4 o'clock plant (\(I^R I^R\)) is crossed with a true-breeding white one (\(I^W I^W\)). What does the F1 look like? Then the F1 plants are crossed with each other. What is the F2 phenotype ratio? (The F1 generation is the offspring of the first parental cross; the F2 generation is the offspring of an F1 \(\times\) F1 cross.)

Every red parent gamete carries \(I^R\); every white parent gamete carries \(I^W\). So every F1 plant is \(I^R I^W\). Here is the first surprise: the F1 are all pink, not red and not white. A brand new in-between color appears, one that neither parent had. Now cross the pink F1 plants: \(I^R I^W \times I^R I^W\). Each pink parent makes \(I^R\) and \(I^W\) gametes in equal amounts, so this is an ordinary heterozygote cross.

Reading the four F2 cells: one \(I^R I^R\) (red), two \(I^R I^W\) (pink), one \(I^W I^W\) (white). The genotype ratio is \(1:2:1\). Because each genotype is a different color, the phenotype ratio is also \(1\) red \(: 2\) pink \(: 1\) white, that is \(1:2:1\). Compare this with the PTC cross \(Tt \times Tt\) in Chapter 7, which gave \(3:1\).

Aha: the red and white phenotypes come back unchanged in the F2. So the alleles did not permanently mix like paint. They stayed separate the whole time. Pink was only how the heterozygote looked, and the original red and white reappear intact in the next generation. We name this pattern below, and return to why the pink is not truly a blend of pigments.

Cross 2 (shorthorn cattle, red \(\times\) white). A red bull (\(C^R C^R\)) is crossed with a white cow (\(C^W C^W\)). What does the F1 calf look like, and how is it different from the pink flowers in Cross 1?

Every red parent gamete carries \(C^R\); every white parent gamete carries \(C^W\). So every F1 calf is \(C^R C^W\). But here the coat is not a blended pink. It is roan: a coat with both red hairs and white hairs mixed together. Look closely and you see individual red hairs next to individual white hairs. Both parent colors are fully present at once, side by side. This is a different outcome from the flowers, where one new color replaced both.

Now cross two roan cattle: \(C^R C^W \times C^R C^W\). This is again a heterozygote cross, so the genotype ratio is \(1\,C^R C^R : 2\,C^R C^W : 1\,C^W C^W\). Each genotype is its own visible phenotype, so the offspring are \(1\) red \(: 2\) roan \(: 1\) white, that is \(1:2:1\). Notice the second surprise: this is the very same \(1:2:1\) we just got from the flowers, and again it is not \(3:1\).

Aha: roan is not a light red and it is not a pink. Under a hand lens you can point to a red hair and, right next to it, a white hair. The flowers gave one new phenotype in between (pink); the cattle keep both parent phenotypes as separate features (red and white hairs together). That difference is the whole test for telling the two patterns apart, and we name them next.

The pattern behind the two crosses

Both crosses started the same way (one homozygote \(\times\) the other homozygote) and both self-crosses gave the same \(1:2:1\), not the \(3:1\) of Chapter 7. Two things stayed constant across them:

  • The heterozygote was always its own visible phenotype. In the flowers it was a new color in between (pink). In the cattle it was both parent colors shown together (roan). Either way, you can look at an individual and tell it is a heterozygote, which you could never do under simple dominance.
  • So no phenotype classes merged, and the phenotype ratio matched the genotype ratio, \(1:2:1\). Under simple dominance the heterozygote hides inside the dominant homozygote, so two classes collapse into one and you get \(3:1\). Here nothing hides, so nothing collapses.

The two crosses differ in only one way, and that difference is the name of each pattern. When the heterozygote is one new intermediate (pink), the pattern is called incomplete dominance. When the heterozygote shows both parent phenotypes fully and separately (roan), it is called codominance. Now we can define both precisely and say why the ratio must be \(1:2:1\).

The core idea

Mendel studied genes with a clean split: one allele was fully dominant (it showed even in a single copy and masked the other) and one was fully recessive (it showed only in a double copy). Many real genes do not work that way. When neither allele is fully dominant, the heterozygote (an individual carrying two different alleles) has a phenotype that differs from both homozygotes. The two crosses above are the two versions of this.

The two patterns, each defined plainly:

  • Incomplete dominance: the heterozygote is an intermediate blend, a single new phenotype partway between the two homozygotes. Case: 4 o'clock flowers. Red (\(I^R I^R\)) \(\times\) white (\(I^W I^W\)) gives pink (\(I^R I^W\)). The superscript notation with a capital \(I\) is common for incomplete dominance, but see the pitfalls: the symbol style does not decide the classification.
  • Codominance: both alleles are fully and separately visible in the heterozygote at the same time. Nothing blends. Case: roan cattle, whose coat carries both red hairs and white hairs mixed together (\(C^R C^W\)). Another case is human blood type AB, which carries both the A and the B markers.
  • Antigen: a marker molecule on the surface of a cell that the immune system can recognize. In ABO blood type, the \(I^A\) allele makes the A antigen and the \(I^B\) allele makes the B antigen.

Why the heterozygote often looks intermediate (incomplete dominance). Frequently one working allele makes only about half as much product as two working alleles. In 4 o'clock flowers, \(I^R I^R\) makes a full amount of red pigment (deep red), \(I^W I^W\) makes none (white), and the heterozygote \(I^R I^W\) makes about half (pink). This is a dosage effect, not a mixing of two pigments. That is why the red and white came back intact in the F2: the alleles never merged.

Why codominance shows both. Each allele makes its own distinct product, and both products appear in the heterozygote. Roan cattle grow red hairs (from \(C^R\)) and white hairs (from \(C^W\)) side by side. A blood type AB person makes both the A antigen and the B antigen. Neither product is diluted or merged.

The single most important exam point. In simple dominance a heterozygote \(\times\) heterozygote cross gives a \(3:1\) phenotype ratio, because the heterozygote looks like the dominant homozygote and the two classes merge. In incomplete dominance and codominance the heterozygote is its own visible phenotype, so nothing merges. The phenotype ratio then equals the genotype ratio:

\[ \text{heterozygote} \times \text{heterozygote} \;\Rightarrow\; \text{genotype } 1:2:1 \;=\; \text{phenotype } 1:2:1 \quad(\text{not } 3:1). \]

Aha: the Punnett square from Chapter 7 does not change at all. A heterozygote cross still gives the genotype ratio \(1:2:1\). What changes is only how you translate those three genotypes into visible phenotypes. When the heterozygote is visible, all three genotypes are three different phenotypes.

Worked examples

Example 1 (codominance in humans, ABO blood type, previewing Chapter 11). Why is human blood type AB an example of codominance, and how does it connect to the Rivera family?

The ABO blood group gene has three alleles: \(I^A\), \(I^B\), and \(i\). The two alleles \(I^A\) and \(I^B\) are codominant with each other. The \(I^A\) allele makes the A antigen and the \(I^B\) allele makes the B antigen, where an antigen is a marker molecule on the surface of a red blood cell. A person whose genotype is \(I^A I^B\) makes both the A antigen and the B antigen. Their blood type is AB. Both alleles are fully and separately expressed, so this is codominance, not incomplete dominance. There is no single blended "in-between" antigen.

In the Rivera family, suppose Elena is type A and David is type B. A child who inherits \(I^A\) from one parent and \(I^B\) from the other is type AB, carrying both antigens at once. The full three-allele ABO system, including the recessive \(i\) allele for type O, is the subject of Chapter 11: Multiple Alleles and Blood Types.

Aha: blood type AB is the everyday proof of codominance in people. The A and B antigens are both physically present as separate molecules. That is different from a pink flower, where there are no separate red and white pigment patches, only a single reduced amount of one pigment. The wiki warns that although the ABO alleles are written with the superscript style (\(I^A\), \(I^B\)) that is often used for incomplete dominance, ABO is codominance. The symbol style does not tell you which one it is.

Example 2 (work backward from a ratio). A gardener crosses two 4 o'clock plants and the offspring come out close to \(1\) red \(: 1\) pink, with no white plants at all. What were the two parents' genotypes and phenotypes?

The offspring that appear are red (\(I^R I^R\)) and pink (\(I^R I^W\)); the class that is missing is white (\(I^W I^W\)). Each offspring takes one allele from each parent, so read the parents off the offspring. Every offspring has at least one \(I^R\), so one parent must donate \(I^R\) to all of them, which makes that parent \(I^R I^R\) (red). The pink offspring also carry an \(I^W\), so the other parent must be able to donate \(I^W\); but no white offspring appear, so that parent cannot be \(I^W I^W\). The only genotype that donates both \(I^R\) and \(I^W\) yet is not \(I^W I^W\) is \(I^R I^W\) (pink). Check the cross \(I^R I^R \times I^R I^W\): the offspring are \(\tfrac12\,I^R I^R\) (red) and \(\tfrac12\,I^R I^W\) (pink), that is \(1\) red \(: 1\) pink, no white. So the parents were one red (\(I^R I^R\)) and one pink (\(I^R I^W\)).

Why it works: in incomplete dominance every genotype is a different phenotype, so the offspring phenotype ratio is exactly the offspring genotype ratio. That lets you read each parent's possible gametes straight off the offspring. The colors that are present tell you which alleles each parent could donate; the color that is absent (white) rules out a gamete pairing, here that both parents carry \(I^W\). The split is \(1:1\) rather than \(1:2:1\), which is the signature of a homozygous parent: one parent donates only a single kind of allele, so only two offspring classes can form.

Pattern (read the parents off the offspring): to deduce parents from an incomplete-dominance or codominance cross, list the offspring genotypes (they equal the phenotypes here), then ask which parent gametes produce exactly those and none of the missing ones. A \(1:2:1\) offspring ratio flags two heterozygotes; a \(1:1\) ratio flags one homozygous parent; all-identical offspring flag a true-breeding parent. An absent class rules out a gamete pairing. This backward reading works only because the heterozygote is visible. Under simple dominance the hidden heterozygote makes several parent genotypes give the same offspring look, so you could not pin the parents down this cleanly.

Common pitfalls:
  • Confusing incomplete dominance with codominance. Look only at the heterozygote. If it is one new intermediate phenotype that is neither parent (pink), it is incomplete dominance, a blend. If both parental phenotypes are fully visible at the same time (roan, or the A and B antigens together), it is codominance. A blend means one merged phenotype; codominance means two separate phenotypes shown together.
  • Classifying by the allele symbols. The convention of an \(I\) with superscripts for incomplete dominance and plain capitals for codominance is loose. The wiki states plainly that the symbol style does not reliably tell the two apart, and ABO uses \(I^A\), \(I^B\) yet is codominance. Classify by the described phenotype, never by the letters.
  • Expecting a \(3:1\) ratio from a heterozygote cross. A \(3:1\) phenotype ratio happens only under simple dominance, when the heterozygote hides inside the dominant homozygote. Here the heterozygote is visible, so the phenotype ratio equals the genotype ratio, \(1:2:1\). Always \(1:2:1\), never \(3:1\), for a one-gene heterozygote cross with incomplete dominance or codominance.
  • Thinking the alleles permanently mix (blending inheritance). Incomplete dominance is not the old blending theory. The alleles stay discrete and separate normally. Red and white reappear intact in the F2 of a pink \(\times\) pink cross, which could never happen if the alleles had truly merged.

Connections

  • ← Chapter 5: Alleles, Genotype and Phenotype: this chapter is entirely about the mapping from genotype to phenotype. The genotypes and the genotype ratio are the ordinary Mendelian ones. Only how each genotype shows up as a visible trait changes.
  • ← Chapter 7: Punnett Squares and Monohybrid Crosses: the same \(2\times2\) grid and the same genotype ratio \(1:2:1\) for a heterozygote cross. The one change is that all three genotypes are now three visible phenotypes, so the phenotype ratio is \(1:2:1\) instead of \(3:1\).
  • ← Chapter 6: Mendel and the Law of Segregation: these patterns are still fully Mendelian. Alleles still segregate one to a gamete and stay discrete. Incomplete dominance and codominance describe the phenotype, not the inheritance of the alleles themselves.
  • → Chapter 11: Multiple Alleles and Blood Types: the ABO system is codominance combined with a third allele. \(I^A\) and \(I^B\) are codominant with each other, and \(i\) (type O) is recessive to both.
  • ↔ Chapter 8: Probability in Genetics: the \(1:2:1\) phenotype ratio means each F2 4 o'clock plant has probability \(\tfrac{1}{4}\) red, \(\tfrac{1}{2}\) pink, and \(\tfrac{1}{4}\) white.

★ Landmark it points toward

This chapter has no separate name-only landmark. The natural next step is the ABO blood group, which combines codominance with more than two alleles for one gene, in Chapter 11: Multiple Alleles and Blood Types. Beyond that, real dominance relationships get richer still, but those involve more than one gene and are separate later topics.

Practice

P1 (predict an incomplete-dominance ratio). In snapdragons, red (\(I^R I^R\)) and white (\(I^W I^W\)) show incomplete dominance, and the heterozygote \(I^R I^W\) is pink. A pink snapdragon is crossed with a white snapdragon. Give the phenotype ratio of the offspring.

AnswerThe pink parent (\(I^R I^W\)) makes \(I^R\) and \(I^W\) gametes; the white parent (\(I^W I^W\)) makes only \(I^W\) gametes. The offspring are \(I^R I^W\) (pink) and \(I^W I^W\) (white) in a \(1:1\) ratio. Phenotype ratio \(1\) pink \(: 1\) white. Because each genotype is its own color, the genotype ratio and the phenotype ratio are the same, \(1:1\).

P2 (classify incomplete vs codominance from a description). A farmer crosses a black chicken with a white chicken. All the offspring have feathers speckled with distinct black patches and distinct white patches (this feather pattern is called "erminette"). Is this incomplete dominance or codominance? How would the offspring have looked if it were the other pattern?

AnswerThis is codominance. The heterozygote shows both parental colors fully and separately, as distinct black patches and white patches in the same bird. If it were incomplete dominance instead, the heterozygote would be one new uniform intermediate shade, gray, with no separate black or white regions.

Why it works: the classification depends only on what the heterozygote looks like. Codominance means both homozygous phenotypes appear at the same time in the same individual, as separate features (both feather colors, both antigens, both hair colors). Incomplete dominance means one new blended phenotype partway between the two, with neither parent's exact appearance present. Distinct patches of each color prove both alleles are fully expressed, so the trait is codominant. A single uniform in-between shade would have proven a blend.

Pattern (classify by the heterozygote, not the symbols): to tell the two apart, look only at the heterozygote's phenotype. If you can still see each homozygous phenotype separately, it is codominance. If the heterozygote is one new intermediate that is neither parent, it is incomplete dominance. Reach for this whenever a problem describes a cross where the offspring differ from both parents. Warning: the allele-symbol style (\(I\) with superscripts versus plain capitals) does not tell you which pattern it is; the wiki notes this convention is loose, so decide from the described phenotype alone.

P3 (why \(1:2:1\), not \(3:1\)). In 4 o'clock flowers, pink \(\times\) pink gives \(1\) red \(: 2\) pink \(: 1\) white. In PTC tasting from Chapter 7, \(Tt \times Tt\) gives \(3\) tasters \(: 1\) non-taster. Both are heterozygote \(\times\) heterozygote crosses of a single gene. Why is the phenotype ratio \(1:2:1\) in one case and \(3:1\) in the other?

AnswerIn both crosses the genotype ratio is exactly the same: \(1\) homozygote-of-one-kind \(: 2\) heterozygote \(: 1\) homozygote-of-the-other-kind, that is \(1:2:1\). The only difference is how many phenotypes those three genotypes produce.

Why it works: with simple dominance (PTC), the heterozygote \(Tt\) looks exactly like the dominant homozygote \(TT\), because both taste. So two of the three genotype classes collapse into one phenotype: \((1+2)\) tasters \(: 1\) non-taster \(= 3:1\). With incomplete dominance or codominance, the heterozygote \(I^R I^W\) has its own visible phenotype (pink), different from both homozygotes. Nothing collapses, so each genotype is its own phenotype and the \(1:2:1\) stays \(1:2:1\).

Pattern (count the visible phenotypes): for any one-gene heterozygote \(\times\) heterozygote cross, start from the genotype ratio \(1:2:1\). If the heterozygote is distinguishable (incomplete dominance or codominance), the phenotype ratio equals the genotype ratio, \(1:2:1\). If the heterozygote is masked (simple dominance), add its count to the dominant homozygote and you get \(3:1\). Same genotypes every time; the phenotype ratio just depends on whether the heterozygote is visible.

Watch / read preview before showing learners

↗ Khan Academy: Multiple alleles, incomplete dominance, and codominance ▶ Amoeba Sisters: Incomplete Dominance, Codominance, Polygenic Traits, and Epistasis (YouTube) ↗ Bozeman Science: Incomplete Dominance and Codominance

Teaching notes

Opener: show a photo (or the figure) of a pink 4 o'clock flower and a roan cow side by side. Ask "which one is a blend and which one shows both colors?" Let students argue for two minutes before naming the two patterns. The pink versus roan contrast is the entire chapter in one image.

Board moves: draw the pink \(\times\) pink Punnett square from Cross 1 and the roan \(\times\) roan square next to it. Point out that both give the genotype ratio \(1:2:1\), and both give the phenotype ratio \(1:2:1\), for the same reason. Then draw the PTC \(Tt \times Tt\) square from Chapter 7 underneath and circle how the \(Tt\) and \(TT\) cells merge into one taster phenotype, giving \(3:1\).

What to hammer: two things. First, the classification test: blend equals incomplete dominance, both-shown-separately equals codominance, and you decide from the phenotype, not the symbols. Second, that a heterozygote cross gives \(1:2:1\) here, not \(3:1\), because the heterozygote is visible.

The common stumble: students assume every heterozygote cross gives \(3:1\) out of habit from Chapter 7. Make them state, every time, whether the heterozygote is visible before they write a ratio.

Pacing: spend most of the time on Cross 1 and Cross 2 and the contrast figure between them. Save the ABO example as the bridge into Chapter 11, where the third allele \(i\) and the four blood types get full treatment.

Multiple Alleles and Blood Types

A gene can have more than two alleles in a population, even though each person still carries only two. The human ABO blood group is the classic case: three alleles, two of them codominant.

Prerequisites: Chapter 10: Incomplete Dominance and Codominance (the heterozygote can be its own visible phenotype, so the phenotype ratio can equal the genotype ratio).
This chapter: multiple alleles (more than two per gene), the ABO blood group, the Rh factor, and blood-type logic for paternity.
Unlocks: Chapter 22: Pedigrees and Paternity (using blood types to trace inheritance through a family and to exclude a possible parent).
🧬 Running example: the Rivera family. Elena is type A, her husband David is type B, and their children include Mateo (type AB) and Sofia (type O). Two of their children are blood types that neither parent has. The cases below explain how, and set up the \(I^A i \times I^B i\) anchor cross reused across the chapter.

Start with two cases

Before naming any rule, work through two blood-type puzzles about the Rivera family. Both look impossible at first, and both are explained by a single new idea.

Case 1 (a type A parent and a type B parent). Elena is type A. David is type B. Their son Mateo is type AB and their daughter Sofia is type O. How can two parents who are type A and type B produce a child who is type AB, and another who is type O, when neither parent is AB or O?

Start with the child whose blood type pins down its genotype. Type O comes from a genotype with no A or B marker at all, written \(ii\). For Sofia to be \(ii\), she must have received one \(i\) from each parent. So Elena, though she looks type A, must secretly carry an \(i\), giving her the genotype \(I^A i\). David, though type B, must also carry an \(i\), giving him \(I^B i\). Each parent is therefore heterozygous, so each makes two kinds of gamete in equal amounts: Elena makes \(I^A\) and \(i\); David makes \(I^B\) and \(i\). Put David's gametes across the top and Elena's down the side, then combine one gamete from each parent in every cell.

Reading the four cells: \(I^A I^B\) is type AB, \(I^A i\) is type A, \(I^B i\) is type B, and \(ii\) is type O. So Elena and David can have children of all four blood types, in the ratio A : B : AB : O = \(1:1:1:1\). Each child has probability \(\tfrac{1}{4}\) of each type. Mateo (AB) and Sofia (O) are simply two of the four possible outcomes.

Aha: this one cross produces all four ABO blood types at once, which no ordinary two-allele gene can do. Two things make it work. The parents together bring three different alleles to the table (\(I^A\), \(I^B\), and \(i\)), and the combination \(I^A I^B\) is its own visible type (AB), showing both markers at once. Because every genotype here maps to a different phenotype, the phenotype ratio equals the genotype ratio, \(1:1:1:1\), not the \(3:1\) of a simple dominant cross.

Case 2 (which blood types are possible). Two type A parents are expecting a child. Which blood types could the child have? In particular, could the child be type O? Could it be type B or type AB?

A type A parent is either \(I^A I^A\) or \(I^A i\), because the recessive \(i\) can hide behind the A marker. If both parents happen to be \(I^A i\), then each can pass either \(I^A\) or \(i\). Their children can be \(I^A I^A\) (type A), \(I^A i\) (type A), or \(ii\) (type O). So two type A parents can indeed have a type O child, exactly the way Elena and David had type O Sofia. But neither type A parent carries an \(I^B\) allele at all, so no child of theirs can be type B or type AB. The child can only be type A or type O.

Aha: to find which blood types are possible, list the alleles each parent could pass, then combine them. A blood type is possible for the child only if the required alleles are actually available from the parents. Two type A parents can never make an \(I^B\) out of nothing, so type B and type AB are ruled out, while a hidden \(i\) in each parent still allows a type O child. This same "is the allele available?" reasoning is how a blood test can rule out a possible parent, which we return to in the worked examples.

The pattern

Both cases turn on the same discovery: the ABO gene comes in more than the two allele versions we saw in earlier chapters. Pulling the cases apart gives the rule this chapter is built on.

  • A gene can have more than two alleles in the population, even though each person still carries exactly two. The ABO gene has three alleles in people (\(I^A\), \(I^B\), \(i\)), but any one person, being diploid, holds only two of them at a time.
  • The three alleles follow a dominance hierarchy. Both \(I^A\) and \(I^B\) are dominant over \(i\) (written \(I^A = I^B > i\)), so \(i\) is hidden whenever an \(I^A\) or \(I^B\) is present. That is why a type A person can be \(I^A i\) and a type B person can be \(I^B i\), each carrying a hidden \(i\).
  • \(I^A\) and \(I^B\) are codominant with each other. Neither hides the other. A person with both makes both markers fully and separately, so \(I^A I^B\) is its own blood type, AB.

The rest of the chapter makes each of these precise, then applies them.

The core idea

Every gene we studied before this chapter had exactly two alleles (for example \(T\) and \(t\) for PTC tasting). That is not a rule of genetics, only a convenience of the first examples. Many genes, like ABO, come in multiple alleles.

  • A multiple-allele gene is a gene that exists in more than two allele versions across a population. An allele is one version of a gene.
  • Any one individual is still diploid (carries two copies of each gene), so a single person carries only two of those alleles at a time. The extra variety lives in the population, not in one genotype.

The human ABO blood group is the standard example. One gene, but three alleles in the population:

  • \(I^A\): builds the A antigen on the surface of red blood cells. An antigen is a marker molecule (here a specific sugar) that the immune system can recognize.
  • \(I^B\): builds the B antigen.
  • \(i\): builds no antigen. It is recessive to both \(I^A\) and \(I^B\).

The key interaction is codominance, not incomplete dominance. \(I^A\) and \(I^B\) are codominant: in a person who carries both, both antigens are made, fully and separately, so the blood type is AB (both A and B markers are present at once). This is different from incomplete dominance, where the heterozygote is an intermediate blend. Type AB blood is not a blend of A and B. It is A and B side by side. The traditional notation writes the alleles as \(I\) with a superscript letter, but that notation does not make it incomplete dominance. It is codominance.

The recessive \(i\) does not blend either. It is simply masked whenever an \(I^A\) or \(I^B\) allele is present, exactly like an ordinary recessive allele. So a person can be blood type A while secretly carrying an \(i\).

Putting the alleles together gives four blood types (four phenotypes) from six genotypes:

Because \(I^A\) and \(I^B\) are codominant, the heterozygote \(I^A I^B\) is its own visible phenotype (type AB). As in Chapter 10, that means the phenotype ratio can equal the genotype ratio instead of collapsing to a 3:1. You will see this directly in the anchor cross below.

The Rh factor: a second, simpler gene

Blood typing usually reports two things at once, such as "A positive." The plus or minus is the Rh factor, a different red-cell antigen controlled by a separate gene with ordinary dominant and recessive alleles:

  • \(Rh^+\) (Rh positive) is dominant. It builds the Rh antigen.
  • \(Rh^-\) (Rh negative) is recessive. It builds no Rh antigen.

So an Rh positive person is \(Rh^+ Rh^+\) or \(Rh^+ Rh^-\), and an Rh negative person is only \(Rh^- Rh^-\). The full blood type combines the two independent genes: type A with \(Rh^+ Rh^-\) reads as A positive. ABO and Rh are inherited independently, so you can treat them as two separate one-gene problems and then report both answers together.

Worked examples

Example 1 (adding the Rh factor). David is B positive with genotype \(I^B i\) for ABO and \(Rh^+ Rh^-\) for Rh. His partner is O positive with genotype \(ii\) and \(Rh^+ Rh^-\). Can they have a child who is O negative?

Treat the two genes separately, then combine. For ABO, the cross is \(I^B i \times ii\). David's gametes are \(I^B\) and \(i\); the partner's are both \(i\). The children are \(I^B i\) (type B) or \(ii\) (type O), a \(1:1\) ratio. So a type O child is possible.

For Rh, both parents are \(Rh^+ Rh^-\), which is exactly a heterozygous cross like \(Aa \times Aa\). The offspring are \(1\,Rh^+Rh^+ : 2\,Rh^+Rh^- : 1\,Rh^-Rh^-\). One quarter are \(Rh^- Rh^-\), which is Rh negative. So an Rh negative child is possible, from two Rh positive parents, because each parent carries a hidden \(Rh^-\).

Combining the two independent genes, the chance of an O negative child is \(P(\text{O}) \times P(\text{Rh negative}) = \tfrac{1}{2} \times \tfrac{1}{4} = \tfrac{1}{8}\).

Aha: ABO and Rh are two separate genes, so "A positive" is really two phenotypes reported together. Solve each gene as its own one-gene problem, then multiply the probabilities (the product rule), the same way a dihybrid cross handles two independent genes. Two Rh positive parents can absolutely have an Rh negative child, because Rh positive can hide a recessive \(Rh^-\).

Example 2 (paternity, which parents can and cannot have an O child). A newborn is type O. The mother is type A. Two men are considered as the father: one is type AB, the other is type B. Blood type alone, which man can be excluded?

A type O child is \(ii\), so the child received an \(i\) allele from each parent. That means both parents must carry at least one \(i\). The mother is type A, and she must be \(I^A i\) (a type A carrier of \(i\)), which is consistent. Now test each man.

The type AB man has genotype \(I^A I^B\). He carries no \(i\) at all, so he can never pass an \(i\). He cannot father a type O child. He is excluded.

The type B man could be \(I^B i\). If so, he can pass an \(i\), so a type O child is possible. He is not excluded.

Aha: blood typing gives exclusion, not proof. It can prove a man is not the father (the type AB man here), but it cannot prove that the type B man is the father. Many unrelated men are also type B carriers of \(i\), so being consistent is not the same as being the parent. This is why paternity is settled by DNA today; blood type only rules people out. The rule to remember: an O child (\(ii\)) is impossible unless both parents carry an \(i\), so two type AB parents can never have a type O child, and neither can any parent who is \(I^A I^B\).
Common pitfalls:
  • Calling ABO "incomplete dominance." The \(I\)-with-a-superscript notation fools people, but \(I^A\) and \(I^B\) are codominant, not incompletely dominant. Type AB shows the A antigen and the B antigen, both fully present. It is not an intermediate blend the way a pink flower is between red and white in incomplete dominance.
  • Assuming a type A person is \(I^A I^A\). Type A can be \(I^A I^A\) or \(I^A i\), because \(i\) is recessive and stays hidden. You cannot read the full genotype off the blood type for type A or type B. Only type AB and type O reveal the genotype exactly.
  • Thinking dominant means common. In many populations the recessive \(i\) allele is actually the most frequent ABO allele, which is why type O is so common. Dominant only means "masks the recessive in a heterozygote." It says nothing about how frequent an allele is.
  • Reading blood-type consistency as proof of parentage. A blood test can exclude a possible parent, never confirm one. "Not excluded" means "still possible," not "is the parent."
  • Mixing the ABO and Rh genes. They are two separate genes on the way to a full blood type. Solve ABO and Rh independently, then combine (multiply probabilities). An Rh negative child from two Rh positive parents is normal, because Rh positive can carry a hidden \(Rh^-\).

Connections

  • ← Chapter 10: Incomplete Dominance and Codominance: ABO is the textbook case of codominance among multiple alleles. Because the heterozygote \(I^A I^B\) is visibly type AB, the phenotype ratio equals the genotype ratio, exactly the Chapter 10 pattern.
  • ← Chapter 7: Punnett Squares and Monohybrid Crosses: the same grid still works with three alleles. Each parent still contributes only one allele per gamete, so a two-by-two square is enough for any single cross.
  • ↔ Chapter 8: Probability in Genetics: the \(1:1:1:1\) ratio means each blood type has probability \(\tfrac{1}{4}\), and combining ABO with Rh uses the product rule for independent events.
  • ↔ Chapter 9: Independent Assortment and Dihybrid Crosses: ABO plus Rh is two independent genes, so it behaves like a dihybrid problem solved one gene at a time.
  • → Chapter 22: Pedigrees and Paternity: blood types are read through family trees, and the exclusion rule (an O child needs an \(i\) from both parents) is a standard way to rule out a possible parent.

★ Landmark it points toward

This chapter has no separate name-only landmark. Two real extensions are worth naming in one line each: there are more than 40 human blood group systems beyond ABO and Rh (for example MN, Duffy, and Kell), and hemolytic disease of the newborn is a medical consequence of Rh incompatibility, when an Rh negative mother carries an Rh positive fetus. Both are named here only so you see the map ahead; neither is drilled at this level.

Practice

P1 (deduce the possible child blood types). Rosa is type A with genotype \(I^A i\). Miguel is type O. What blood types can their children have, and in what ratio?

AnswerMiguel is type O, so his genotype must be \(ii\) and every one of his gametes carries \(i\). Rosa (\(I^A i\)) makes \(I^A\) and \(i\) gametes in equal amounts. The children are \(I^A i\) (type A) or \(ii\) (type O), so the ratio is type A : type O = \(1:1\).

Why it works: to find the possible child blood types, first write each parent's gametes straight from their genotype, then combine gametes in a small Punnett square, then translate each resulting genotype back into a phenotype using the ABO table. The type O parent is the key. Since \(ii\) can only give an \(i\), the children's second allele comes entirely from Rosa, so exactly half inherit her \(I^A\) and half inherit her hidden \(i\).

Pattern (genotype to gametes to phenotype): for any blood-type cross, do not guess from the phenotypes. Convert phenotype to genotype (using the ABO table, keeping both possibilities when a type is ambiguous), list gametes, cross them, then convert back. Reach for this whenever a problem asks which blood types are possible in the children. A homozygous recessive parent (type O, \(ii\)) is especially informative because it can only pass \(i\), so any dominant antigen in a child must have come from the other parent.

P2 (a paternity exclusion). A baby is type AB. The mother is type O. A man who is type A is named as the possible father. Can blood type exclude him?

AnswerYes, he is excluded, but by the mother, not the father. A type AB baby is \(I^A I^B\), so it needs an \(I^A\) from one parent and an \(I^B\) from the other. The mother is type O, genotype \(ii\), so she can only pass \(i\). She cannot supply either \(I^A\) or \(I^B\). A type O mother can never have a type AB child, no matter who the father is, so the whole scenario is impossible and every man is beside the point.

Why it works: before testing a possible father, check that the mother can even produce the required child. The baby's genotype must be built from one allele from each parent. If the known parent cannot supply an allele the child clearly has, the case is broken at the start. Here the child must carry both \(I^A\) and \(I^B\), yet the \(ii\) mother contributes neither.

Pattern (exclusion, not proof): to test parentage by blood type, ask whether each claimed parent could supply the child's alleles. If a required allele cannot come from either listed parent, someone is excluded. Blood typing can only exclude a possible parent; it can never prove parentage, because many unrelated people share the same genotype. Contrast the two mirror cases: a type O child needs an \(i\) from both parents (so an \(I^A I^B\) parent is excluded), and a type AB child needs \(I^A\) and \(I^B\) from opposite parents (so an \(ii\) parent is excluded).

P3 (genotype from phenotype). Mateo is type AB and his sister Sofia is type O. Write each one's ABO genotype, and say which siblings' genotypes are certain and which are not.

AnswerType AB is always \(I^A I^B\), and type O is always \(ii\). Both are certain, because AB and O are the only two blood types whose genotype is fixed. (A type A or type B sibling would be ambiguous, either homozygous or a carrier of \(i\).) Both genotypes are also consistent with parents \(I^A i\) and \(I^B i\), the Rivera anchor cross.

Watch / read preview before showing learners

↗ Khan Academy: Multiple alleles, incomplete dominance, and codominance ▶ Amoeba Sisters: Alleles and Genes (codominance and multiple alleles) (YouTube) ↗ Nature Scitable: Multiple alleles and the ABO blood groups

Teaching notes

Opener: ask the class why two type O parents cannot have a type A child, but two type A parents sometimes can have a type O child. The puzzle forces out the two ideas of the chapter: an O genotype is fixed (\(ii\)), and a type A parent can secretly carry an \(i\).

Board moves: draw the anchor cross \(I^A i \times I^B i\) and label the axes with single alleles (\(I^A\), \(i\) on one side; \(I^B\), \(i\) on the other), stressing that each gamete still carries only one allele even though three alleles exist in the family. Fill the grid, then translate each cell's genotype into a blood type out loud. Circle the AB cell and name it codominance.

What to hammer: codominance versus incomplete dominance (AB is both antigens, not a blend); that type A and type B are genotype-ambiguous while AB and O are not; and that "dominant" does not mean "common" (the recessive \(i\) is often the most frequent allele). Also hammer exclusion versus proof for paternity.

The common stumble: students write type A as \(I^A I^A\) automatically and forget the \(I^A i\) carrier. Keep returning to Sofia being type O, which proves both Elena and David carry a hidden \(i\).

Pacing: spend most of the time on the opening anchor cross (the \(1:1:1:1\) cross in Case 1) and the paternity exclusion (Example 2), which is the reasoning students find hardest and the one that appears most on tests. Treat the Rh factor (Example 1) as a short second pass: it is just an ordinary one-gene problem run alongside ABO. Save the exclusion logic as the bridge into Chapter 22.

Sex-Linked Traits

Some genes sit on the sex chromosomes (X and Y), so their inheritance depends on the sex of the parent and the child. This is why traits like red-green color blindness and hemophilia show up far more often in males.

Prerequisites: Chapter 4: Meiosis (the sex chromosomes separate into gametes, so each parent passes one sex chromosome to each child) · Chapter 7: Punnett Squares and Monohybrid Crosses (the grid method) · Chapter 8: Probability in Genetics (turning a square into a probability).
This chapter: genes carried on the X and Y chromosomes, and why an X-linked recessive trait lands mostly in males.
Unlocks: Chapter 22: Pedigrees (tracing an X-linked trait through a family tree) · Chapter 23: Genetic Disorders and the Frontier (the real X-linked disorders).
🧬 Running example: the Rivera family. We follow red-green color blindness, a real X-linked recessive trait. The gene sits on the X chromosome and has two alleles: \(X^C\) (normal color vision, dominant) and \(X^c\) (color blindness, recessive). Elena is a carrier (\(X^C X^c\)): she sees color normally but carries one copy of the recessive allele. David has normal vision (\(X^C Y\)). Their children are Mateo, Lucia, and Sofia. We reuse this one cross as the anchor for the chapter, and we bring in hemophilia (another X-linked recessive trait) for the second cross.

Start with one real cross

Genes usually sit on ordinary chromosomes, but a few ride on the sex chromosomes, X and Y. When a gene does, its inheritance stops being symmetric between the sexes. Rather than state that rule cold, work one real cross first and let the pattern appear. We follow the Rivera family and a real X-linked trait, red-green color blindness, with the alleles introduced above. Recall from Chapter 4: Meiosis that each parent passes exactly one sex chromosome to each child: the mother always gives an X, and the father gives either his X (which makes a daughter) or his Y (which makes a son).

Example 1 (the anchor cross). Elena is a carrier for color blindness (\(X^C X^c\)) and David has normal vision (\(X^C Y\)). What fraction of their sons and of their daughters will be color-blind?

Put David's gametes across the top. He makes two kinds: an X carrying \(X^C\), and a Y. Put Elena's gametes down the side. She makes \(X^C\) and \(X^c\). The column a child lands in also decides its sex: the \(X^C\) column (father's X) gives daughters, and the \(Y\) column (father's Y) gives sons.

Read the two columns separately, because sex is decided by which column a child lands in. The sons (the \(Y\) column) are \(X^C Y\) and \(X^c Y\), so half of the sons are color-blind. The daughters (the \(X^C\) column) are \(X^C X^C\) and \(X^C X^c\), so no daughter is color-blind, but half of the daughters are carriers. This matches the classic result: a carrier mother crossed with a normal father gives half of sons color-blind and no daughters affected.

Aha: a son's single X always comes from his mother, because his father gave him the Y. So a son's X-linked trait is decided entirely by his mother. That is why the mother's carrier status, not the father's vision, controls whether the sons are color-blind here.

The real-world clue. This matches what you actually see. Red-green color blindness affects about 1 in 12 boys but only about 1 in 200 girls. The cross shows why: a son has a single X, so one \(X^c\) is enough to make him color-blind, while a daughter needs \(X^c\) on both of her X chromosomes. The outcome also depends on WHICH parent carries the allele, an asymmetry that never happens for a gene on an ordinary chromosome.

Name the pattern. Two ideas fall out of this one cross, and they drive the whole chapter. First, a male carries a single X, so he has just one copy of every X-linked gene. Biologists call this being hemizygous (defined fully below). One recessive allele is therefore enough to make him show an X-linked recessive trait, which is why such traits show up far more often in males. Second, an X-linked allele travels a zig-zag path between the sexes, called criss-cross inheritance: a father passes his X, and any allele on it, only to his daughters, while a mother passes an X to her sons. So an affected grandfather's allele reaches his grandsons through his unaffected carrier daughters, and the trait appears to skip the daughters' generation. The rest of this chapter explains why.

The core idea

All of Mendel's rules so far assumed a gene sits on an autosome, meaning any chromosome that is not a sex chromosome. Humans have 46 chromosomes: 22 pairs of autosomes plus one pair of sex chromosomes. A gene carried on a sex chromosome is called sex-linked, and its inheritance breaks the tidy Mendelian pattern because the two sexes do not carry the sex chromosomes in the same way.

First, some vocabulary, each defined plainly on first use:

  • The two sex chromosomes are called X and Y. A person with two X chromosomes (\(XX\)) develops as female; a person with one X and one Y (\(XY\)) develops as male. The Y chromosome carries the SRY gene that triggers male development, so any Y makes a male.
  • The father determines the sex of the child. The mother is \(XX\), so every egg carries an X. The father is \(XY\), so half his sperm carry X and half carry Y. A child who gets the father's X becomes a daughter (\(XX\)); a child who gets the father's Y becomes a son (\(XY\)). The mother has no Y to give, so she cannot set the sex.
  • X-linked means the gene sits on the X chromosome. Most sex-linked genes are X-linked, because the X chromosome is large and carries many genes. Y-linked means the gene sits on the Y chromosome, which is small and carries very few genes.
  • Hemizygous means having only one copy of a gene instead of the usual two. A male is hemizygous for every X-linked gene: he has just one X, so he has only one allele of each X-linked gene, with no second copy to mask it. A female has two X chromosomes, so she has two alleles like any autosomal gene.

The load-bearing consequence. For an X-linked recessive trait, a single recessive allele is enough to make a male show the trait, because he is hemizygous and has no second X to carry a dominant allele. A female needs the recessive allele on both X chromosomes to show the trait. That asymmetry is why X-linked recessive traits, such as red-green color blindness and hemophilia, appear far more often in males than in females.

A quick test for sex linkage: the reciprocal cross. For a gene on an ordinary chromosome it makes no difference which parent carries a recessive allele; the two reciprocal crosses give matching results in sons and daughters. For an X-linked gene it makes all the difference. An affected mother (\(X^c X^c\)) crossed with a normal father (\(X^C Y\)) gives ALL sons color-blind and no daughters affected, while the reciprocal cross, a normal mother (\(X^C X^C\)) with a color-blind father (\(X^c Y\)), gives NO sons color-blind and again no daughters affected. Getting different results from the same two phenotypes, simply swapped between the parents, is the fingerprint of a sex-linked gene.

Notation. We write the allele as a superscript on the chromosome it rides on. For color blindness the female genotypes are \(X^C X^C\) (normal), \(X^C X^c\) (carrier, unaffected), and \(X^c X^c\) (color-blind). The male genotypes are \(X^C Y\) (normal) and \(X^c Y\) (color-blind). The Y carries no color-vision gene at all, so we write it as a bare \(Y\).

The rule that traps most students. A father gives every son his Y chromosome, never his X. So a father can never pass an X-linked allele to a son. A color-blind father does not make his sons color-blind. Instead he passes his \(X^c\) to every daughter, who becomes a carrier. The allele reaches his grandsons only through those carrier daughters. This is why an X-linked recessive trait seems to skip a generation and reappear in males.

Y-linked traits are the mirror image. A Y-linked allele passes only down the male line, from a father to all of his sons and none of his daughters, because only sons receive the father's Y. The wiki notes some forms of baldness as Y-linked, although in reality most baldness is controlled by many genes and hormones, so treat Y-linked as the pattern to know rather than a claim that baldness is simple.

Worked examples

These two cases build on the anchor cross above. Example 2 shows the one situation in which a female can be affected; Example 3 shows the mirror-image Y-linked pattern.

Example 2 (a female CAN be affected). This is the wiki's hemophilia cross. Hemophilia is an X-linked recessive bleeding disorder, with alleles \(X^H\) (normal clotting, dominant) and \(X^h\) (hemophilia, recessive). Cross a father who has hemophilia (\(X^h Y\)) with a mother who is a carrier (\(X^H X^h\)). What are the children?

The affected father makes an \(X^h\) gamete and a \(Y\) gamete. The carrier mother makes \(X^H\) and \(X^h\). Set up the square the same way, with the father's gametes across the top.

The daughters (father's \(X^h\) column) are \(X^H X^h\) (carrier) and \(X^h X^h\) (hemophilia), so half of the daughters are affected. The sons (the \(Y\) column) are \(X^H Y\) (normal) and \(X^h Y\) (hemophilia), so half of the sons are affected. So an X-linked recessive trait CAN appear in a female, but it takes an affected father and a carrier (or affected) mother so that she receives the recessive allele on both X chromosomes.

Aha: notice where each affected son's \(X^h\) came from. It came from the mother, not from the affected father, because the father handed his sons the \(Y\). The father's own \(X^h\) went only to his daughters. Even in a cross with an affected father, there is still no father-to-son transmission of the X-linked allele.

Example 3 (a Y-linked trait). Suppose a trait is Y-linked, meaning its gene sits on the Y chromosome. Write the father's Y that carries the trait allele as \(Y^{B}\). Cross a father who has the trait (\(X\,Y^{B}\)) with a mother who does not (\(XX\), since she has no Y). Which children have the trait?

The mother has no Y, so she cannot carry a Y-linked allele at all, and every one of her gametes is a plain X. The father makes an X gamete and a \(Y^{B}\) gamete. Only the sons receive the \(Y^{B}\).

Every son is \(X\,Y^{B}\) and shows the trait, and every daughter is \(XX\) and cannot. A Y-linked trait therefore passes straight down the male line: father to all sons, generation after generation, and never to a daughter. This is the simplest inheritance pattern of all, because only one parent can carry the gene and only one kind of child can receive it.

Aha: X-linked and Y-linked are mirror images. An X-linked recessive allele can hide in a carrier female and skip a generation, while a Y-linked allele can never hide and never skip: it is either passed to every son or it is not present at all.

Common pitfalls

  • Thinking a father can pass an X-linked trait to his son. He cannot. A son gets his father's Y and his mother's X, so a son's X-linked alleles all come from the mother. A color-blind father has color-blind sons only if the mother also contributes an \(X^c\). Watch for exam questions that bait this trap by asking whether an affected father's sons will be affected.
  • Assuming an X-linked recessive trait cannot appear in females. It is rarer in females, not impossible. A female shows the trait when she is \(X^c X^c\), which needs the recessive allele from both parents (see Example 2). The correct statement is that the trait appears MORE OFTEN in males because males are hemizygous, not that it never appears in females.
  • Calling a carrier "affected." A carrier female (\(X^C X^c\)) has normal vision. She carries the recessive allele but the dominant allele on her other X masks it, so she is unaffected. In a pedigree she is drawn as unaffected (often with a dot), never as filled-in.
  • Treating the dominant and recessive labels as perfectly clean for X-linked genes. Because of X-inactivation (one X chromosome is randomly switched off in each cell of a female, described below), a carrier female is a patchwork of cells: some express one allele and some the other. For most X-linked traits the working allele covers enough cells that she looks unaffected, but the strict dominant/recessive model is an approximation for X-linked genes, not an exact law.

X-inactivation (a real nuance)

Females have two X chromosomes, but males have one, so a female would otherwise make a double dose of every X-linked protein. The body balances this by X-inactivation: early in development, each cell randomly shuts down one of its two X chromosomes and condenses it into a small clump called a Barr body. Every cell descended from that cell keeps the same X switched off. A carrier female is therefore a mosaic, meaning a mixture of two kinds of cells, some expressing the \(X^C\) allele and some the \(X^c\) allele. A real and visible example is the calico or tortoiseshell cat, whose orange-versus-black coat color is X-linked. The random patches of orange and black fur are literally a map of which X chromosome was inactivated in each region of skin. This is why the wiki notes that the dominant/recessive model is less accurate for X-linked genes than for autosomal ones.

Connections

  • ← Chapter 4: Meiosis: the X and Y separate during meiosis, so each parent passes exactly one sex chromosome to each gamete. That segregation is what puts the sex chromosomes on the axes of the square.
  • ← Chapter 7: Punnett Squares and Monohybrid Crosses: the same grid method, but now the axes carry the X and Y chromosomes, and the column a child lands in also decides its sex.
  • ← Chapter 8: Probability in Genetics: "what is the chance their next son is color-blind?" is a conditional probability. Condition on the child being a son first, then read the odds within that column.
  • ↔ Chapter 10: Incomplete Dominance and Codominance: a sibling "beyond Mendel" pattern. Here the twist is the chromosome the gene rides on; there the twist is how the two alleles blend or both show.
  • → Chapter 22: Pedigrees: in a family tree, an X-linked recessive trait shows a telltale sign: it is mostly in males and it skips generations through carrier mothers. The Rivera family and the real Queen Victoria hemophilia pedigree both show this.
  • → Chapter 23: Genetic Disorders and the Frontier: hemophilia, red-green color blindness, and Duchenne muscular dystrophy are the real X-linked recessive disorders you are expected to know.

★ Landmark it points toward

This chapter has no separate name-only landmark. The natural next step is to trace these traits across a whole family in Chapter 22: Pedigrees. The deeper idea already introduced above, X-inactivation (also called lyonization), is the frontier here: it explains why the clean dominant/recessive model bends for X-linked genes, and it is the reason a carrier female is a mosaic rather than uniformly one phenotype.

Practice

P1 (predict affected sons and daughters). A woman who is a carrier for color blindness (\(X^C X^c\)) marries a color-blind man (\(X^c Y\)). What fraction of their sons, and what fraction of their daughters, will be color-blind?

AnswerThe father's gametes are \(X^c\) and \(Y\); the mother's are \(X^C\) and \(X^c\). Daughters (father's \(X^c\) column) are \(X^C X^c\) (carrier) and \(X^c X^c\) (color-blind), so half of daughters are color-blind. Sons (the \(Y\) column) are \(X^C Y\) (normal) and \(X^c Y\) (color-blind), so half of sons are color-blind. Result: \(\tfrac{1}{2}\) of sons and \(\tfrac{1}{2}\) of daughters color-blind. This is the routine case; it applies the same square as the worked examples.

P2 (the "can this father pass it to his son?" trap). Miguel, the Rivera grandfather, is color-blind (\(X^c Y\)). His daughter Elena has normal vision. Two questions: (a) Could Miguel have passed color blindness directly to a son of his own? (b) Can Elena's sons be color-blind, and if so how did the allele get to them?

Answer(a) No. Miguel gives every son his \(Y\), never his \(X\), so he cannot pass an X-linked allele to a son. His sons get their single X from their mother, so their color vision depends entirely on her, not on Miguel.

Why it works: Miguel's \(X^c\) can only travel on his X chromosome, and his X only goes to daughters. So all of Miguel's daughters, including Elena, receive his \(X^c\) and become carriers (\(X^C X^c\), assuming their mother gave a normal \(X^C\)). Elena is a carrier even though she has normal vision. Now Elena can pass \(X^c\) to her own children. Her sons get either her \(X^C\) or her \(X^c\), so \(\tfrac{1}{2}\) of Elena's sons are color-blind. The allele reached her sons through her, one generation after Miguel.

Pattern (X-linked recessive skips through carrier daughters): an X-linked recessive trait travels grandfather to carrier daughter to grandson, appearing to skip the daughter's generation because she is an unaffected carrier. Whenever a question asks whether an affected father transmits the trait to his sons, the answer is no; look instead for the trait to resurface in his daughters' sons. Contrast this with a Y-linked trait, which passes father to every son and never skips.

P3 (deduce carrier status from a child). A couple both have normal color vision. Their first son is color-blind. What must the mother's genotype be, and what is the father's? Explain how you know.

AnswerThe mother must be a carrier, \(X^C X^c\). The father is \(X^C Y\) (he has normal vision).

Why it works: the color-blind son is \(X^c Y\). His single X came from his mother, because his father gave him the Y. So the mother had to carry an \(X^c\) to pass on. Since the mother herself has normal vision, her other X must be \(X^C\), making her the carrier \(X^C X^c\). The father cannot be the source of the son's \(X^c\) at all (no father-to-son X transmission), and since the father has normal vision he is \(X^C Y\).

Pattern (read backward from an affected son): an affected son of an unaffected mother proves the mother is a carrier for that X-linked recessive gene, because the son's only X came from her. This backward deduction, inferring a hidden genotype from an affected child, is exactly the reasoning used in Chapter 22: Pedigrees, where you cannot set up crosses and must read genotypes from who is affected.

Watch / read preview before showing learners

↗ Khan Academy: X-linked inheritance ▶ Amoeba Sisters: Sex-Linked Traits (YouTube) ↗ Bozeman Science: Sex-Linked Traits

Teaching notes

Opener: ask "why are there so many more color-blind boys than girls in this class?" Let them guess, then reveal that the gene is on the X and that boys have only one X. That single fact drives the whole chapter.

Board moves: draw Example 1 and immediately split the square into a "daughters" column and a "sons" column. Insist that students read the two columns separately, because the question almost always asks about one sex. Then draw Example 2 beside it so they see that a female CAN be affected when both parents supply the recessive allele.

What to hammer: no father-to-son X transmission. Make students trace, in Example 2, that each affected son's \(X^h\) came from the mother, not the affected father. This is the single most tested point and the one most students get wrong.

The common stumble: students collapse "more common in males" into "only in males" and forget carriers. Ask repeatedly whether a given female is normal, a carrier, or affected, so the three female genotypes stay distinct.

Pacing: spend most of the time on Examples 1 and 2 and on P2 (the trap). Treat Example 3 (Y-linked) and X-inactivation as shorter beats: name the pattern, show one square, move on. Save P3 (deducing carrier status) as the bridge into Chapter 22: Pedigrees.

Chapter 13: Polygenic Traits and Pleiotropy

Two ways real inheritance breaks the neat single-gene picture: polygenic traits are one trait controlled by many genes whose effects add up (giving a smooth range, not discrete classes), and pleiotropy is one gene that affects many separate traits.

Prerequisites: Chapter 5: Alleles, Genotype and Phenotype (genotype is the alleles you carry; phenotype is the observable trait) · Chapter 9: Independent Assortment and Dihybrid Crosses (genes on different chromosomes are inherited independently, which lets many genes combine freely).
This chapter: polygenic inheritance (many genes, one continuous trait) and pleiotropy (one gene, many traits), plus how the environment shifts phenotype.
Unlocks: Chapter 23: Genetic Disorders Casebook (complex, multifactorial disease, where many genes and the environment together set the risk).

Start with two real cases

Chapters 6 through 12 followed traits controlled by a single gene, which sort offspring into a few discrete (separate, countable) classes with clean ratios: a PTC taster or non-taster, blood type A or B or AB or O. Count them and you get a tidy number like \(3:1\). Two very common real patterns do not fit that picture at all. Before naming either one, work through two concrete cases from the Rivera family and watch how each breaks the single-gene mold in its own direction.

Case 1 (the Rivera children's skin tone varies smoothly). Mateo, Lucia, and Sofia do not fall into two or three sharp skin-color categories; their tones vary a little across a smooth range. Model skin color with three genes, \(A\), \(B\), and \(C\), on different chromosomes. Each capital-letter allele adds one unit of pigment and each lowercase allele adds none, and the units simply add up (this is a simplified teaching model; real skin color involves many more genes). If Elena and David are each triple heterozygous, \(AaBbCc\), what range of skin tones can their children show, and how often does each appear?

Each child inherits \(6\) of these alleles total (two per gene, one from each parent). The pigment level is just the number of capital-letter (contributing) alleles, which can be anything from \(0\) (genotype \(aabbcc\), lightest) to \(6\) (genotype \(AABBCC\), darkest). That is \(7\) shade classes.

You cannot force this into a Mendelian ratio like \(3:1\) or \(9:3:3:1\), because we are not sorting children into "dominant" and "recessive" boxes. We are counting how many contributing alleles each child inherits. Each parent is heterozygous at each gene, so each of the \(6\) inherited allele slots is independently a capital letter with probability \(\tfrac12\). The number of contributing alleles therefore follows a binomial pattern, and the count of ways to get exactly \(k\) contributing alleles out of \(6\) is \(\binom{6}{k}\):

\[\binom{6}{0}:\binom{6}{1}:\binom{6}{2}:\binom{6}{3}:\binom{6}{4}:\binom{6}{5}:\binom{6}{6} = 1:6:15:20:15:6:1\]

These add to \(2^6 = 64\). So out of every \(64\) children, on average \(1\) is lightest, \(20\) are in the exact middle shade, \(1\) is darkest, and the rest fall in between.

Aha: the bell shape comes from arithmetic, not magic. There are simply many more ways to inherit a middle mix of contributing and non-contributing alleles than to inherit all-contributing or all-none. The single most common class (\(20\) out of \(64\)) is the exact average, and the two extremes (\(1\) each) are the rarest. This is why "most people are close to average height" is a fact about counting, not about height being special.

Case 2 (one sickle cell allele, many effects at once). The Riveras could carry the sickle cell allele, a single-base change in the gene for hemoglobin. Someone with two copies (homozygous recessive) has sickle cell disease. List the separate traits this one gene affects, and explain why a single gene causes so many.

The mutation makes an abnormal hemoglobin (called HbS) that clumps together when oxygen is low. That reshapes round red blood cells into stiff, curved (sickle) shapes. Every effect below flows from that one shape change, so a single gene drives all of them at once:

So this one allele affects red blood cell shape, oxygen delivery (anemia), pain episodes, organ health, and malaria risk. It causes many traits because they all trace back to a single altered protein.

Aha: this also explains a puzzle: why has such a harmful allele stayed common in some populations? A carrier (one sickle cell allele plus one normal allele, so an unaffected heterozygote) is largely healthy but resists malaria. In regions with heavy malaria, that protection kept the allele frequent, even though two copies cause disease. One gene with both a cost and a benefit; that is the many-effects pattern in action, and it ties to the Riveras as an allele they could carry (see Chapter 23).

The pattern: many-to-one versus one-to-many

The two cases break the single-gene picture in opposite directions, and telling them apart is the whole skill of this chapter.

  • Case 1 was many genes acting on one trait. Three genes (really many more) each added a little to skin tone, and their effects summed into a smooth, bell-shaped range instead of a few discrete classes. The arrow runs many genes → one continuous trait.
  • Case 2 was one gene acting on many traits. A single change in the hemoglobin gene rippled outward into anemia, pain crises, organ damage, and malaria resistance all at once. The arrow runs one gene → many traits.

Those are the two mechanisms this chapter names: polygenic inheritance (many genes → one trait) and pleiotropy (one gene → many traits). Now that you have seen each one happen, here are the precise definitions and why each produces the pattern it does.

The core idea

A single gene gives a small number of discrete phenotype classes, so counting them yields a clean ratio. Many real traits are not like that. Height, skin color, and eye color form a continuous range: line up 100 people and their heights blend smoothly from short to tall with every value in between, not two piles labeled "short" and "tall." A trait measured on a numeric scale like this is called a quantitative trait. The two mechanisms below are why real inheritance so often looks continuous, or tangled, rather than obeying a tidy Mendelian ratio.

Polygenic inheritance: many genes, one trait

A polygenic trait is one trait controlled by many genes acting together (the name means "many genes": poly = many, genic = of genes). The genes are usually inherited independently (see Chapter 9) and their effects are additive, meaning each contributing allele nudges the trait a small, fixed amount in one direction and the total phenotype is the sum of those contributions. Call an allele that pushes the trait up a contributing allele (often written with a capital letter) and an allele with no push a non-contributing allele (lowercase). The more contributing alleles an individual carries, the further the trait is expressed. Because each gene adds only a little, the trait varies smoothly instead of jumping between classes.

Across a whole population the counts of contributing alleles pile up in the middle: most individuals carry a mix of contributing and non-contributing alleles and land near the average, while carrying almost all contributing alleles (very high) or almost none (very low) is rare. The result is a bell-shaped distribution (also called a normal distribution): a hump in the middle that tails off symmetrically on both sides, exactly the shape Case 1 produced. The more genes involved, and the more the environment adds its own variation, the smoother that bell becomes. Real height is influenced by hundreds of genes, so its curve is essentially continuous.

Pleiotropy: one gene, many traits

Pleiotropy is the reverse situation: one gene affects many separate traits (the name means "more turnings": one gene turns several dials). This happens because one gene often codes for one protein, and that single protein can be used in many tissues or sit at the start of a chain of events that ripples outward. Change the gene and every downstream trait changes together, which is exactly what the sickle cell allele did in Case 2: one altered hemoglobin protein, then anemia, pain, organ damage, and malaria resistance all at once (the mutation itself is covered in Chapter 20: Mutations).

Do not confuse three multi-gene patterns

Three different ideas all involve genes interacting, and exams test whether you can tell them apart. The direction of the arrows is what separates them.

Telling the three patterns apart
PatternGenes and traitsWhat it looks like
Polygenic (this chapter)many genes act on one traiteffects add up; a continuous, bell-shaped range with no simple ratio
Pleiotropy (this chapter)one gene acts on many traitsseveral unrelated-looking traits change together when the gene changes
Epistasis (Chapter 14)two genes, one trait, but one gene masks the otherdiscrete classes with a modified ratio (for example \(9:3:4\) or \(12:3:1\))

Phenotype = genotype + environment

A final, exam-critical point that all of this depends on: the genotype is not a one-to-one readout of the phenotype. The observable trait is set by the genes and by the environment the individual grows up in.

\[\text{phenotype} = \text{genotype} + \text{environment}\]

A child with a "tall" combination of height alleles still needs good nutrition to reach that height. Skin darkens with sun exposure. Two people with the same genotype can end up with different phenotypes, and two people with different genotypes can look the same. Continuous traits are especially sensitive to the environment, which is one more reason they blur into a smooth range.

Worked examples

Example 1 (how many classes, for any number of genes). Case 1 used three additive genes and gave \(7\) shade classes in the ratio \(1:6:15:20:15:6:1\). Suppose instead a flower's petal redness is set by just two additive genes, \(R_1\) and \(R_2\), each red-adding allele contributing one unit of red. Cross two double heterozygotes, \(R_1 r_1 R_2 r_2 \times R_1 r_1 R_2 r_2\). How many color classes appear, and in what ratio? Then give the rule for \(n\) genes.

Each child inherits \(2\) alleles per gene \(\times\, 2\) genes \(= 4\) alleles, so the number of red-adding alleles runs from \(0\) to \(4\). That is \(5\) classes. Each of the \(4\) inherited allele slots is independently red-adding with probability \(\tfrac12\), so the classes appear as \(\binom{4}{k}\):

\[\binom{4}{0}:\binom{4}{1}:\binom{4}{2}:\binom{4}{3}:\binom{4}{4} = 1:4:6:4:1\]

which add to \(2^4 = 16\). The middle class (\(2\) red-adding alleles) is the commonest at \(6/16\), and the two extremes are \(1/16\) each. Same bell shape as Case 1, just fewer, coarser steps.

The general rule. With \(n\) additive genes, a child carries \(2n\) relevant alleles, so the number of contributing alleles runs from \(0\) to \(2n\). That is \(2n+1\) phenotype classes, and their frequencies are the binomial coefficients \(\binom{2n}{k}\) out of \(2^{2n}\) (the terms of \(\left(\tfrac12+\tfrac12\right)^{2n}\)). For \(n = 2\) that is \(5\) classes summing to \(16\); for \(n = 3\) it is \(7\) classes summing to \(64\); hundreds of genes give so many finely spaced classes that the trait looks perfectly continuous.

Aha: more genes do not change the shape, only its resolution. Every additive polygenic trait gives the same bell curve; adding genes just adds more, finer classes (\(2n+1\) of them) until the steps blur into a smooth continuum. The rule also runs backward, which is a common exam move: if a cross of two heterozygotes yields \(5\) evenly graded classes, then \(2n+1 = 5\), so \(n = 2\) additive genes are at work.

Example 2 (same genes, different phenotype). Suppose the three Rivera children inherited very similar sets of height alleles, so genetically they should all be tall. Mateo, raised with steady, complete nutrition, grows tall. Imagine a version of Lucia raised with long stretches of poor nutrition in childhood. Do the genes alone decide her adult height?

No. Height is polygenic and strongly shaped by the environment. The height alleles set a range of possible outcomes, but reaching the top of that range needs good nutrition, sleep, and health during growth. With poor childhood nutrition, the same "tall" genotype can produce a shorter adult. The genotype did not change; the phenotype did.

Aha: \(\text{phenotype} = \text{genotype} + \text{environment}\). A genotype sets a range of possibilities, and the environment picks a point within that range. This is why you can never read a continuous trait's phenotype as a direct printout of its genes, and why studies of traits like height must account for nutrition and other conditions.
Common pitfalls:
  • Swapping polygenic and pleiotropy. They point in opposite directions. Polygenic is many genes acting on one trait (height). Pleiotropy is one gene affecting many traits (sickle cell). Count the genes and count the traits, then check which is "many."
  • Confusing polygenic with epistasis. Both use several genes for one trait, but they behave differently. In polygenic inheritance the genes add up and give a continuous, bell-shaped range with no simple ratio. In epistasis (Chapter 14) one gene masks another, and you still get discrete classes with a modified ratio such as \(9:3:4\) or \(12:3:1\).
  • Hunting for a Mendelian ratio on a continuous trait. A polygenic trait does not give \(3:1\) or \(9:3:3:1\). It gives a distribution (a bell curve). Expecting a single tidy ratio is a category error; describe the spread, not one ratio.
  • Reading genotype as a one-to-one printout of phenotype. The environment matters. \(\text{phenotype} = \text{genotype} + \text{environment}\). Two identical genotypes can look different, and two different genotypes can look the same, especially for continuous traits.
  • Thinking "more genes" means "more dominant." In the additive model there is no masking at all. Each contributing allele simply adds its small amount. Dominance (one allele hiding another at the same gene) is a different idea from many genes adding across several genes.

Connections

  • ← Chapter 5: Alleles, Genotype and Phenotype: this chapter stretches the genotype-to-phenotype link. Phenotype is now the sum of many genes plus the environment, not a single gene's readout.
  • ← Chapter 9: Independent Assortment and Dihybrid Crosses: polygenic inheritance is just many independent genes at once. Independent assortment is what lets their contributing alleles combine freely, which produces the binomial spread.
  • ↔ Chapter 8: Probability in Genetics: the \(1:6:15:20:15:6:1\) pattern is the binomial from counting how many contributing alleles out of \(6\) a child inherits, using the same multiply-and-add rules.
  • → Chapter 14: Epistasis and Lethal Alleles: another multi-gene pattern, but there one gene masks another and the classes stay discrete with a modified ratio. Contrast it with the smooth additive curve here.
  • → Chapter 20: Mutations: the sickle cell allele is a single-base missense mutation. One tiny change in one gene, then pleiotropy spreads its effects across the body.
  • → Chapter 23: Genetic Disorders Casebook: most common diseases (heart disease, diabetes) are complex, meaning polygenic plus environmental. This chapter is the model behind "multifactorial" risk.
  • ↔ Statistics: a continuous trait is described by its mean and spread (a bell curve), the same tools statistics uses for any measurement.

★ Landmark it points toward

Quantitative genetics and heritability. The branch of genetics that measures continuous traits. Its central number, heritability (written \(h^2\)), estimates how much of a trait's variation in a population comes from genetic differences versus environmental differences. Modern work adds up thousands of small gene effects into a polygenic risk score. Name-only here; you do not compute \(h^2\) at Division B.

Practice

P1 (classify each pattern). Label each description as polygenic, pleiotropy, or epistasis, and say how you decided. (a) A single gene in cats affects both coat color and, in some cats, crossed eyes and hearing. (b) Grain color in wheat ranges smoothly from white to dark red and is set by three genes whose red-adding alleles sum together. (c) In Labrador retrievers, one gene (\(E\)) decides whether any pigment reaches the coat at all, hiding the black-versus-brown gene when the dog is \(ee\) (yellow).

Answer (a) Pleiotropy. (b) Polygenic. (c) Epistasis.

Why it works: read each description as arrows between genes and traits. In (a) it says "a single gene" affects several different traits (coat, eyes, hearing), so it is one gene to many traits, which is pleiotropy. In (b) three genes all act on one trait (grain color) and their alleles "sum together," which is many genes adding into one continuous trait, so polygenic; the smooth white-to-red range confirms it. In (c) two genes act on one trait (coat color) but one gene masks the other (the \(ee\) genotype hides the black-or-brown gene entirely), which is masking, not adding, so it is epistasis.

Pattern (the arrow test): to classify any gene-interaction description, count the genes and the traits, then check the verb. One gene, many traits = pleiotropy. Many genes, one trait, effects add = polygenic (look for a continuous range or a bell curve). Two genes, one trait, one masks the other = epistasis (look for discrete classes and a modified ratio). The trap is that polygenic and epistasis both have many genes and one trait; the deciding word is "add" (polygenic) versus "mask" or "hide" (epistasis).

P2 (why no simple ratio). Your classmate insists a polygenic trait like height must reduce to some Mendelian ratio if you draw a big enough Punnett square. Explain why a continuous trait has no single ratio, using the three-gene skin-color model.

Answer A continuous trait has a distribution, not one ratio. In the three-gene model, \(AaBbCc \times AaBbCc\) gives contributing-allele counts of \(0\) through \(6\) in the ratio \(1:6:15:20:15:6:1\) out of \(64\), which is a bell curve, not a single number.

Why it works: a Mendelian ratio like \(3:1\) counts a few discrete phenotype boxes from one gene. A polygenic trait is the sum of contributions from many genes, so the phenotype is a running total that can land on many values, and there are many more ways to reach a middle total than an extreme one (that is what makes the middle class the tallest bar). Add even more genes plus environmental variation and the steps merge into a smooth curve with intermediate values that no small whole-number ratio can capture. A giant Punnett square only makes more, finer classes; it never collapses to one ratio.

Pattern (ratio versus distribution): when a trait falls into a few discrete classes from one or two genes, report a ratio. When a trait is the additive sum of many genes (and the environment), report a distribution (its center and spread, a bell curve). Signal words for the distribution case: "continuous," "ranges smoothly," "quantitative," or "many genes." Do not force a distribution into a ratio.

P3 (genes versus environment). Two tomato plants have the identical set of fruit-size genes. One is grown with plenty of water and rich soil; the other is grown in dry, poor soil. The well-watered plant produces larger tomatoes. Did the genes for fruit size change? What decided the difference?

AnswerNo, the genes did not change; the genotype is identical in both plants. The environment (water and soil) decided the difference. This is \(\text{phenotype} = \text{genotype} + \text{environment}\): the genotype sets the possible range of fruit size, and the growing conditions set where in that range each plant lands.

Watch / read preview before showing learners

↗ Khan Academy: Polygenic inheritance and environmental effects ▶ Amoeba Sisters: Alleles and Genes (background on multiple-gene traits, YouTube) ↗ National Human Genome Research Institute: Pleiotropy (glossary)

Teaching notes

Opener: have every student stand and line up by height. The class forms a smooth gradient, not two groups. Ask "where do we draw the line between short and tall?" The answer (nowhere clean) motivates continuous, polygenic traits, and leads straight into Case 1 before you name anything.

Board moves: build the \(1:6:15:20:15:6:1\) bars live by listing how many ways to get \(0,1,2,\dots,6\) contributing alleles (\(\binom{6}{k}\)), then sketch the bell curve over the bars.
