For pleiotropy, draw one gene box and let students call out the different effects as you fan arrows outward. Keep both diagrams on the board side by side so the "many-to-one" versus "one-to-many" contrast is visual.

What to hammer: the arrow test for classifying polygenic versus pleiotropy versus epistasis, that a continuous trait has a distribution not a ratio, and the general count \(2n+1\) classes for \(n\) additive genes. Repeat \(\text{phenotype} = \text{genotype} + \text{environment}\) until it is automatic.

The common stumble: students mix up polygenic and epistasis because both have many genes and one trait. Anchor the difference on one word: additive genes add (smooth curve); an epistatic gene masks (discrete modified ratio, saved for Chapter 14).

Pacing: spend most of the time on Case 1 (the bell curve is the load-bearing idea) and P1 (classification is the most-tested skill). Use Example 1 to push from the fixed three-gene case to the general \(2n+1\) rule. Save the malaria-resistance point in Case 2 for a strong class; it previews balancing selection and the disorders casebook in Chapter 23.

Epistasis and Lethal Alleles

Two different genes can interact so that one hides the other, and a genotype that kills its carrier can quietly erase a whole class of offspring. Both bend the familiar Mendelian ratios into new, recognizable numbers.

Prerequisites: Chapter 9: Independent Assortment and Dihybrid Crosses
This chapter: Epistasis and Lethal Alleles
Unlocks: Chapter 23: Genetic Disorders and the Frontier (real gene interactions and lethal genotypes in human disease)

Start with the crosses

In Chapter 9, crossing two double heterozygotes (\(AaBb \times AaBb\)) gave the clean dihybrid ratio \(9:3:3:1\): four visible classes, from two genes assorting independently. That prediction assumes the two genes act separately, each showing its own trait. Real breeders often count something different. Below are four documented crosses. Look at the observed numbers first, before we explain them.

Cross 1: Labrador coat color

Two genes set a Labrador retriever's coat. Gene \(B\) is pigment color, with \(B\) (black) dominant over \(b\) (brown, called chocolate). Gene \(E\) decides whether that pigment reaches the fur, with \(E\) (deposited) dominant over \(e\) (not deposited); an \(ee\) dog is yellow no matter what gene \(B\) says. Cross two double heterozygotes, \(BbEe \times BbEe\). The pups come in the ratio \(9\) black : \(3\) brown : \(4\) yellow, not the \(9:3:3:1\) you would predict.

Cross 2: Summer squash fruit color

Gene \(W\) can switch color off: \(W\) (white) is dominant, and any \(W\_\) plant is white because the dominant allele blocks all pigment. Only a \(ww\) plant lets a second gene show. Gene \(Y\) then makes the fruit yellow (\(Y\_\)) or green (\(yy\)). Cross two double heterozygotes, \(WwYy \times WwYy\). The fruits come in the ratio \(12\) white : \(3\) yellow : \(1\) green.

Cross 3: Sweet pea flower color

Two genes, \(C\) and \(P\), each make one enzyme in the single pathway that builds purple pigment. A plant is purple only if it has a dominant allele at BOTH genes; if either enzyme is missing, the pathway breaks and the flower is white. Cross two double heterozygotes, \(CcPp \times CcPp\). The flowers come in the ratio \(9\) purple : \(7\) white.

Cross 4: Yellow coat color in mice (a lethal allele)

The mouse allele \(A^Y\) makes a yellow coat and is dominant, but the homozygote \(A^Y A^Y\) dies before birth. \(A^Y a\) is yellow and \(aa\) is agouti (a brown-banded color). Because \(A^Y A^Y\) never survives, every living yellow mouse is \(A^Y a\). Cross two of them, \(A^Y a \times A^Y a\). This is only a one-gene cross, so Chapter 9 predicts a \(3:1\) phenotype ratio; instead the mice actually born come in the ratio \(2\) yellow : \(1\) agouti.

Find the pattern, then name it

Every ratio above is a familiar ratio with some classes hidden. You do not build a special square. Fill the ordinary Punnett square as usual, then let the biology decide which boxes look identical (or which class never survives). The counts regroup:

  • Labrador \(9:3:4\). The recessive \(ee\) genotype masks gene \(B\), so both classes that carry \(ee\) (\(B\_ee\) and \(bbee\)) turn yellow and merge: \(3 + 1 = 4\). This is recessive epistasis.
  • Squash \(12:3:1\). A single dominant \(W\) masks gene \(Y\), so every \(W\_\) fruit is white and the two \(W\_\) classes merge: \(9 + 3 = 12\). This is dominant epistasis.
  • Sweet pea \(9:7\). A plant needs a dominant allele at BOTH genes to be purple, so only \(C\_P\_ = 9\) is purple and the other three classes merge: \(3 + 3 + 1 = 7\). This is duplicate recessive epistasis.
  • Mice \(2:1\). Nothing is masked here. The genotype ratio is the normal \(1\ A^YA^Y : 2\ A^Ya : 1\ aa\), but the \(A^YA^Y\) class dies, so it is deleted from the count and the survivors are \(2:1\). This is a lethal allele.

The first three are all epistasis: one gene hiding a different gene, which merges classes of the \(9:3:3:1\) ratio (so every epistasis ratio still sums to \(16\)). The fourth is a lethal allele: a genotype that kills its carrier, so its class vanishes from the survivors. The rest of the chapter defines both ideas precisely, derives the full set of named ratios, and works these crosses out box by box.

The core idea

The four crosses above break the \(9:3:3:1\) prediction in two distinct ways. Three of them (Labrador, squash, sweet pea) are epistasis, where one gene hides another gene. The fourth (yellow mice) is a lethal allele, where a whole genotype dies. This section defines each mechanism precisely and shows how the numbers change.

Epistasis: one gene masks another gene

Epistasis is when one gene hides or changes the effect of a different gene. The gene doing the masking is called epistatic. The gene being masked is called hypostatic. Read this carefully, because it is the single most important distinction in the chapter: epistasis is an interaction between two separate genes, not between two alleles of one gene. Two alleles of one gene competing (the way \(T\) masks \(t\) in a heterozygote) is dominance, which you already know. Epistasis is a second, higher-level gene stepping in and overriding what a first gene would otherwise show.

The classic example is albinism (the inability to make the pigment melanin). Picture two genes. Gene \(B\) sets eye color, with \(B\) (brown) dominant over \(b\) (blue). Gene \(P\) decides whether any pigment is made at all, with \(P\) (pigment made) dominant over \(p\) (no pigment). If a person is \(P\_\) (at least one \(P\) allele, so pigment is produced), their eye color follows gene \(B\) in the normal way. But if a person is \(pp\), no pigment is made anywhere, so they are albino and their eyes look reddish regardless of whether gene \(B\) says \(BB\), \(Bb\), or \(bb\). The \(pp\) genotype at gene \(P\) completely overrides gene \(B\). Gene \(P\) is epistatic. Gene \(B\) is hypostatic. The Riveras could carry a recessive albinism allele the same way they carry other recessive alleles, and it would mask their eye-color gene in exactly this manner.

Epistasis reshapes the 9:3:3:1 ratio

When two genes act separately you get four visible classes in the ratio \(9:3:3:1\). Under epistasis some of those four classes look identical, so they merge, and the counts add together. Because merging classes only regroups the same 16 offspring, every epistasis ratio still adds up to 16. There are four standard named types. You should be able to recognize each one from its ratio and name it.

Here is where each number comes from, starting from the four dihybrid classes \(A\_B\_ = 9\), \(A\_bb = 3\), \(aaB\_ = 3\), \(aabb = 1\):

  • Dominant epistasis, \(12:3:1\). A dominant allele at gene A masks gene B. So every \(A\_\) plant looks the same, merging \(9 + 3 = 12\); then \(aaB\_ = 3\) and \(aabb = 1\) stay separate.
  • Recessive epistasis, \(9:3:4\). The homozygous recessive \(aa\) masks gene B. So both \(aa\) classes look the same, merging \(3 + 1 = 4\); \(A\_B\_ = 9\) and \(A\_bb = 3\) stay separate.
  • Duplicate dominant epistasis, \(15:1\). A dominant allele at either gene gives the same phenotype. So \(9 + 3 + 3 = 15\) merge, leaving only \(aabb = 1\) different.
  • Duplicate recessive epistasis, \(9:7\). You need a dominant allele at both genes. Only \(A\_B\_ = 9\) qualifies; the other \(3 + 3 + 1 = 7\) all look the same.

Lethal alleles: a class that dies is a class you never count

A lethal allele is an allele whose genotype causes death before the individual can reproduce. Setting up the Punnett square does not change at all. What changes is that one genotype class dies, so it is missing from the offspring you actually observe. Removing that class rescales the ratio among the survivors. A genotype ratio that started as \(1:2:1\) can appear as \(2:1\) once the lethal class is gone.

Worked examples

Example 1 (name the interaction). In albinism, a person of genotype \(ppBB\) and a person of genotype \(ppbb\) both look albino, even though their gene \(B\) genotypes are opposite. Is this dominance or epistasis, and which gene is epistatic?

Why it is not dominance. Dominance is one gene's two alleles competing, for example \(B\) masking \(b\) at gene \(B\). But here the two people have different gene \(B\) genotypes (\(BB\) versus \(bb\)) and still look the same. Something outside gene \(B\) is overriding it.

The interaction. Both people are \(pp\) at gene \(P\). With no pigment made, gene \(B\) has nothing to color, so gene \(P\) hides gene \(B\). Two different genes are interacting, so this is epistasis. Gene \(P\) is epistatic (it does the masking) and gene \(B\) is hypostatic (it is masked).

Aha: the test for epistasis versus dominance is simple. Ask how many genes are involved. One gene's alleles overriding each other is dominance. A second gene overriding a first gene is epistasis.

Example 2 (recessive epistasis, \(9:3:4\)). Coat color in Labrador retrievers uses two genes. Gene \(B\) sets pigment color: \(B\) (black) is dominant over \(b\) (brown, called chocolate). Gene \(E\) decides whether that pigment is deposited in the fur: \(E\) (deposited) is dominant over \(e\) (not deposited). A dog that is \(ee\) makes a yellow coat no matter what gene \(B\) says, because no dark pigment reaches the fur. Cross two double heterozygotes, \(BbEe \times BbEe\). What coat-color ratio results?

Set it up as a normal dihybrid cross. Each parent makes four gamete types (\(BE\), \(Be\), \(bE\), \(be\)), so you fill a 4 by 4 square of 16 boxes exactly as in Chapter 9. Then read each box with the coat rule: \(B\_E\_\) is black, \(bbE\_\) is brown, and any \(\_\_ee\) is yellow.

Count the phenotypes.

  • Black (\(B\_E\_\)): 9 boxes.
  • Brown (\(bbE\_\)): 3 boxes.
  • Yellow (\(B\_ee\) plus \(bbee\)): \(3 + 1 = 4\) boxes.

So the ratio is \(9\) black : \(3\) brown : \(4\) yellow, which is \(9:3:4\), recessive epistasis. The \(ee\) genotype at gene \(E\) is homozygous recessive and masks gene \(B\), merging the two would-be classes (\(B\_ee\) and \(bbee\)) into one yellow class of 4.

Aha: you never invent a special epistasis square. You fill the ordinary \(9:3:3:1\) square, then let the biology decide which boxes look identical. The masking merges classes, and the merged counts are the modified ratio.

Example 3 (a lethal allele, \(2:1\)). In Mexican hairless dogs, gene \(H\) controls coat. The genotype \(hh\) is a normal hairy dog, \(Hh\) is a hairless dog, and \(HH\) dies as an embryo. So \(H\) acts as a dominant allele for the visible trait (one copy makes a dog hairless) but has a recessive lethal effect (only two copies kill). Cross two hairless dogs, \(Hh \times Hh\). What ratio appears among the puppies that survive?

Set up the Punnett square normally. This is just a monohybrid heterozygous cross, so the genotype ratio is the usual \(1\ HH : 2\ Hh : 1\ hh\).

Remove the lethal class. Every \(HH\) embryo dies, so no \(HH\) puppies are ever born. Cross that class out. The survivors are \(2\ Hh : 1\ hh\), which is \(2\) hairless : \(1\) hairy, a \(2:1\) ratio. About one quarter of the conceived embryos are lost, so the litters are smaller than a normal cross would predict.

Aha: a lethal allele does not change how you build the square. It deletes a class afterward. When two carriers of a dominant, homozygous-lethal allele are crossed, the tell-tale sign is a \(2:1\) ratio instead of the naive \(3:1\), plus smaller litters.

Common pitfalls

Confusing epistasis with dominance. Dominance is two alleles of ONE gene, where the dominant allele masks the recessive allele in a heterozygote. Epistasis is TWO DIFFERENT genes, where one gene masks another gene entirely. Always count the genes. If a second gene is overriding the first, it is epistasis, never dominance.
Inventing a special Punnett square for epistasis. There is no separate epistasis square. You fill the ordinary dihybrid square, then merge the boxes that end up looking identical. The named ratios (\(12:3:1\), \(9:3:4\), \(15:1\), \(9:7\)) are just the \(9:3:3:1\) classes regrouped, so they always sum to 16.
Forgetting to remove the lethal class. A lethal genotype changes the observed ratio only after the fact. Build the Punnett square normally, get the usual genotype ratio, then delete the class that dies and read what is left. A \(1:2:1\) becomes \(2:1\); do not report the \(3:1\) or \(1:2:1\) you would expect if nothing died.

Connections

  • ← Chapter 9: Independent Assortment and Dihybrid Crosses: every epistasis ratio is the \(9:3:3:1\) dihybrid ratio with some classes merged, so you must be fluent with the plain dihybrid square first.
  • ↔ Chapter 10: Incomplete Dominance and Codominance: another way traits break the simple \(3:1\) rule, but that happens within one gene (the heterozygote shows a distinct phenotype). Epistasis happens across two genes.
  • ↔ Chapter 13: Polygenic Traits and Pleiotropy: polygenic traits are many genes adding up to one trait, while epistasis is genes in a hierarchy where one overrides another. Both are gene interaction, seen from different angles.
  • ← Chapter 5: Alleles, Genotype and Phenotype: the whole chapter rests on the difference between a gene and an allele; epistasis is gene versus gene, dominance is allele versus allele.
  • → Chapter 23: Genetic Disorders and the Frontier: real human traits are built from interacting genes, and some human alleles are lethal in the homozygous state, exactly the two ideas here applied to disease.

★ Landmark it points toward

Gene interaction networks. Real cells run thousands of genes whose products switch each other on and off, so epistasis is just the two-gene glimpse of a huge web. Biologists map these as gene regulatory networks and metabolic pathways. Meet the name for now.

Practice

P1 (name the epistasis type). Two double-heterozygous sweet pea plants are crossed, and their offspring appear in the ratio \(9\) purple : \(7\) white for flower color. Which of the four epistasis types is this, and what genotype rule explains it?

Answer This is duplicate recessive epistasis, ratio \(9:7\). Purple flowers appear only in the \(A\_B\_\) class (9 out of 16); every other class (\(A\_bb\), \(aaB\_\), and \(aabb\), which is \(3 + 3 + 1 = 7\)) is white.

Why it works: start from the four dihybrid classes \(9 : 3 : 3 : 1\) and ask which ones look the same. A \(9:7\) split means only the top class of 9 is one phenotype and all the rest merge into the other. That merging of \(3 + 3 + 1 = 7\) means a plant needs a dominant allele at BOTH genes to be purple; if it is homozygous recessive at either gene, it is white. Biologically, both genes make enzymes in one pigment pathway, and if either enzyme is missing the pathway breaks and no pigment forms. This is why it is also called complementary gene action: the two genes must cooperate.

Pattern (read a ratio back to its type): identify an epistasis type by seeing which parts of \(9:3:3:1\) got summed. \(9+3=12\) gives \(12:3:1\) (dominant epistasis); \(3+1=4\) gives \(9:3:4\) (recessive epistasis); \(9+3+3=15\) gives \(15:1\) (duplicate dominant); \(3+3+1=7\) gives \(9:7\) (duplicate recessive). Every valid epistasis ratio sums to 16, so if your parts do not add to 16 you have mislabeled it.

P2 (a lethal-allele cross). In mice, the coat allele \(A^Y\) makes a yellow coat and is dominant, but \(A^Y A^Y\) is lethal and dies before birth. The genotype \(A^Y a\) is yellow and \(aa\) is agouti (brown-banded). Two yellow mice are crossed, \(A^Y a \times A^Y a\). What phenotype ratio appears among the mice that are born, and what does that tell you about breeding a pure-breeding yellow line?

Answer Among survivors the ratio is \(2\) yellow : \(1\) agouti, a \(2:1\) ratio. You can never establish a pure-breeding (true-breeding) yellow line.

Why it works: fill the square normally for \(A^Y a \times A^Y a\) and you get \(1\ A^Y A^Y : 2\ A^Y a : 1\ aa\). The \(A^Y A^Y\) class is lethal and dies before birth, so it is deleted from the counts. The mice actually born are \(2\ A^Y a : 1\ aa\), which is \(2\) yellow : \(1\) agouti. A pure-breeding yellow mouse would have to be homozygous \(A^Y A^Y\), but that genotype is never viable, so every living yellow mouse is the heterozygote \(A^Y a\). Crossing two of them always regenerates the same \(2:1\), never all yellow.

Pattern (recessive lethal in a two-carrier cross): when both parents carry one copy of a dominant allele that is lethal when homozygous, cross them and you get \(1:2:1\), then strike the dead homozygous class to leave \(2:1\) among survivors. The signature is a \(2:1\) ratio (not \(3:1\)), smaller litters (about a quarter are lost), and the impossibility of a true-breeding line for the dominant trait. This is exactly the Mexican hairless dog situation with different animals.

P3 (epistasis versus simple dominance). Using the Labrador genes from Example 2, two statements describe coat color. Statement 1: black is expressed and brown is hidden in a \(Bb\) dog. Statement 2: a \(ee\) dog is yellow no matter whether it is \(BB\), \(Bb\), or \(bb\). Which statement is simple dominance and which is epistasis, and why?

Answer Statement 1 is simple dominance. Statement 2 is epistasis.

Why it works: in Statement 1, black (\(B\)) and brown (\(b\)) are two alleles of the SAME gene, gene \(B\). In a \(Bb\) heterozygote the dominant \(B\) masks the recessive \(b\), which is the ordinary definition of dominance: one gene, two alleles. In Statement 2, the masking is done by gene \(E\), a DIFFERENT gene, whose \(ee\) genotype overrides gene \(B\) completely so the \(B\) alleles never show. That is two genes interacting, which is epistasis (here recessive epistasis, since it is the homozygous recessive \(ee\) that masks). The clean test: count the genes involved. Statement 1 involves one gene; Statement 2 involves two.

Pattern (one gene versus two genes): to sort dominance from epistasis, name the genes. If a dominant allele hides a recessive allele of the same gene, it is dominance. If a whole second gene hides the effect of a first gene, it is epistasis. The words look similar and the ratios can both deviate from the simple cases, but the underlying count of genes tells them apart every time.

Watch / read preview before showing learners

↗ Khan Academy: Epistasis (article) ↗ Khan Academy: Lethal alleles (article) ▶ Bozeman Science: Epistasis (YouTube search)

Teaching notes

Opener: put two albino people on the board, one written \(ppBB\) and one \(ppbb\), and ask "why do they look the same when their eye-color genes are opposite?" Let them wrestle. The answer forces the idea that a second gene is overriding the first, which is epistasis. Do not define the word until they have felt the need for it.

Board moves: write the four dihybrid classes \(A\_B\_ = 9\), \(A\_bb = 3\), \(aaB\_ = 3\), \(aabb = 1\) once, big, and leave them up. Derive all four epistasis ratios live by circling which classes merge (\(9+3=12\); \(3+1=4\); \(9+3+3=15\); \(3+3+1=7\)). This shows they are all one ratio regrouped and every one sums to 16.

What to hammer: epistasis is genes versus genes, dominance is alleles versus alleles. Say "count the genes" until it is automatic. This is the single most tested distinction in this chapter.

Common stumble: for lethal alleles, students report the naive \(3:1\) or \(1:2:1\) and forget to delete the dead class. Make them physically cross out the \(HH\) box, then recount, so the \(2:1\) comes from the survivors only.

Pacing: spend about 15 minutes on the epistasis-versus-dominance distinction and the albinism example, 15 minutes deriving the four ratio types and working the Labrador \(9:3:4\) square, and 15 minutes on lethal alleles and the \(2:1\) result. Save the ratio-type table for review after the Labrador example, so they see a worked case before the summary.

15. DNA Replication

Before a cell divides it copies all of its DNA. A team of enzymes unzips the double helix and builds a new partner for each old strand, so every copy is semi-conservative: one old strand plus one new strand.

🧬 How to read this chapter: this is a molecular chapter, so the examples use real DNA strands and the enzymes that copy them, not the Rivera family. The copying machinery here runs during S phase of the cell cycle, the same S phase you met in Chapter 3: Mitosis and the Cell Cycle.
Prerequisites: Chapter 1: DNA: Nucleotides and Structure (you need base pairing, antiparallel strands, and the 5' and 3' ends).
This chapter: the replication fork, each enzyme and its one job, why one new strand is continuous and the other is made in pieces, and why replication is semi-conservative.
Unlocks: Chapter 20: Mutations (a mistake made while copying DNA is one way a mutation arises).

Copy one short molecule by hand first

Before meeting the machinery, do what the cell does. Here is a very short parent DNA molecule, just six base pairs. Its two strands are complementary and antiparallel (Chapter 1): A pairs with T, G pairs with C, and one strand runs 5' to 3' while its partner runs the opposite way, 3' to 5'.

top strand    3'- T A C G G T -5'
bottom strand 5'- A T G C C A -3'

Step 1: unwind. Pull the two strands apart down the middle, breaking the weak hydrogen bonds that hold the pairs together. Each old strand now sits alone and becomes a template, a pattern to copy from.

Step 2: build each new partner by base pairing. Read each template base and place its complement next to it, writing the new strand antiparallel to its template:

top template   3'- T A C G G T -5'
NEW strand     5'- A T G C C A -3'

bottom template 5'- A T G C C A -3'
NEW strand     3'- T A C G G T -5'

Step 3: read the direction. Filling in the bases is just the Chapter 1 pairing rule. What is new is direction. DNA is only ever built in the 5' to 3' direction, always by adding to a free 3' end. Say the fork opens moving to the right. On the top template the new strand's 3' end points toward the fork on the right, so it can be built in one continuous run that chases the fork. That is the leading strand. On the bottom template the new strand's 3' end points the other way, away from the fork, so it can only be built backward in short pieces called Okazaki fragments. That is the lagging strand (Figure 1).

Step 4: the result. When the strands rewind you have two daughter double helices, and each one is one old strand paired with one brand-new strand. Neither daughter is all-old or all-new. That is the whole idea of replication, seen on six base pairs before any enzyme has been named.

The pattern

Three facts fall out of that one short copy, and they hold for copying any DNA:

  • Replication is semi-conservative. "Semi" means half. Each daughter double helix is one old parent strand plus one brand-new strand. No daughter is entirely old or entirely new. This was one of the first predictions the double helix made, and experiments confirmed it (see Example 3 and Figure 2).
  • Each old strand is a template for its new partner. Because A pairs with T and G pairs with C, one strand already carries all the information needed to rebuild the other. Copying is just base pairing done twice, once on each old strand.
  • Synthesis runs 5' to 3' only. New DNA is always built by adding onto a free 3' end, never a 5' end. Because the two templates are antiparallel, this single rule forces one new strand to be built continuously (leading) and the other in short pieces (lagging).

The place where the two strands are pulled apart and copied is called the replication fork. It is a Y-shaped region: ahead of it the DNA is still zipped up, and behind it two new double helices trail off. As replication runs, the fork moves along the DNA like a zipper opening (Figure 1).

Aha: there is only one rule to remember and everything else follows from it. DNA polymerase builds 5' to 3' only. Because the two templates are antiparallel, that single rule makes one new strand continuous (leading) and forces the other to be built backward in pieces (lagging).

The mechanism: the enzymes at the fork

Building both strands by hand was easy because you could see the whole molecule. The cell cannot. It runs a relay of enzymes, and an enzyme is a protein that speeds up one specific chemical step. Each enzyme below has one clear job. Learn the job, not just the name, because Science Olympiad questions ask you to match them.

  • Helicase unwinds the double helix. It breaks the hydrogen bonds (the weak attractions) between the paired bases, separating the two strands and creating the replication fork.
  • Topoisomerase relieves supercoiling. When helicase unwinds the helix, the DNA ahead of the fork gets twisted tighter and tighter (supercoiling). Topoisomerase cuts, unwinds, and rejoins the DNA to release that tension ahead of the fork.
  • Single-stranded binding protein (often written SSB) coats the separated single strands and keeps them apart, so they do not snap back together (re-anneal) before they are copied.
  • Primase lays down a short RNA primer. DNA polymerase cannot start a strand from nothing. It can only add on to an existing end. Primase makes that starting end: a short piece of RNA that pairs with the template and gives DNA polymerase a place to begin.
  • DNA polymerase III is the main builder. It reads each template and adds matching DNA nucleotides one at a time, extending the new strand. It has one strict limit: it can only add nucleotides in the 5' to 3' direction. It always adds the next nucleotide to the free 3' end of the growing strand, and never to the 5' end. (In bacteria this main enzyme is DNA polymerase III; eukaryotes use their own polymerases for the same job.)
  • DNA polymerase I removes the RNA primers and replaces them with DNA. It uses its 5' to 3' exonuclease activity ("exo" means from the end) to trim the RNA nucleotides off the front of each fragment, then fills the gap with DNA. So the finished strand is all DNA with no RNA left in it.
  • Ligase seals the pieces together. It joins the sugar-phosphate backbones of neighboring segments by forming a phosphodiester bond (the covalent bond that links the 3' end of one nucleotide to the 5' phosphate of the next). Ligase is the glue that turns separate segments into one continuous strand.

Why one strand is continuous and the other is not

Here is the puzzle that shapes the whole fork, the same one you met in Step 3 above. The two template strands are antiparallel: one runs 5' to 3' and its partner runs 3' to 5' (Chapter 1). But DNA polymerase can build in only one direction, 5' to 3'. So the two new strands cannot both be made the same easy way.

  • On one template, DNA polymerase can chase the fork and build one long unbroken strand toward the opening fork. This new strand is the leading strand, and it is made continuously. It needs just one primer to start.
  • On the other template, the 5' to 3' rule forces the polymerase to build away from the fork. So as the fork opens more template, the polymerase has to keep restarting: it builds a short piece, jumps back toward the fork, and builds the next short piece. This new strand is the lagging strand, and it is made in pieces. Each piece is called an Okazaki fragment, and each fragment needs its own new primer.

The lagging strand is where DNA polymerase I and ligase do most of their work. Every Okazaki fragment started with an RNA primer, so DNA polymerase I must replace each primer with DNA, and ligase must then seal the gaps between fragments into one continuous backbone.

One origin or many

Replication starts at a spot called an origin of replication. Prokaryotes (bacteria, whose DNA is a single circular loop) usually have just one origin: replication begins there and travels around the whole loop. Eukaryotes (organisms whose cells have a nucleus, including humans) have very long, straight chromosomes, so they use many origins at once. Several sets of enzymes copy different sections at the same time, and ligase later joins those sections into one continuous chromosome. Many origins is how a huge genome gets copied quickly enough.

Worked examples

Example 1 (order the enzymes at the fork). A stretch of DNA is about to be copied. List, in order, what happens at the fork and name the enzyme (or protein) responsible for each step.

Follow the fork from front to back, matching each event to its one enzyme:

  1. Topoisomerase relieves the supercoiling building up ahead of the fork.
  2. Helicase unwinds the helix and breaks the hydrogen bonds between the bases, opening the replication fork.
  3. Single-stranded binding protein coats the separated strands so they do not re-pair.
  4. Primase lays down an RNA primer to give a starting end.
  5. DNA polymerase III extends the new strand 5' to 3', continuously on the leading strand and in Okazaki fragments on the lagging strand.
  6. DNA polymerase I removes each RNA primer and fills the gap with DNA.
  7. Ligase seals the backbones together with phosphodiester bonds.
Aha: read the fork like a relay. Unwind (helicase, with topoisomerase and single-stranded binding protein helping), start (primase), build (DNA polymerase III), then clean up and seal (DNA polymerase I and ligase).

Example 2 (build the new strands, then say which is leading). A short parent DNA molecule is
top strand 5'-G T C A A G-3' and bottom strand 3'-C A G T T C-5'. The fork opens moving toward the right. Write the new strand made on each template, and say which one is the leading strand.

Each new strand is the complement of its template, written antiparallel, using A-T and G-C (Chapter 1). Copy each template base to its partner:

top template    5'- G T C A A G -3'
new strand     3'- C A G T T C -5'

bottom template 3'- C A G T T C -5'
new strand     5'- G T C A A G -3'

Now decide leading versus lagging by direction. DNA polymerase builds 5' to 3' only. This time it is the bottom template whose new strand has its 3' end pointing toward the fork on the right, so that new strand is made continuously chasing the fork. That is the leading strand. The new strand on the top template has its 3' end pointing left, away from the fork, so it must be built backward in Okazaki fragments. That is the lagging strand.

Aha: leading is not always "the top strand." In the opening case the top template was leading; here the bottom template is. The only thing that decides it is strand direction, so always check the 5' and 3' ends: the template whose new 3' end points toward the fork gives the leading strand, and its antiparallel partner is the lagging strand.

Example 3 (semi-conservative result). You start with one DNA double helix in which both strands are the original "old" strands. It goes through one full round of replication. Describe the two daughter helices. Then say what conservative replication would have predicted instead.

The two old strands separate, and a new partner is built on each. So each daughter double helix has one old strand and one new strand. Both daughters look the same in makeup: half old, half new. That is what semi-conservative means (see Figure 2).

Conservative replication (which does not happen) would predict something different: one daughter would be the fully original helix (both strands old) and the other would be entirely new (both strands new). Experiments show the real result is the semi-conservative one, where every daughter carries exactly one original strand.

Aha: after one round, zero daughter helices are all-old and zero are all-new. Every daughter is a hybrid of one old strand and one new strand. That single test result is what ruled out the conservative model.
Common pitfalls:
  • Swapping enzyme jobs. Helicase unwinds and breaks hydrogen bonds. Ligase seals backbones. Primase makes the RNA primer. DNA polymerase III adds DNA nucleotides. Do not trade these roles. A common mix-up is saying helicase or polymerase joins the fragments; the enzyme that joins is ligase.
  • Thinking DNA polymerase can build both directions. It builds 5' to 3' only, always adding to the 3' end. This single limit is the reason the lagging strand exists at all.
  • Calling the wrong strand continuous. The leading strand is the continuous one (one primer). The lagging strand is the one made in Okazaki fragments (many primers). Leading follows the fork; lagging is built away from it. Which template is which depends on strand direction, not on top-versus-bottom.
  • Misreading semi-conservative. After one round no daughter is all-old and none is all-new. Each daughter is exactly one old plus one new strand. Conservative (one all-old, one all-new) is the model that was ruled out.
  • Confusing RNA and DNA in the copy. Primase uses RNA for the primer, but the finished strand is all DNA. DNA polymerase I removes the RNA and replaces it with DNA.

Connections

  • → Chapter 1: DNA: Nucleotides and Structure: complementary base pairing (A-T, G-C) and antiparallel strands are exactly what make copying possible and what force the leading versus lagging split.
  • → Chapter 3: Mitosis and the Cell Cycle: replication happens in S phase of interphase. Copying the DNA is what turns each chromosome into two identical sister chromatids before a cell divides.
  • → Chapter 4: Meiosis: gametes are made after the DNA is replicated once, and the copied chromosomes are then divided twice.
  • → Chapter 16: RNA: Types and Structure: the RNA primers here are made of the same RNA you meet next; RNA uses ribose and uracil instead of deoxyribose and thymine.
  • → Chapter 20: Mutations: if the wrong base is added during copying and it is not fixed, the error becomes a permanent change in the sequence, which is one way a mutation starts.
  • ↔ Chemistry: ligase builds a phosphodiester bond, a covalent bond between a 3' hydroxyl group and a 5' phosphate. That is the same backbone link from Chapter 1, now being formed by an enzyme.

★ Landmark it points toward

PCR (polymerase chain reaction): the laboratory version of replication. It uses a heat-stable DNA polymerase and short primers to copy a chosen stretch of DNA over and over in a tube, doubling it each cycle, until there are millions of copies to study. It powers DNA fingerprinting, disease testing, and much of modern genetics. (Just meet the name for now.)

Practice

P1. Match each enzyme or protein to its one job: helicase, topoisomerase, single-stranded binding protein, primase, DNA polymerase III, DNA polymerase I, ligase.

Answer

The matches:

  • Helicase unwinds the double helix and breaks the hydrogen bonds, forming the replication fork.
  • Topoisomerase relieves the supercoiling (over-twisting) that builds up ahead of the fork.
  • Single-stranded binding protein keeps the separated strands apart so they do not re-pair.
  • Primase lays down the short RNA primer that gives a starting end.
  • DNA polymerase III adds DNA nucleotides to the 3' end, extending the new strand 5' to 3'.
  • DNA polymerase I removes the RNA primers and replaces them with DNA.
  • Ligase seals neighboring backbones with phosphodiester bonds.

Why it works: replication is a relay, and each enzyme is specialized for one chemical step, so the fork moves in a fixed order: open it, protect it, start it, build it, clean it up, seal it. Grouping the enzymes by that order (unwinding, then starting, then building, then finishing) is easier to recall than a flat list, because each group answers one question about the fork. Naming the job, not just the enzyme, is what the event actually tests.

Pattern (match by the verb): for any "match the enzyme" item, attach a single action verb to each name (unwind, relieve, hold, prime, extend, remove, seal) and the matches fall out. Watch the two easiest to swap: primase makes RNA (a starter), while DNA polymerase III makes DNA (the real strand), and ligase seals gaps while DNA polymerase I removes primers. Same fork, opposite jobs.

P2. Explain why the lagging strand is made in short Okazaki fragments instead of one continuous piece.

Answer

The reason: DNA polymerase can only add nucleotides to the 3' end of a strand, so it builds in the 5' to 3' direction only. The two template strands are antiparallel, meaning they run in opposite directions. On one template that 5' to 3' rule lets the polymerase run continuously toward the fork (the leading strand). On the other template, building toward the fork would require going 3' to 5', which is not allowed, so the polymerase must build away from the fork instead. Each time the fork opens more template, the polymerase has to jump back to the fork and start a new short piece, and each of those pieces (an Okazaki fragment) needs its own new RNA primer. DNA polymerase I then replaces every primer with DNA and ligase seals the fragments into one strand.

Why it works: the whole problem comes from one fixed rule meeting one fixed geometry. The rule is 5' to 3' synthesis; the geometry is antiparallel strands. Because the fork keeps opening in one direction while one new strand can only grow in the opposite direction, that strand can never keep up in a single run. Making short pieces, each restarted near the fork, is the only way to copy a template whose direction points against the fork.

Pattern (rule meets geometry): when a one-way process (5' to 3' only) has to act on a two-way structure (antiparallel strands), one side runs smoothly and the other side must be broken into restartable pieces. Whenever you see a "why discontinuous" question, look for the fixed rule and the opposing direction, then the fragments explain themselves. Contrast the two strands directly: leading equals one direction plus one primer, lagging equals the opposite direction plus many primers.

P3. A DNA double helix made of two original strands is replicated once. Of the two daughter helices, how many contain an original strand, and how does this differ from the conservative model?

AnswerBoth daughter helices contain an original strand: each is one old strand plus one new strand, because replication is semi-conservative. Under the conservative model (which does not happen) only one daughter would contain the original strands, both of them, and the other daughter would be entirely new.

Watch / read preview before showing students

↗ Khan Academy: Molecular mechanism of DNA replication ↗ Learn.Genetics (University of Utah): How DNA is copied ↗ National Human Genome Research Institute: DNA replication (Talking Glossary) ↗ PDB-101 (RCSB): DNA polymerase molecule of the month

Teaching notes

Opener: write one strand on the board, 3'-T A C G G T-5', and ask "if a cell had to copy this whole molecule, what would it need to do first?" Lead them to the answer that the strands must come apart and each one becomes a template, then have the class build both new strands by base pairing before you name a single enzyme. That is the examples-first opening, and it sets up the fork, complementarity, and the leading/lagging split from one concrete case.

Board moves: draw the Y-shaped fork once and label it as you narrate the relay: helicase at the fork, topoisomerase ahead, single-stranded binding proteins on the open strands, then primer, then polymerase, then ligase. Then write the one rule big: "DNA polymerase builds 5' to 3' only." Derive leading versus lagging from that rule live, rather than stating it.

What to name: replication fork, template, semi-conservative, helicase, topoisomerase, single-stranded binding protein, primase, RNA primer, DNA polymerase III, DNA polymerase I, leading strand, lagging strand, Okazaki fragment, phosphodiester bond, origin of replication. Emphasize the job attached to each enzyme.

Check for understanding: give the enzyme list scrambled and have students match each to its verb (P1). Ask "which new strand needs only one primer, and why?" to test leading versus lagging. Ask "after one round, is any daughter helix all old or all new?" to catch the semi-conservative point (answer: no, each is one old plus one new).

Common stumble: students say helicase or polymerase joins the Okazaki fragments. Correct it immediately: ligase joins, DNA polymerase I removes primers, and those are different enzymes. The other frequent slip is claiming DNA polymerase can run both directions.

Pacing: spend about 15 minutes on the hand-copied example and the enzyme relay (Figure 1), 15 minutes on the leading versus lagging logic and Example 2, and 10 minutes on semi-conservative replication (Figure 2) and practice. Save Example 3 and Figure 2 for the end, once the mechanism is solid, since the semi-conservative result is a conclusion about the whole process.

16. RNA: Types and Structure

RNA is a nucleic acid built like DNA but with three key changes: a ribose sugar, the base uracil in place of thymine, and usually a single strand. Three kinds of RNA do the work of turning a gene into a protein.

🧬 How to read this chapter: this is a molecular chapter, so the examples use real RNA sequences and cell machinery, not the Rivera family. This chapter opens the middle of the map, the flow from gene to protein: DNA is copied into RNA, and RNA is read to build a protein.
Prerequisites: Chapter 1: DNA: Nucleotides and Structure (nucleotides, base pairing, the 5' and 3' ends).
This chapter: how RNA differs from DNA, and the jobs of the three major RNAs (mRNA, tRNA, rRNA), plus the names of the minor RNAs.
Unlocks: Chapter 17: Transcription and RNA Processing (DNA to RNA) · Chapter 18: Translation (RNA to protein)

Start with what RNA does

Before any rules, watch RNA at work on one short gene. Two concrete cases below carry the whole chapter. First a DNA template is copied into RNA. Then three different RNAs turn that copy into a protein. Read both, and then we will name what they have in common.

Case 1: copy a gene into RNA

A gene's template strand (the DNA strand that gets read) reads 3'-T A C A A G-5'. To copy it into RNA, pair each DNA base with its partner, exactly as in DNA, but with one change: RNA has no thymine, so wherever the template has an A, the RNA gets a U (uracil), never a T. Pairing base by base (A pairs U, T pairs A, G pairs C, C pairs G) gives the messenger RNA 5'-A U G U U C-3'.

Every place the template had an A, the RNA has a U, and there is not a single T in the answer. This copy is called messenger RNA (mRNA), and the copying step is transcription (Chapter 17: Transcription and RNA Processing).

Case 2: three RNAs build the protein

That mRNA now has to become a protein, and that takes three different RNAs, each doing one job. Read the mRNA three bases at a time. Each three-base unit is a codon. Our mRNA 5'-A U G U U C-3' holds two codons, AUG and UUC.

  • mRNA carries the message. It is the copy of the gene, and its codons spell out the order of amino acids for the protein.
  • tRNA brings an amino acid. A transfer RNA (tRNA) carries a three-base anticodon that base-pairs with a codon. For the codon AUG, a tRNA whose anticodon is 3'-U A C-5' pairs it and delivers the amino acid methionine.
  • rRNA forms the machine. Ribosomal RNA (rRNA), together with proteins, makes the ribosome. The ribosome threads the mRNA and joins the delivered amino acids into a chain.

The pattern

Both cases lean on the same handful of facts, and these are the heart of the chapter:

  • RNA is single-stranded, uses the sugar ribose, and uses the base uracil (U) instead of thymine (T). Those three traits are exactly what make the copy in Case 1 RNA and not DNA.
  • Different RNAs do different jobs. mRNA is the message, tRNA is the adapter that matches a codon to its amino acid, and rRNA is the machine that links the amino acids. The first letter of each name states its job: Messenger, Transfer, Ribosomal.

The rest of the chapter earns these facts. First, why RNA has that structure. Then the full cast of RNAs.

RNA versus DNA: the structure, and why

RNA (ribonucleic acid) is a chain of nucleotides, just like DNA. It uses the same three-part building block you met in Chapter 1: DNA: Nucleotides and Structure: a phosphate group, a five-carbon sugar, and a nitrogenous base. Because the building block is the same shape, RNA and DNA behave in many of the same ways. But three differences set RNA apart, and every one of them shows up on the exam.

Three differences from DNA

  • The sugar is ribose, not deoxyribose. Both are five-carbon sugars. The difference is a single atom on the 2' carbon (the carbon numbered "two prime"). Ribose carries a hydroxyl group there, an oxygen plus hydrogen written OH. Deoxyribose carries only a hydrogen atom there. "Deoxy" means exactly this: missing that one oxygen. That extra 2' OH is why the sugar is called ribose and the whole molecule ribonucleic acid.
  • The base uracil replaces thymine. RNA still uses adenine (A), guanine (G), and cytosine (C). But it never uses thymine (T). In its place RNA uses uracil (U). Uracil is a pyrimidine (one ring), the same family as cytosine and thymine, and it pairs the same way thymine did. So in RNA, A pairs with U (2 hydrogen bonds), and G still pairs with C (3 hydrogen bonds).
  • RNA is usually single-stranded. DNA is normally a double helix, two strands wound together. RNA is normally just one strand. That single strand can still fold back and pair with itself in short stretches, which is exactly how a tRNA gets its shape, but there is no second full strand running alongside it. One consequence: Chargaff's rule (%A = %T and %G = %C) applies to double-stranded DNA, so it need not hold for a single RNA strand.

The three major RNAs

Making a protein takes three different RNAs working together (Figure 2). Each has one job. Learn the job with the name.

  • Messenger RNA (mRNA) carries the gene's code. It is the copy of a gene, made from DNA during transcription, that leaves the DNA and travels to the ribosome. Its sequence of bases, read three at a time in units called codons, spells out the order of amino acids for a protein. Think of mRNA as the working copy of the recipe.
  • Transfer RNA (tRNA) brings amino acids to the ribosome. It is a short RNA, about 80 nucleotides, that folds into a cloverleaf shape. One end (the 3' end) holds a single amino acid. A three-base stretch called the anticodon sits on the opposite end. The anticodon base-pairs with a matching mRNA codon, which is how the right amino acid gets delivered for the right codon. So tRNA is the adapter that matches a codon to its amino acid.
  • Ribosomal RNA (rRNA), together with proteins, makes up the ribosome. The ribosome is the machine that reads the mRNA and joins the amino acids into a chain. rRNA is not just structural padding: it is the part of the ribosome that catalyzes the peptide bonds, which is why the ribosome is called a ribozyme (an RNA that acts as an enzyme).

The minor RNAs (recognize the names)

Beyond the big three, the exam asks you to recognize a few more RNAs by name and one-line job. You do not need their mechanisms yet.

  • Non-coding RNA (ncRNA): any RNA that is not translated into a protein. tRNA and rRNA are themselves ncRNAs, since neither codes for a protein.
  • MicroRNA (miRNA): a very short RNA that pairs with a matching mRNA and silences its expression, blocking that mRNA from being translated.
  • Small nuclear RNA (snRNA): helps carry out splicing, the removal of introns from pre-mRNA. Splicing happens only in eukaryotes.
  • Pre-messenger RNA (pre-mRNA): a freshly transcribed RNA that has not yet been processed into finished mRNA. You will meet its processing in Chapter 17: Transcription and RNA Processing.

Where RNA sits in the flow

These pieces set up the central story of molecular genetics, the flow of genetic information in one direction: DNA to RNA to protein. DNA is copied into mRNA (transcription, Chapter 17). The mRNA is then read by ribosomes, with tRNAs delivering amino acids, to build a protein (translation, Chapter 18). RNA is the working intermediate that carries a gene's instructions out of the DNA and turns them into a real product.

Worked examples

Example 1 (spot the RNA). A student is handed a nucleic acid sample and finds three facts: its sugar carries an OH on the 2' carbon, it contains uracil but no thymine, and it is a single strand. Is this DNA or RNA, and which one fact alone would have been enough?

It is RNA. All three facts point the same way, and each one is a giveaway on its own. Refer to Figure 3: the 2' OH means the sugar is ribose (DNA's deoxyribose has only H there); uracil in place of thymine is unique to RNA; and being single-stranded is the normal state for RNA, not DNA. Any single one of these identifies RNA, because DNA has none of them.

Aha: three independent clues, one answer. If you only remember one, remember uracil, because thymine belongs to DNA and uracil belongs to RNA, with no overlap.

Example 2 (transcribe DNA to RNA). A gene's template strand reads 3'-T A C G A C-5'. Write the mRNA transcribed from it, in the correct 5' to 3' direction.

Use the same rule as Case 1: copy the complement of the template, but write U wherever you would have written T (A pairs U, T pairs A, G pairs C, C pairs G). RNA polymerase reads the template 3' to 5' and builds the mRNA 5' to 3', so the two strands run antiparallel.

DNA template  3'- T  A  C  G  A  C -5'
mRNA         5'- A  U  G  C  U  G -3'

The mRNA is 5'-A U G C U G-3': two codons, AUG then CUG. As always, not one T appears in the RNA.

Aha: transcription is just complementary base pairing with U swapped in for T. The finished mRNA also matches the DNA's other strand (the coding strand, here 5'-A T G C T G-3') letter for letter, only with U wherever that strand had a T.

Example 3 (match each RNA to its job). During translation, which of the three major RNAs (a) carries the code that says which amino acids to use, (b) delivers each amino acid and reads a codon with its anticodon, and (c) forms the machine that joins the amino acids together?

Match each job to the RNA whose name states it (see Figure 2): (a) is mRNA (messenger: it carries the message, the codons); (b) is tRNA (transfer: it transfers amino acids and carries the anticodon); (c) is rRNA (ribosomal: with proteins it builds the ribosome, the machine that links amino acids). For the mRNA in Figure 2, the first codon 5'-A U G-3' is read by a tRNA whose anticodon is 3'-U A C-5', which delivers methionine.

Aha: the first letter of each name tells you the job. Messenger carries the message, Transfer transfers the amino acids, Ribosomal builds the ribosome.
Common pitfalls:
  • Leaving thymine in RNA. RNA never uses thymine. When you transcribe or write any RNA, replace every T with U. Writing a T in an RNA answer is an automatic error.
  • Wrong sugar, wrong carbon. RNA uses ribose, DNA uses deoxyribose, and the only difference is on the 2' carbon (ribose OH, deoxyribose H). Do not say the difference is at the 3' or 5' carbon.
  • Treating RNA as double-stranded. RNA is usually a single strand. Do not draw it as a double helix or apply Chargaff's rule (%A = %T, %G = %C) to it, since that rule is for double-stranded DNA.
  • Confusing codon and anticodon. The codon is on the mRNA. The anticodon is on the tRNA. They base-pair with each other; they are not the same three letters.
  • Mixing up the three RNAs. Only mRNA carries the protein code. tRNA and rRNA never code for a protein (they are non-coding RNAs).

Connections

  • → Chapter 1: DNA: Nucleotides and Structure: RNA is built from the same nucleotide plan, then changed in three ways (ribose, uracil, single strand). The base-pairing idea from Chapter 1 is what lets RNA be copied from DNA.
  • → Chapter 15: DNA Replication: replication copies DNA into more DNA. Transcription, coming next, copies DNA into RNA instead. Both rely on complementary base pairing.
  • → Chapter 17: Transcription and RNA Processing: the step that makes mRNA from a DNA template, and turns pre-mRNA into finished mRNA (cap, tail, splicing).
  • → Chapter 18: Translation: the step where the ribosome (rRNA) reads mRNA codons and tRNAs deliver amino acids to build a protein.
  • → Chapter 19: The Genetic Code: the codon chart that says which amino acid each mRNA codon stands for.
  • ↔ Chemistry: the extra 2' OH on ribose makes RNA more reactive and less stable than DNA, which is one reason cells keep their long-term records in DNA and use RNA as a short-lived working copy.

★ Landmark it points toward

The RNA world hypothesis and ribozymes: the idea that early life may have used RNA for both storing information and speeding up reactions, before DNA and proteins took over those two jobs. A ribozyme is an RNA that acts as an enzyme; the ribosome's own rRNA is a real example. (Just meet the names for now.)

Practice

P1. State three ways RNA differs from DNA.

AnswerRNA uses the sugar ribose (with an OH on the 2' carbon) instead of deoxyribose; RNA uses the base uracil (U) instead of thymine (T); and RNA is usually single-stranded, while DNA is usually double-stranded.

P2. A gene's template strand reads 3'-T A C C G G-5'. Write the transcribed mRNA in the correct 5' to 3' direction.

Answer

The calculation: pair each template base with its RNA partner (A pairs U, T pairs A, G pairs C, C pairs G), then read the RNA 5' to 3':

DNA template  3'- T  A  C  C  G  G -5'
mRNA         5'- A  U  G  G  C  C -3'

The mRNA is 5'-A U G G C C-3'.

Why it works: RNA polymerase reads the template strand and builds a complementary RNA copy, one base at a time. It uses the ordinary base-pairing rule with a single change: since RNA has no thymine, wherever the template has an A the enzyme adds a U instead of a T. The RNA grows in the 5' to 3' direction as the enzyme moves along the template in the 3' to 5' direction, so the two strands end up antiparallel, just like the two strands of DNA.

Pattern (complement, then swap T for U): to transcribe, take the complement of the template exactly as you would in DNA, but write U everywhere you would have written T. A fast check: the finished mRNA matches the other DNA strand (the coding strand) letter for letter, differing only by U in place of T. Contrast this with replication in Chapter 15: DNA Replication, which makes a DNA copy and keeps T. Only transcription introduces U.

P3. Sort these jobs to mRNA, tRNA, or rRNA: (a) forms the ribosome along with proteins; (b) holds an amino acid at its 3' end and carries an anticodon; (c) carries a gene's code as codons to the ribosome.

Answer

The calculation: (a) rRNA; (b) tRNA; (c) mRNA.

Why it works: each name names its job. Ribosomal RNA is a building block of the ribosome, so (a) is rRNA. Transfer RNA transfers amino acids to the ribosome, and to know which amino acid goes where it reads an mRNA codon with its own three-base anticodon, so (b) is tRNA. Messenger RNA is the message: it is the copy of the gene, read three bases at a time as codons, so (c) is mRNA. Only mRNA codes for a protein; tRNA and rRNA are non-coding RNAs.

Pattern (name states the job): when a set of parts have descriptive names, decode the name before guessing. Messenger carries the message, Transfer transfers the cargo, Ribosomal builds the ribosome. The same habit sorts the minor RNAs: microRNA silences an mRNA, small nuclear RNA works in the nucleus on splicing. Contrast the three by what they carry: mRNA carries codons, tRNA carries an amino acid plus an anticodon, rRNA carries no code at all.

Watch / read preview before showing students

↗ Khan Academy: DNA, RNA, and the flow of genetic information ↗ Khan Academy: tRNA and ribosomes ↗ Learn.Genetics (University of Utah): Tour of the Basics ↗ National Human Genome Research Institute: Talking Glossary of Genomic and Genetic Terms

Teaching notes

Opener: do the two opening cases live before any rules. Transcribe 3'-T A C A A G-5' into 5'-A U G U U C-3' on the board (Case 1), then walk that mRNA through the three RNAs (Case 2). Only after both cases work, write "DNA to RNA to protein" and name the three changes from DNA. Keep the arrow up all unit and point back to it.

Board moves: draw a DNA ladder next to a single RNA strand so double versus single is visual. Circle the 2' carbon on each sugar and mark H versus OH. Write the base swap big: "T becomes U in RNA." Then transcribe one short template live, saying "complement, but U for T" out loud as you go.

What to name: ribose, deoxyribose, the 2' OH, uracil, single-stranded, mRNA, tRNA, rRNA, codon, anticodon, and the minor RNAs (ncRNA, miRNA, snRNA, pre-mRNA). Name ribozyme and the RNA world only as landmarks.

Check for understanding: give a template strand like 3'-T A C G G T-5' and ask for the mRNA (answer 5'-A U G C C A-3'); watch for a stray T. Ask "codon or anticodon, and on which molecule?" Ask which RNA is non-coding (answer: tRNA and rRNA both are).

Pacing: about 10 minutes on the two opening cases, 15 minutes on RNA versus DNA and the two structure figures, 15 minutes on the three RNAs and their jobs, 10 minutes transcribing short strands. Save the transcription practice (P2) until after the T-to-U swap is automatic, since it is the step students slip on when the codon work begins in the next chapters.

17. Transcription and RNA Processing

Transcription copies a gene's DNA into RNA using the enzyme RNA polymerase. It is the first step of gene expression, and in eukaryotes it happens in the nucleus. Afterward, a eukaryotic transcript is processed into finished mRNA by adding a cap and a tail and by splicing out introns.

🧬 How to read this chapter: this is a molecular chapter, so the examples use real DNA and RNA sequences and cell machinery, not the Rivera family. It is the first arrow in the central flow of molecular genetics: DNA is copied into RNA here, and that RNA is read to build a protein in the next chapter.
Prerequisites: Chapter 16: RNA: Types and Structure (mRNA, uracil, the 5' and 3' ends, base pairing with U).
This chapter: how RNA polymerase transcribes a gene in three stages (initiation, elongation, termination), how to transcribe a DNA template into mRNA, and how eukaryotes process pre-mRNA (5' cap, poly-A tail, splicing).
Unlocks: Chapter 18: Translation (RNA to protein) · Chapter 19: The Genetic Code (which amino acid each codon spells)

See transcription happen first

Before any rules, watch a gene get copied and cleaned up on real sequences. Two concrete cases below carry the whole chapter. First a short DNA template is transcribed into mRNA. Then a freshly made eukaryotic transcript is processed into a finished, working message. Read both, and then we will name the pattern and only then meet the machinery that does it.

Case 1: transcribe a gene into RNA

A gene's template strand (the one DNA strand that gets read) reads 3'-T A C G G T-5'. To copy it into RNA, pair each DNA base with its partner exactly as in DNA, but with one change: RNA has no thymine, so wherever the template has an A the RNA gets a U (uracil), never a T. Pairing base by base (A pairs U, T pairs A, G pairs C, C pairs G) and writing the RNA in its 5' to 3' direction gives the messenger RNA 5'-A U G C C A-3'.

Every place the template had an A, the mRNA has a U, and there is not a single T in the answer. The gene's other DNA strand, the coding strand, reads 5'-A T G C C A-3'. Compare it to the mRNA: they match letter for letter, differing only by U in place of T. That is not a coincidence, and the pattern below says why.

Case 2: process the pre-mRNA into a mature mRNA

In a eukaryote, the RNA that comes straight off the gene is not yet a working message. It is called pre-mRNA, and before it can leave the nucleus and be read it is processed. Take a pre-mRNA made of, in order, a front (5') end, then Exon 1, an Intron, Exon 2, an Intron, Exon 3, then a back (3') end. Exons are the parts that code for the protein; introns are non-coding stretches sitting between them. Processing does three things: it adds a protective 5' cap to the front, adds a poly-A tail to the back, and splices the transcript, meaning it cuts out both introns and joins the exons directly. The finished message is cap + Exon 1 + Exon 2 + Exon 3 + poly-A tail.

The two introns are gone; the three exons are joined with no gaps; the cap and tail sit on the two ends. This whole clean-up step happens only in eukaryotes. A bacterium would skip it entirely.

The pattern

Both cases are the same two moves that this chapter is about.

  • Transcription is complementary base pairing with U swapped in for T. Read the template 3' to 5', pair each base, write U wherever the DNA rule would give T, and build the RNA 5' to 3'. The finished mRNA always equals the coding strand letter for letter, with U in place of every T. That is the shortcut Case 1 revealed.
  • A eukaryotic transcript is then processed: cap the front, tail the back, splice out the introns. EXons are EXpressed (kept and joined); INtrons are removed. The cap and tail are protective additions to the two ends, not code. That is what Case 2 did.

Now meet the machinery that carries out these two moves, and why each step is built the way it is.

The core idea

Transcription is the copying of a gene's DNA sequence into a strand of RNA. The enzyme that does the copying is RNA polymerase, a protein that reads one DNA strand and builds a matching RNA strand from free RNA nucleotides. Transcription is the first step of gene expression, which is the whole process of using the information in a gene to make a functional product, usually a protein. In eukaryotes (organisms whose cells have a nucleus), transcription takes place inside the nucleus, where the DNA is kept.

Transcription happens in three ordered stages: initiation, elongation, and termination. Learn them in order, because that order is a common exam question.

1. Initiation (get the machinery in place)

Proteins called transcription factors (proteins that recognize and bind a specific DNA sequence) attach to the promoter. The promoter is a DNA sequence located just before the gene that marks where transcription should begin. RNA polymerase then binds at the promoter, joining the transcription factors to form a complex that is ready to start. One point the exam tests: the promoter is where the machinery binds, but the promoter itself is not transcribed. It is a signpost, not part of the message. That is the shaded box at the left of Figure 1.

2. Elongation (build the RNA)

RNA polymerase unwinds the DNA double helix a short stretch at a time and reads one of the two strands, called the template strand. It reads the template in the 3' to 5' direction and builds the new RNA in the 5' to 3' direction, adding free-floating RNA nucleotides one at a time. The two strands run antiparallel, exactly as in DNA. The base-pairing rule is the DNA rule with one change, because RNA has no thymine:

template A → mRNA U  ·  template T → mRNA A  ·  template G → mRNA C  ·  template C → mRNA G

Because of how it is built, the finished RNA reads the same as the gene's other DNA strand, called the coding strand, letter for letter, except with U wherever the coding strand had a T. This is exactly what Case 1 showed. RNA polymerase works quickly, at a typical rate of about 10 to 100 nucleotides per second, and several polymerases can copy one gene at the same time.

3. Termination (release the transcript)

When RNA polymerase reaches a termination signal in the DNA, a sequence that marks the end of the gene, the polymerase stops and releases the newly made RNA. In eukaryotes this fresh transcript is called pre-mRNA, because it is not yet ready to be read. It must first be processed, which is the second half of this chapter.

Pre-mRNA processing (eukaryotes only)

In eukaryotes the pre-mRNA is not yet a working message. Before it can leave the nucleus and be translated, it is processed in three ways, exactly as Case 2 showed. This processing happens only in eukaryotes. Prokaryotes (cells without a nucleus, such as bacteria) skip it entirely, and in them transcription and translation can even happen at the same time.

  • Add a 5' cap. A modified guanine nucleotide (called 7-methylguanosine) is attached to the 5' end of the transcript. The cap protects that end from being broken down by enzymes, and it is the handhold the ribosome grabs to attach to the mRNA at the start of translation.
  • Add a 3' poly-A tail. A string of about 100 to 200 adenine (A) nucleotides is added to the 3' end. The tail stabilizes the mRNA and protects the coding part from being chewed away by enzymes, so the message lasts longer.
  • Splicing. A eukaryotic gene is interrupted by introns, sections of the transcript that do not code for the protein, sitting between exons, the sections that do. In splicing, complexes called snRNPs (small nuclear ribonucleoproteins, made of small nuclear RNA plus protein) cut out the introns and join the remaining exons into one continuous coding sequence. A memory aid: exons are EXpressed (kept), INtrons are IN the way (removed).

Splicing does more than clean up the message. By keeping different combinations of exons from the same pre-mRNA, a cell can make several different proteins from a single gene. This is called alternative splicing, and it is one reason organisms make far more proteins than they have genes.

Worked examples

Example 1 (transcribe a different template). A gene's template strand reads 3'-T A C G T A-5'. Write the mRNA that RNA polymerase transcribes from it, in the correct 5' to 3' direction. Then state what the coding strand reads.

Use the same move as Case 1. RNA polymerase reads the template 3' to 5' and builds a complementary RNA copy, using the base-pairing rule with U in place of T (A pairs U, T pairs A, G pairs C, C pairs G). Pair each template base in turn:

DNA template  3'- T  A  C  G  T  A -5'
mRNA         5'- A  U  G  C  A  U -3'

The mRNA is 5'-A U G C A U-3'. The coding strand is the gene's other DNA strand, which reads the same as the mRNA but with T for U: 5'-A T G C A T-3'.

Aha: transcription is just complementary base pairing with U swapped in for T. The finished mRNA equals the coding strand letter for letter, differing only by U wherever the coding strand had a T. Everywhere the template had an A, the RNA has a U, never a T.

Example 2 (order the three stages). A student lists these events out of order: "RNA polymerase releases the finished pre-mRNA," "transcription factors and RNA polymerase bind the promoter," and "RNA polymerase reads the template and adds RNA nucleotides one at a time." Put them in the order they happen and name each stage.

Transcription runs in a fixed sequence. First the machinery must assemble, then it can build, then it must stop and let go. In order:

  1. Initiation: transcription factors and RNA polymerase bind the promoter. Nothing is copied yet; this just positions the enzyme at the start of the gene.
  2. Elongation: RNA polymerase reads the template 3' to 5' and adds RNA nucleotides one at a time, growing the mRNA 5' to 3'.
  3. Termination: at the termination signal the polymerase stops and releases the finished pre-mRNA.
Aha: the three stages are always initiation, then elongation, then termination. The same three-stage naming returns for translation in Chapter 18: Translation, so learning the pattern once pays off twice.

Example 3 (splice a two-exon transcript). A eukaryotic pre-mRNA is made of, in order, Exon 1, Intron, Exon 2, plus a 5' cap and a 3' poly-A tail. Which parts end up in the finished mRNA, and which are removed? Why is any of this done, and would it happen in a bacterium?

Apply the same processing as Case 2 (Figure 2), now with just one intron. During splicing, snRNPs remove the single intron and join the two exons directly. The finished mRNA keeps the 5' cap, then Exon 1 and Exon 2 joined with no gap, then the poly-A tail. The intron is cut out and discarded. The cap and tail were added during processing: the 5' cap protects the front end and lets the ribosome attach, and the poly-A tail protects and stabilizes the transcript so it lasts longer. No, this would not happen in a bacterium; pre-mRNA processing occurs only in eukaryotes.

Aha: EXons are EXpressed (kept and joined); INtrons are removed. The cap and tail are protective additions at the two ends, not code. Keeping different exon combinations (alternative splicing) lets one gene code for several proteins.
Common pitfalls:
  • Reversing the reading direction. RNA polymerase reads the template 3' to 5' and builds the mRNA 5' to 3'. Do not build the RNA in the same direction you read the template; the two strands are antiparallel.
  • Leaving thymine in the RNA. RNA never uses thymine. When you transcribe, write U wherever the DNA rule would give T. A T in an mRNA answer is an automatic error.
  • Transcribing the promoter. The promoter is bound by proteins to start transcription, but it is not copied into RNA. It is a signpost before the gene, not part of the message.
  • Confusing template and coding strand. RNA polymerase reads the template strand. The mRNA matches the coding strand (with U for T), not the template strand.
  • Giving processing to prokaryotes. The 5' cap, poly-A tail, and splicing happen only in eukaryotes. Prokaryotes have no nucleus and skip processing; in them transcription and translation can occur at the same time.
  • Swapping exons and introns. Exons are kept, introns are removed. Splicing removes introns and joins exons.

Connections

  • → Chapter 16: RNA: Types and Structure: transcription makes the mRNA you met there, using ribose, uracil, and a single strand. The pre-mRNA and snRNA named in that chapter get their jobs here.
  • → Chapter 15: DNA Replication: replication copies DNA into more DNA and keeps thymine; transcription copies DNA into RNA and uses uracil. Both rely on complementary base pairing and both build in the 5' to 3' direction, but replication uses DNA polymerase while transcription uses RNA polymerase.
  • → Chapter 18: Translation: the finished mRNA travels to the ribosome, which reads it three bases at a time to build a protein. Translation also runs in the three stages initiation, elongation, termination.
  • → Chapter 19: The Genetic Code: the codon chart says which amino acid each mRNA codon spells; you transcribe first, then translate.
  • ↔ Cell biology: in eukaryotes transcription happens in the nucleus and translation in the cytoplasm, which is exactly why there is time to process the pre-mRNA before it leaves. Prokaryotes have no such separation.

★ Landmark it points toward

Regulation of gene expression: cells do not transcribe every gene all the time. Which genes are turned on, and how strongly, is controlled at many steps, and much of that control acts at transcription through the promoter and its transcription factors. This is why a liver cell and a nerve cell, with the same DNA, are so different. (Just meet the name for now.)

Practice

P1. A gene's template strand reads 3'-T A C T T G-5'. Write the transcribed mRNA in the correct 5' to 3' direction, and state the coding strand.

Answer

The calculation: pair each template base with its RNA partner (A pairs U, T pairs A, G pairs C, C pairs G), reading the RNA 5' to 3':

DNA template  3'- T  A  C  T  T  G -5'
mRNA         5'- A  U  G  A  A  C -3'

The mRNA is 5'-A U G A A C-3', and the coding strand is 5'-A T G A A C-3'.

Why it works: RNA polymerase reads the template strand in the 3' to 5' direction and builds a complementary RNA copy one base at a time. It uses the ordinary base-pairing rule with a single change: since RNA has no thymine, wherever the template has an A the enzyme adds a U instead of a T. The RNA grows 5' to 3' as the polymerase moves 3' to 5' along the template, so the two strands end up antiparallel, just like the two strands of DNA.

Pattern (complement, then swap T for U): to transcribe, take the complement of the template exactly as you would in DNA, but write U everywhere you would have written T. A fast check: the finished mRNA matches the coding strand letter for letter, differing only by U in place of T. Contrast this with replication in Chapter 15: DNA Replication, which copies DNA and keeps T. Only transcription introduces U.

P2. Name the three stages of transcription in order, and say in one line what happens in each.

AnswerInitiation: transcription factors and RNA polymerase bind the promoter (nothing is copied yet). Elongation: RNA polymerase reads the template 3' to 5' and builds the mRNA 5' to 3'. Termination: at the termination signal the polymerase stops and releases the pre-mRNA.

P3. In eukaryotic pre-mRNA processing, what does splicing remove and what does it keep, and what two things are added to the ends? Would this processing happen in a bacterium?

Answer

The calculation: splicing removes the introns and keeps the exons, joining the exons into one continuous coding sequence. A 5' cap is added to the front (5') end and a 3' poly-A tail to the back (3') end. No, this would not happen in a bacterium: pre-mRNA processing occurs only in eukaryotes.

Why it works: a eukaryotic gene carries non-coding introns between the coding exons, so the raw transcript cannot be read straight through. snRNPs cut out the introns and stitch the exons together, producing a message made only of coding sequence. The 5' cap protects the front end and gives the ribosome a place to attach, and the poly-A tail protects and stabilizes the transcript so it survives longer. Prokaryotes have no nucleus; they do not process their transcripts and can begin translation while transcription is still going.

Pattern (EXons are EXpressed, INtrons are removed; ends get protected): when a problem asks what splicing does, keep the exons and cut the introns; when it asks about the ends, remember the two protective additions (5' cap and 3' poly-A tail). A related idea to name: keeping different exon combinations (alternative splicing) lets one gene make several proteins. And whenever a step is "cap, tail, or splice," it is eukaryote-only, which contrasts with the shared, universal machinery of transcription itself.

Watch / read preview before showing students

↗ Khan Academy: Overview of transcription ↗ Khan Academy: Eukaryotic pre-mRNA processing (cap, tail, splicing) ↗ Learn.Genetics (University of Utah): Transcribe and Translate a Gene ↗ National Human Genome Research Institute: Talking Glossary of Transcription

Teaching notes

Opener: do the two opening cases live before any rules. Put "DNA → RNA → protein" on the board and point to the first arrow. Transcribe 3'-T A C G G T-5' into 5'-A U G C C A-3' on the board (Case 1), writing "U for T" big, then draw the exon-intron strip and physically cut the introns out to show processing (Case 2). Only after both cases work, reveal RNA polymerase and the three stages.

Board moves: draw the two DNA strands, mark the promoter as a box before the gene (write "not transcribed" on it), and set RNA polymerase on the template. Say out loud "read 3' to 5', build 5' to 3'" while sliding your hand along. For processing, draw the cap circle and the poly-A tail, then snip the introns and slide the exons together.

What to name: transcription, RNA polymerase, gene expression, promoter, transcription factors, template strand, coding strand, initiation, elongation, termination, pre-mRNA, 5' cap, poly-A tail, splicing, introns, exons, snRNPs, alternative splicing. Name regulation of gene expression only as a landmark.

Check for understanding: give a template like 3'-T A C G A T-5' and ask for the mRNA (answer 5'-A U G C U A-3'); watch for a stray T and for a reversed direction. Ask "is the promoter transcribed?" (no). Ask "cap, tail, splicing: which organisms?" (eukaryotes only). Ask "exon or intron kept?" (exon).

Pacing: about 15 minutes on the two opening cases and the three stages (Case 1, Case 2, Figures 1-2), 15 minutes on the mechanism and processing details, 10 minutes on the worked examples and practice. Save P3 until after splicing is drawn once, since the exon-versus-intron swap is the step students slip on.

18. Translation

Translation is the step that builds a protein from an mRNA. It happens at the ribosome in the cytosol: the ribosome reads the mRNA three bases at a time, and a tRNA delivers the matching amino acid for each three-base codon.

🧬 How to read this chapter: this is a molecular chapter, so the examples use real mRNA sequences and ribosome machinery, not the Rivera family. This is the second half of the gene-to-protein story. In Chapter 17 the gene was copied into mRNA. Here that mRNA is read out and turned into a protein.
Prerequisites: Chapter 17: Transcription and RNA Processing (how the mRNA is made from a DNA template), which relies on Chapter 16: RNA: Types and Structure (mRNA, tRNA, rRNA, codons, anticodons).
This chapter: the ribosome and its A, P, and E sites; codons paired by tRNA anticodons; and the three stages of translation (initiation, elongation, termination).
Unlocks: Chapter 19: The Genetic Code (the codon chart that says which amino acid each codon stands for) · Chapter 20: Mutations (how a change in the mRNA changes the protein).

Start with a real translation

Before any machinery, watch translation actually happen on one real mRNA. Here is a short messenger RNA, written 5' to 3'. Its very first codon is already the start codon AUG:

5'- AUG GGC UAC AAG UGA -3'

The ribosome, the machine that runs translation, reads this mRNA three bases at a time. Each three-base unit is a codon. So split the strand into codons, left to right, from the start:

AUG | GGC | UAC | AAG | UGA

Now look up each codon in the codon chart of Chapter 19: The Genetic Code. That chart is the fixed rulebook that says which amino acid each codon stands for. An amino acid is one of the 20 small building-block molecules that link together to make a protein. For every codon a matching transfer RNA (tRNA) arrives carrying that one amino acid, and the chain grows one amino acid longer:

  • AUG → methionine (Met). AUG is the start codon. It marks where translation begins, and it also codes for methionine, so Met is the first amino acid of every new chain.
  • GGC → glycine (Gly). The chain is now Met-Gly.
  • UAC → tyrosine (Tyr). The chain is now Met-Gly-Tyr.
  • AAG → lysine (Lys). The chain is now Met-Gly-Tyr-Lys.
  • UGA → stop. UGA is a stop codon. No amino acid is added. Translation ends and the finished chain is released.

The protein this mRNA codes for is the four-amino-acid chain Met-Gly-Tyr-Lys. That is a whole translation, start to stop. Figure 1 shows the same read as a picture: the mRNA held by the ribosome, a tRNA delivering each amino acid, and the growing chain.

The pattern
