Every translation you will ever do repeats the same moves from that one example:

  • Codons become amino acids. Read the mRNA 5' to 3' in non-overlapping groups of three. Each codon is one amino acid.
  • AUG starts, and it is methionine. The first AUG sets the reading frame (where the three-base grouping begins) and puts Met at the front of the chain.
  • A stop codon ends it. UAA, UAG, or UGA codes for no amino acid. Reaching one releases the finished chain.
  • tRNA anticodons do the matching. Each codon is read by a tRNA whose three-base anticodon pairs the codon (running in the opposite direction, A with U and G with C), and that tRNA carries the one amino acid the codon calls for.

The rest of this chapter explains the machine that carries out these moves (the ribosome and its parts), the three stages it runs through, and why each move works.

The core idea

As the opening example showed, translation reads an mRNA and builds a protein from it. Its name says what it does: it translates the language of nucleic acids (a sequence of bases) into the language of proteins (a sequence of amino acids). This is the last arrow in the central story of molecular genetics: DNA to RNA to protein. Transcription made the RNA. Translation makes the protein.

An amino acid is one of the 20 small building-block molecules that link together to make a protein. A chain of amino acids is called a polypeptide, and one or more folded polypeptides make a protein. Translation joins amino acids in the exact order the mRNA specifies.

Where it happens and what does the work

Translation takes place in the cytosol, the fluid inside the cell outside the nucleus. The machine that carries it out is the ribosome. A ribosome is built from ribosomal RNA (rRNA) plus proteins, and it has two parts:

  • The small subunit binds the mRNA and holds it so the codons can be read in order.
  • The large subunit holds the tRNAs and carries out protein synthesis. Its rRNA is what catalyzes the bond between amino acids, which is why the ribosome is called a ribozyme, an RNA that acts as an enzyme.

Between the two subunits are three chambers, called sites, that each tRNA passes through in a fixed order:

  • A site (aminoacyl-tRNA site): where a new tRNA carrying its amino acid first enters. "Aminoacyl-tRNA" just means a tRNA that is carrying (charged with) an amino acid.
  • P site (peptidyl-tRNA site): where the peptide bond forms and where the tRNA holding the growing polypeptide chain sits. A peptide bond is the chemical link that joins one amino acid to the next.
  • E site (exit site): where the now-empty tRNA leaves the ribosome.

So every tRNA travels A site, then P site, then E site, in that order. Remember it as A to P to E: it Arrives, forms the Peptide bond, then Exits.

Codons and anticodons

The mRNA is read in three-base units called codons. Each codon is matched by a three-base anticodon on a tRNA, and that tRNA carries one specific amino acid. The codon sits on the mRNA; the anticodon sits on the tRNA; the two base-pair with each other, running in opposite directions (antiparallel), following the RNA pairing rule A with U and G with C. That pairing is how the correct amino acid is brought to the correct codon. There are 64 possible codons but only 20 amino acids, so more than one codon can stand for the same amino acid. The codon chart that spells this out is Chapter 19: The Genetic Code.

The three stages

Translation runs in three stages, in the same order every time.

  • Initiation. The small subunit attaches to the mRNA and slides to the start codon AUG. A special methionine tRNA (its anticodon 3'-U A C-5' pairs AUG) binds there. Then the large subunit joins, completing the ribosome. The methionine tRNA begins in the P site, so methionine is the first amino acid of every newly translated chain.
  • Elongation. The chain grows one amino acid at a time. A charged tRNA enters the A site and its anticodon pairs the codon there. The rRNA of the large subunit catalyzes a peptide bond, transferring the growing chain onto the new amino acid. The ribosome then shifts one codon toward the 3' end: the tRNA in the A site moves to the P site, the tRNA in the P site moves to the E site, and the empty tRNA in the E site exits. The polypeptide grows from its N-terminus (the amino end, made first) to its C-terminus (the acid end, made last).
  • Termination. Eventually a stop codon (UAA, UAG, or UGA) reaches the A site. No tRNA pairs a stop codon. Instead, proteins called release factors bind there, and the finished polypeptide is freed from the ribosome. The chain is now ready to fold into its working shape.

One note about prokaryotes. Prokaryotes (such as bacteria) have no nucleus, so their mRNA is not sealed away. A ribosome can start translating an mRNA while that same mRNA is still being transcribed. So in prokaryotes transcription and translation can happen at the same time on the same molecule. In eukaryotes they cannot, because transcription is inside the nucleus and translation is outside it in the cytosol.

Worked examples

Example 1 (trace one elongation step). Using Figure 3, name what sits in the A, P, and E sites at this moment, and describe what happens in the next step of elongation. In which direction does each tRNA move?

Read the three sites from left (5' side) to right (3' side), which is E, then P, then A. Refer to Figure 3:

  • A site (codon GGA): a tRNA has just arrived carrying glycine (Gly); its anticodon 3'-C C U-5' pairs the codon.
  • P site (codon UUC): a tRNA holds the growing chain so far, N-Met-Phe.
  • E site (codon AUG): an empty tRNA is about to leave.

Next, the rRNA of the large subunit forms a peptide bond, transferring the N-Met-Phe chain onto the glycine in the A site, so the chain becomes N-Met-Phe-Gly. Then the ribosome shifts one codon toward the 3' end. The A-site tRNA (now holding the chain) moves into the P site, the old P-site tRNA moves into the E site, and the empty E-site tRNA exits. The A site is now open over the next codon, UAA, which is a stop codon.

Aha: a tRNA always travels A to P to E. It Arrives in A, the Peptide bond forms as it moves to P, and it Exits from E. The mRNA is read 5' to 3', and the protein is built N-terminus to C-terminus.

Example 2 (find the start and stop). An mRNA reads 5'-G G A U G U U C G G A U A A C-3'. Where does translation start, what codons are read, and where does it stop?

Translation does not always begin at the very first base. The ribosome scans until it reaches the first start codon AUG, and that AUG sets the reading frame (where each three-base codon begins). Scan the sequence for AUG:

5'- G G A U G U U C G G A U A A C -3'

The first AUG begins at the third base. Everything before it (the G G) is not translated. Now read in threes from that AUG until a stop codon appears:

G G | AUG | UUC | GGA | UAA | C

So the codons read are AUG (methionine, the start), UUC (phenylalanine), GGA (glycine), then UAA, which is a stop codon. Translation stops there. The trailing C after the stop is not translated. This is the same mRNA drawn in Figure 3, and the polypeptide is Met-Phe-Gly.

Aha: find the first AUG first, because it fixes the reading frame. The same bases split into completely different codons if you start counting from the wrong place. Read forward in threes from AUG until you hit UAA, UAG, or UGA.

Example 3 (count the codons). A protein is a polypeptide of \(N\) amino acids. How many codons long is the coding region of its mRNA, from the start codon through the stop codon, and how many mRNA bases is that? Check your rule on the short Met-Phe-Gly chain from Example 2, where \(N=3\).

The first amino acid is the methionine coded by the start codon AUG, so the start codon is already one of the amino-acid codons; it is not extra. That means \(N\) amino acids need exactly \(N\) codons. On top of that there is one stop codon, which codes no amino acid. So the coding region is

\[N + 1 \text{ codons} = 3(N+1) \text{ bases.}\]

Check with the Met-Phe-Gly chain, where \(N=3\): that is \(3+1=4\) codons (AUG, UUC, GGA, UAA), which is \(3 \times 4 = 12\) bases, matching 5'-A U G U U C G G A U A A-3' exactly. For a real protein of, say, \(N=200\) amino acids, that is \(201\) codons, or \(603\) bases. The real mRNA is longer still, because it also carries untranslated regions at each end plus (in eukaryotes) a 5' cap and a poly-A tail from Chapter 17: Transcription and RNA Processing.

Aha: do not add one for the start codon (methionine is a real amino acid you already counted) but do add one for the stop codon (it codes no amino acid). So codons \(=N+1\), bases \(=3(N+1)\).
Common pitfalls:
  • Reading the sites in the wrong order. The order a tRNA travels is A to P to E, not alphabetical A, B, C. A is where it Arrives, P is where the Peptide bond forms, E is the Exit.
  • Building the protein backward. The mRNA is read 5' to 3', and the polypeptide grows from its N-terminus to its C-terminus. The first amino acid (methionine) is at the N-terminus. Always write a peptide sequence N to C.
  • Thinking a stop codon codes an amino acid. UAA, UAG, and UGA code no amino acid. No tRNA pairs them; release factors end the chain there.
  • Confusing codon and anticodon. The codon is on the mRNA; the anticodon is on the tRNA. They base-pair, running antiparallel, and are not the same three letters.
  • Starting from base 1 instead of the first AUG. The start codon sets the reading frame. Bases before the first AUG are not translated, and counting from the wrong base gives the wrong codons.

Connections

  • → Chapter 16: RNA: Types and Structure: the three RNAs all meet here. mRNA carries the codons, tRNA delivers amino acids with its anticodon, and rRNA (with proteins) builds the ribosome that runs translation.
  • → Chapter 17: Transcription and RNA Processing: the previous step, which makes the mRNA that translation reads. Together the two steps are the flow DNA to RNA to protein.
  • → Chapter 19: The Genetic Code: the codon chart that says which amino acid each of the 64 codons stands for. Translation is where you use that chart; there are 64 codons for 20 amino acids, so the code is redundant.
  • → Chapter 20: Mutations: a change in the DNA changes the mRNA, which changes the codons the ribosome reads, which can change the protein. The sickle cell allele the Rivera family could carry is a single-base change that swaps one amino acid in one codon.
  • ↔ Chemistry: the peptide bond the ribosome forms is an amide bond between the amino end of one amino acid and the acid end of the next. That is why the two ends of a polypeptide are named the N-terminus (amino) and the C-terminus (carboxyl, an acid group).

★ Landmark it points toward

Protein folding and proteomics: a finished polypeptide folds into a specific three-dimensional shape, and that shape decides what the protein does. In eukaryotes, folding is helped by chaperone proteins, and much of it happens at the rough endoplasmic reticulum. Proteomics is the large-scale study of all the proteins a cell or organism can make. (Just meet the names for now.)

Practice

P1. Name the three sites of the ribosome, say what happens at each, and give the order a tRNA passes through them.

Answer

The answer: the sites are the A site (a tRNA carrying an amino acid arrives and its anticodon pairs the codon), the P site (the peptide bond forms and the tRNA holding the growing chain sits here), and the E site (the now-empty tRNA exits). A tRNA passes through them in the order A to P to E.

Why it works: the names describe the jobs. "A" is for aminoacyl-tRNA, a tRNA still carrying its amino acid, so that is where a new one enters. "P" is for peptidyl-tRNA, the tRNA that holds the peptide (the growing chain), so that is where the peptide bond forms. "E" is for exit. As the ribosome moves one codon along the mRNA, each tRNA shifts one site toward the exit, so a single tRNA is seen first in A, then in P, then in E.

Pattern (order by function, not the alphabet): whenever a part has a lettered name, decode the letter before guessing the order. Here the flow is A (Arrive) to P (Peptide bond) to E (Exit), which is not the alphabetical A, B, C order some students expect. The same habit of reading the name for the job sorts the three RNAs in Chapter 16: RNA: Types and Structure (Messenger, Transfer, Ribosomal).

P2. An mRNA reads 5'-A C A U G G C C U G A U U-3'. Find the start codon, list the codons that are translated, and name where translation stops.

Answer

The calculation: scan for the first AUG. It begins at the third base:

A C | AUG | GCC | UGA | U U

From that AUG the codons are AUG (methionine, the start), GCC (alanine), then UGA, a stop codon. Translation stops at UGA. The leading A C and the trailing U U are not translated. The polypeptide is Met-Ala.

Why it works: the ribosome reads the mRNA in three-base codons, but it does not start at base 1. It scans until it finds the first AUG, and that AUG fixes the reading frame, meaning where every later codon begins. Starting the count anywhere else would split the same bases into different, wrong codons. Reading forward in threes from AUG, you continue until the first stop codon (UAA, UAG, or UGA) lands in the A site, and there the chain is released.

Pattern (anchor on the start codon): to read any mRNA, first locate the first AUG, then group into threes from there, then stop at the first UAA, UAG, or UGA. Bases outside that span (before the AUG or after the stop) are untranslated. This is exactly the technique of Example 2, applied to a new sequence with a different stop codon (UGA here, UAA there); all three stops end translation the same way. A memory aid for the stop codons UAA, UAG, and UGA is the phrase "U Are Away, U Are Gone, U Give Away."

P3. A polypeptide is 150 amino acids long. How many codons make up the coding region of its mRNA, from the start codon through the stop codon, and how many bases is that?

AnswerThe first amino acid is the methionine of the start codon, so 150 amino acids use 150 codons; adding one stop codon gives \(150+1=151\) codons, which is \(3 \times 151 = 453\) bases.

Watch / read preview before showing students

↗ Khan Academy: Stages of translation ↗ Khan Academy: tRNA and ribosomes ↗ Learn.Genetics (University of Utah): Tour of the Basics ↗ National Human Genome Research Institute: Talking Glossary (Translation)

Teaching notes

Opener: put "DNA to RNA to protein" back on the board and point to the last arrow. Say transcription (last chapter) made the RNA; today the ribosome reads that RNA and builds the protein. Ask where in the cell this happens (the cytosol, on ribosomes).

Board moves: draw the ribosome as two stacked blobs with an mRNA threaded between them, then mark the three sites E, P, A from the 5' side to the 3' side. Walk one elongation cycle live: a tRNA arrives in A, the peptide bond forms, the ribosome shifts, and the empty tRNA exits from E. Say "A to P to E" out loud each cycle. Then write a short mRNA and read it in threes from the first AUG to a stop codon.

What to name: ribosome, small subunit, large subunit, rRNA, the A, P, and E sites, aminoacyl-tRNA, peptidyl-tRNA, peptide bond, codon, anticodon, start codon AUG, stop codons UAA, UAG, UGA, release factors, N-terminus, C-terminus, polypeptide. Name protein folding, chaperones, and proteomics only as landmarks.

Check for understanding: give the mRNA 5'-C C A U G A A A U A G G-3' and ask for the start, the codons, and the stop (start at the AUG beginning at base 3: AUG, AAA, then UAG stop, so Met-Lys). Ask "codon or anticodon, and on which molecule?" Ask which stage a stop codon belongs to (termination). Ask how many codons code a 10-amino-acid peptide plus its stop (11).

Pacing: about 10 minutes on the ribosome and its three sites, 15 minutes walking the three stages with Figure 3, 10 minutes reading mRNAs from the first AUG to a stop, and 5 minutes on the codon-count arithmetic. Save the counting example (Example 3) for last, once reading a frame from AUG to a stop is automatic.

19. The Genetic Code

The genetic code is the fixed rulebook that says which amino acid each three-base mRNA codon stands for. It turns a string of A, U, G, and C into an ordered chain of amino acids, and you read it with a codon chart.

🧬 How to read this chapter: this is a molecular chapter, so the examples use real DNA and RNA sequences and the standard codon chart, not the Rivera family. Here you learn to run the full pipeline: take a DNA template, transcribe it to mRNA, then translate the mRNA codon by codon into an amino acid sequence.
Prerequisites: Chapter 17: Transcription and RNA Processing (making mRNA from a DNA template) · Chapter 18: Translation (the ribosome reading codons, tRNAs delivering amino acids).
This chapter: why the code has 64 codons but only 20 amino acids, what degeneracy and wobble mean, the start and stop codons, and how to read a codon chart to convert a sequence into a polypeptide.
Unlocks: Chapter 20: Mutations (a change in a single base can change, keep, or delete an amino acid, and the code tells you which).

Start with the chart: decode some real codons

The genetic code is a lookup table, so before any theory, use it. A codon is a group of three consecutive mRNA bases that the ribosome reads as one unit. An amino acid is one of the 20 small molecules that link together to build a protein. The chart in Figure 1 assigns exactly one amino acid (or a stop signal) to each codon. To read a codon, fix its first base, then its second base, then its third base, and land on one entry. Work the three short cases below against the chart, then we will name what they have in common.

UUC Phe

Case 1, decode single codons. Take four codons whose first base is U and look each one up in Figure 1. Fix the first base (U), pick the column for the second base, then read the line for the third base:

  • UUU → Phe (phenylalanine)
  • UCC → Ser (serine)
  • UGG → Trp (tryptophan)
  • UAA → Stop (no amino acid; this codon ends translation)

Figure 2 zooms into just the first-base-U block so you can watch the three-step read happen.

Case 2, a short mRNA to a peptide. Now chain lookups together. An mRNA reads 5'-AUG UUU UGG UAA-3'. Split it into codons and look up each one in Figure 1, reading left to right (5' to 3'):

  • AUG → Met (methionine), the start
  • UUU → Phe (phenylalanine)
  • UGG → Trp (tryptophan)
  • UAA → Stop; translation ends and no amino acid is added

The peptide is Met-Phe-Trp, written from the N end (made first) to the C end (made last). The stop codon is not part of the chain; it only ends it.

Case 3, two codons for one amino acid. Look back at Case 1: UUU coded for Phe. Now look up UUC, which differs only in its third base. It also codes for Phe. So UUU and UUC are two different codons that mean the same amino acid. A change in the third base here does nothing to the protein.

The pattern these examples share

Those three cases reveal the five defining properties of the genetic code. Name them now; the next section explains why each one has to be true.

  • Triplet. Every codon is exactly three bases (Cases 1 and 2). The ribosome reads the mRNA three bases at a time.
  • Degenerate (redundant). Several different codons can code for the SAME amino acid (Case 3: UUU and UUC both Phe; Figure 2: all four UC codons are Ser). Usually only the third base differs, a flexibility called wobble.
  • Unambiguous. Each codon codes for exactly ONE amino acid (or one stop signal), never two. Degeneracy runs one way only: many codons can map to one amino acid, but no codon maps to many amino acids. So a lookup is never a guess.
  • Start and stop signals. AUG marks where translation begins (and codes for methionine); UAA, UAG, and UGA end translation and code for no amino acid at all (Case 2).
  • Near-universal. Nearly every organism on Earth uses these same codon assignments (named as a landmark at the end of the chapter).

The core idea: why the code looks like this

In Chapter 18: Translation you saw the ribosome move along the mRNA three bases at a time. The genetic code is the lookup table that assigns an amino acid to each of those three-base codons. Here is why the code has the shape you just used.

Why three bases per codon

There are only four RNA bases: A, U, G, and C. Ask how many bases a codon must have to name 20 different amino acids.

  • One base could name only 4 things (\(4^1 = 4\)). Too few.
  • Two bases could name \(4^2 = 16\) things. Still fewer than 20. Too few.
  • Three bases can name \(4^3 = 64\) things. That is more than enough for 20.

So the code is a triplet code: it reads three bases at a time. This gives \(4^3 = 64\) possible codons. But there are only 20 standard amino acids, so there are far more codons than amino acids to assign them to.

The code is degenerate (redundant)

Because 64 codons must cover only 20 amino acids plus a stop signal, most amino acids are named by more than one codon. This property, where several different codons code for the same amino acid, is called degeneracy (also called redundancy). Degeneracy does not mean the code is sloppy. It means the code has spare capacity, and a single amino acid usually has a small family of codons.

The extra codons are not spread at random. Usually, but not always, the codons for one amino acid share the same first two bases and differ only in the third base. This flexibility in the third base is called wobble. For example, all four codons that begin with UC (UCU, UCC, UCA, UCG) code for serine: the third base can be anything and the amino acid does not change. You can see this at a glance in the UC column of Figure 2. Wobble is why the third base is often the least important base in a codon.

The start and stop codons

Three facts about the code are worth memorizing, because reading a sequence starts and ends with them.

  • Start codon: AUG. The codon AUG codes for the amino acid methionine (Met), and it also marks where translation begins. Every polypeptide starts with methionine at its first position (although a cell may later trim that methionine off). AUG is special twice: it is the start signal and it is methionine wherever else it appears.
  • Stop codons: UAA, UAG, UGA. These three codons code for no amino acid at all. When the ribosome reaches a stop codon, it releases the finished polypeptide and translation ends. A mnemonic for the three stops is "U Are Amazing, U Are Genius, U Got A's" (UAA, UAG, UGA).

Direction: N-terminus to C-terminus

A polypeptide is a chain of amino acids (a protein is one or more polypeptides). The chain has two different ends. The end made first has a free amino group and is called the N-terminus. The end made last has a free carboxyl group and is called the C-terminus. Because the ribosome reads mRNA from 5' to 3' and adds amino acids in that order, a polypeptide is always written from N-terminus to C-terminus, matching the 5' to 3' reading of the mRNA. So the amino acid from the first codon is on the left (N end) and the last one is on the right (C end).

The full pipeline: DNA to mRNA to protein

Putting the last three chapters together, converting a gene into an amino acid sequence is a two-step process, and the order matters. The one rule to hold onto: transcribe first, then translate. Do not try to read amino acids straight off DNA.

  1. Transcribe. Take the DNA template strand and build the complementary mRNA, using the base-pairing rule with uracil in place of thymine (A pairs U, T pairs A, G pairs C, C pairs G), reading the finished mRNA 5' to 3'. This is Chapter 17: Transcription and RNA Processing.
  2. Translate. Break the mRNA into codons starting at the AUG, then look up each codon on the chart to get its amino acid, stopping at the first stop codon. This is Chapter 18: Translation plus this chapter's chart.

Worked examples

Example 1 (the full pipeline, the standard wiki example). A gene's DNA template strand reads 3'-TAC AGG TAG CTA GTT ATT-5'. Transcribe it to mRNA, then translate the mRNA into an amino acid sequence, written N to C.

Step 1, transcribe. Pair each template base with its RNA partner (A pairs U, T pairs A, G pairs C, C pairs G) and read the mRNA 5' to 3'. Every T on the template becomes an A, and every A becomes a U, never a T:

DNA template  3'- TAC AGG TAG CTA GTT ATT -5'
mRNA         5'- AUG UCC AUC GAU CAA UAA -3'

Step 2, translate. The mRNA already begins with AUG, so the reading frame is set from the first base. Look up each codon on the chart, one at a time:

  • AUG → Met (methionine), the start
  • UCC → Ser (serine); see the highlighted cell in Figure 2
  • AUC → Ile (isoleucine)
  • GAU → Asp (aspartic acid)
  • CAA → Gln (glutamine)
  • UAA → Stop; translation ends and no amino acid is added

The polypeptide is Met-Ser-Ile-Asp-Gln, written N to C (in one-letter code, MSID Q). The stop codon is not part of the chain; it only ends it.

Aha: transcribe first, then translate. The AUG at the start does double duty: it fixes the reading frame and it is the first amino acid, methionine. Watch the third codon carefully: CAA is glutamine (Gln), not aspartic acid; only GAU and GAC are Asp.

Example 2 (degeneracy in action). The DNA template codon for serine in Example 1 was AGG, giving the mRNA codon UCC. Suppose the third DNA base changed so the template codon became AGA instead. Transcribe the new codon and translate it. Does the amino acid change?

Transcribe AGA (template, 3' to 5') the same way: A pairs U, G pairs C, A pairs U, giving the mRNA codon UCU (5' to 3'). Look up UCU on the chart in Figure 2: it is in the UC column, so it codes for serine (Ser), exactly like UCC. The amino acid does not change. Both UCU and UCC code for serine because they share the first two bases UC and differ only in the wobble (third) base.

Aha: a change in the third base of a codon often does nothing to the protein, because degeneracy and wobble let several third-base variants code for the same amino acid. This is exactly why a "silent mutation" exists, which you meet in Chapter 20: Mutations.

Example 3 (find the start, set the reading frame). An mRNA reads 5'-C A U G G C U U A A-3'. There are bases before the start codon. Find the AUG, set the reading frame, and translate to the first stop.

The reading frame is fixed by the first AUG, not by the physical 5' end. Scan left to right for AUG. The first base C is not part of any AUG. Starting at the second base gives A-U-G, the start codon. So the frame begins at the second base, and the leading C is untranslated. Group from there in threes:

C | AUG | GCU | UAA

  • AUG → Met (start)
  • GCU → Ala (alanine)
  • UAA → Stop

The polypeptide is Met-Ala. The single leading C and everything after the stop are ignored.

Aha: the start codon, not the end of the molecule, sets the reading frame. Grouping the same bases from a different starting point (a different frame) would spell completely different codons, which is why an insertion or deletion that shifts the frame is so damaging.

Reference: the 20 standard amino acids

You do not need to memorize every codon. You should recognize the 20 amino acids by their three-letter and one-letter codes, because exam answers use both (for example, the peptide from Example 1 is Met-Ser-Ile-Asp-Gln, or MSIDQ). Methionine is marked as the start.

Amino acid3-letter1-letter Amino acid3-letter1-letter
AlanineAlaA LeucineLeuL
ArginineArgR LysineLysK
AsparagineAsnN Methionine (Start)MetM
Aspartic acidAspD PhenylalaninePheF
CysteineCysC ProlineProP
GlutamineGlnQ SerineSerS
Glutamic acidGluE ThreonineThrT
GlycineGlyG TryptophanTrpW
HistidineHisH TyrosineTyrY
IsoleucineIleI ValineValV
Common pitfalls:
  • Reading amino acids straight off DNA. Always transcribe DNA to mRNA first, then translate the mRNA. Codon charts are written in RNA bases (they use U, not T), so you cannot look up a DNA triplet directly.
  • Forgetting to swap T for U. During transcription, an A on the template becomes U in the mRNA, never T. RNA has no thymine. A stray T means you skipped the swap.
  • Grouping into codons before finding AUG. The reading frame is set by the start codon, not by the first base of the molecule. Find the AUG, then group in threes from there. Bases before the AUG are not translated.
  • Treating a stop codon as an amino acid. UAA, UAG, and UGA code for no amino acid. They end the chain and are never written into the polypeptide.
  • Thinking degeneracy means one codon codes for many amino acids. It is the reverse. Degeneracy means many codons can code for the SAME amino acid. Each codon still codes for exactly one amino acid (or stop).
  • Confusing the codon and the anticodon. The codon is on the mRNA and is what you look up on the chart. The anticodon is on the tRNA and pairs with the codon. Look up the mRNA codon, not the tRNA anticodon.

Connections

  • → Chapter 16: RNA: Types and Structure: mRNA carries the codons, tRNA carries the matching anticodon, and rRNA builds the ribosome that reads the code. The genetic code is the rulebook those RNAs follow.
  • → Chapter 17: Transcription and RNA Processing: transcription makes the mRNA whose codons you translate here. Step 1 of the pipeline.
  • → Chapter 18: Translation: translation is the ribosome physically reading codons and joining amino acids N to C. This chapter is the chart that tells it which amino acid goes with each codon.
  • → Chapter 20: Mutations: a mutation changes one or more bases, which changes codons. Whether the protein changes depends on the code: a third-base change is often silent (degeneracy), a change to a different amino acid is missense, and a change to a stop codon is nonsense.
  • → Chapter 5: Alleles: the sickle cell allele the Rivera family could carry is a single DNA base change that turns one mRNA codon from glutamic acid (Glu) into valine (Val). The genetic code is what makes that single base swap change the protein.

★ Landmark it points toward

The near-universality of the genetic code: almost every organism on Earth, from bacteria to humans, uses the same codon-to-amino-acid assignments. AUG means methionine in a bacterium and in you. This shared code is strong evidence that all life descends from a common ancestor, and it is why a human gene can be read correctly inside a bacterium. A few minor exceptions exist (for example in some mitochondria), but the standard code is essentially universal. (Just meet the idea for now.)

Practice

P1. A gene's DNA template strand reads 3'-TAC ACC GGA ATC-5'. Transcribe it to mRNA and translate it to a polypeptide (N to C). Use this codon key: AUG = Met, UGG = Trp, CCU = Pro, UAG = Stop.

Answer

The calculation: transcribe first (A pairs U, T pairs A, G pairs C, C pairs G; read 5' to 3'), then translate.

DNA template  3'- TAC ACC GGA ATC -5'
mRNA         5'- AUG UGG CCU UAG -3'

Translate with the key: AUG = Met, UGG = Trp, CCU = Pro, UAG = Stop. The polypeptide is Met-Trp-Pro (the stop adds nothing).

Why it works: a codon chart is written in RNA, so you cannot read amino acids off the DNA. You must transcribe to mRNA first, swapping U for every T, then read the mRNA in triplets from the AUG. The mRNA already starts with AUG, so the frame is set from the first base. You stop at the first stop codon and do not write it into the chain. Notice UGG is the single codon for tryptophan: it is one of the few amino acids with no wobble partner, so its third base is not free.

Pattern (transcribe then translate): for any DNA-to-protein problem, do it in two locked steps in this order. Step 1: build the mRNA by complementary base pairing with U for T, and write it 5' to 3'. Step 2: group from the AUG in threes and look each codon up, stopping at the first stop. Reversing the order, or reading triplets off the DNA, is the most common way to get a wrong protein. Contrast this with a pure translation problem (like P2), where the mRNA is already given and you skip step 1.

P2. An mRNA reads 5'-G C A U G A A A U G A-3'. Find the start codon, set the reading frame, and translate to the first stop. Use this key: AUG = Met, AAA = Lys, UGA = Stop.

Answer

Scan for the first AUG. The bases G and C are not it; starting at the third base gives A-U-G. So the frame starts at the third base, and the leading GC is untranslated. Group in threes from there:

GC | AUG | AAA | UGA

Translate: AUG = Met, AAA = Lys, UGA = Stop. The polypeptide is Met-Lys.

Why it works: the ribosome does not start at the physical 5' end. It scans for the first AUG and locks the reading frame there, so any bases before the AUG are ignored. Once the frame is set, you read strict, non-overlapping triplets until a stop codon appears. Here the stop UGA ends the chain after two amino acids, and the last base is inside that stop codon.

Pattern (start codon sets the frame): before you group anything, hunt for the AUG. Grouping the same string from a different offset (a different frame) spells different codons and a different protein, which is why finding the correct start is step zero of every translation. This is also the reason a frameshift, an insertion or deletion whose length is not a multiple of three, is so destructive: it shifts every downstream codon.

P3. The codons GGU, GGC, GGA, and GGG all code for glycine (Gly). (a) What property of the genetic code does this show? (b) Which base position is free to vary, and what is that flexibility called? (c) In one sentence, why does having 64 codons for 20 amino acids make this possible?

Answer

The calculation (the answers): (a) degeneracy (redundancy): several codons code for the same amino acid. (b) the third base; that flexibility is called wobble. (c) with \(4^3 = 64\) codons and only 20 amino acids, there are far more codons than amino acids, so the spare codons are used to give most amino acids a family of synonyms.

Why it works: the code has to assign 64 codons to just 20 amino acids plus a stop signal, so on average each amino acid gets about three codons. The extra codons are not random: codons for the same amino acid usually share their first two bases and differ only in the third. That is wobble, and it makes the third base the least informative position in a codon. For glycine, all four GG codons collapse to the same amino acid, so the third base carries no information at all.

Pattern (degeneracy versus specificity): read the mapping in the correct direction. Each codon still specifies exactly one amino acid (the code is not ambiguous). It is the reverse direction that is many-to-one: one amino acid can be named by many codons. When you see a set of codons sharing the first two bases mapping to one amino acid, name it degeneracy and point to the wobble (third) base. This is the concept that makes a silent mutation possible in Chapter 20: Mutations: change the wobble base and the protein is unchanged.

Watch / read preview before showing students

↗ Khan Academy: The genetic code ↗ Khan Academy: Translation (mRNA to protein) ↗ Learn.Genetics (University of Utah): How to read the codon table ↗ National Human Genome Research Institute: Genetic Code glossary entry

Teaching notes

Opener: hand out the full codon chart (Figure 1) and, before any theory, have the class decode three or four codons cold and then translate the short mRNA 5'-AUG UUU UGG UAA-3' to Met-Phe-Trp (Figures 3 and 4). Let the properties fall out of the doing. Then put "4 bases, 3 at a time" on the board and ask how many codons that makes. Walk the class from \(4^1=4\) to \(4^2=16\) to \(4^3=64\), then ask why 64 codons for only 20 amino acids. That surplus is the whole chapter: it forces degeneracy.

Board moves: show one block of the codon chart (the first-base-U block from Figure 2) rather than the whole 64-cell table at once, so the structure is visible. Circle the UC column and say "four codons, one amino acid, that is degeneracy." Then transcribe and translate the wiki example live, aligning codons under amino acids exactly as in Figure 5, saying "transcribe first, then translate" out loud.

What to name: codon, triplet code, \(4^3=64\), amino acid, degeneracy (redundancy), wobble, unambiguous (one codon means one amino acid), start codon AUG (methionine), stop codons UAA, UAG, UGA, polypeptide, N-terminus, C-terminus. Name the near-universality of the code only as a landmark.

Common stumble: students group the mRNA into codons before finding the AUG, or they read triplets straight off the DNA. Drill the two-step order (find AUG, then transcribe-then-translate) until it is automatic. Also watch for a stray T left in a transcribed mRNA.

Pacing: about 10 minutes decoding real codons off the chart (the examples-first opening), 10 minutes on why the code is a degenerate triplet code, 10 minutes on the start and stop codons and direction, 15 minutes on the full DNA-to-protein pipeline with Figure 5, then 10 minutes on practice. Save P3 (degeneracy and wobble) for last, since it lands best once students have seen a third-base change do nothing in Example 2.

20. Mutations

A mutation is a change in the DNA base sequence. It is the ultimate source of new alleles, the raw material that variation and evolution act on. A mutation can be harmful, harmless, or once in a while helpful.

🧬 How to read this chapter: this is a molecular chapter, so the examples use real codon sequences, a codon key, and real disorders, not the Rivera family. We start by changing one base at a time on a short mRNA and watching what happens to the protein, then we name the types. One natural tie-back appears along the way: the sickle cell allele the Riveras could carry is a specific single-base change.
Prerequisites: Chapter 15: DNA Replication (a copying error that is not fixed becomes a mutation) · Chapter 19: The Genetic Code (codons, the reading frame, and the redundancy of the code).
This chapter: gene-level point mutations (silent, missense, nonsense) and indels (frameshift or in-frame), then chromosome-level mutations (addition, deletion, translocation), with sickle cell as the worked anchor.
Unlocks: Chapter 21: Chromosomal Disorders and Karyotypes (chromosome-scale mutations on a karyotype) · Chapter 23: Genetic Disorders and the Frontier (the disorders these mutations cause)

See it first: change one base at a time

Before we define any terms, let us do the thing itself. Take one short piece of mRNA, translate it, then change a single base and re-translate. The whole chapter is contained in what you see happen. To read the codons (groups of three mRNA bases) you need a way to turn each codon into an amino acid. In a real problem you use the full codon chart from Chapter 19: The Genetic Code. Here is the small key used throughout this chapter.

CodonAmino acid CodonAmino acid CodonAmino acid
AUGMet (start)UAUTyrGGUGly
AAALysUGUCysGGAGly
AAGLysUGGTrpCAAGln
AGAArgUAASTOPAUAIle
GAGGluUGASTOPGUGVal

Our starting mRNA is 5'-AUG AAA UAU GGU-3'. Read it in codons with the key: AUG = Met, AAA = Lys, UAU = Tyr, GGU = Gly. So the protein is Met-Lys-Tyr-Gly. Now watch three different single-base swaps, each on this same sequence.

Case 1 (change the 4th codon, GGU to GGA). Swap the last base: GGU becomes GGA. Re-translate the changed codon. What happens to the protein?

The key gives GGU = Gly and GGA = Gly. The base changed, but the amino acid is still Gly, so the protein Met-Lys-Tyr-Gly is completely unchanged. See the "Silent" row of Figure 1. This is possible because the genetic code is degenerate (redundant): most amino acids are spelled by more than one codon, so some base swaps land on a different spelling of the same amino acid.

Case 2 (change the 2nd codon, AAA to AGA). Swap the middle base: AAA becomes AGA. Re-translate. What happens to the protein?

The key gives AAA = Lys and AGA = Arg. This time exactly one amino acid changes: the protein reads Met-Arg-Tyr-Gly instead of Met-Lys-Tyr-Gly. One base swapped, one codon changed, one amino acid replaced. See the "Missense" row of Figure 1.

Case 3 (change the 3rd codon, UAU to UAA). Swap the last base: UAU becomes UAA. Re-translate. What happens to the protein?

The key gives UAU = Tyr, but UAA is a STOP codon. When the ribosome reaches a stop it releases the chain, so translation ends early. The protein is cut short (truncated) to just Met-Lys, and Tyr and Gly are never added. See the "Nonsense" row of Figure 1.

Aha: one single-base swap did three completely different things across the three cases: nothing at all (Case 1), one amino acid changed (Case 2), or the protein cut short (Case 3). The outcome depends entirely on which codon changed and which position inside it changed, so you must always translate the codon before and after and compare.

A real missense: sickle cell anemia

Case 2 above (one amino acid swapped) is not just a toy. It is exactly what causes sickle cell anemia, a serious inherited disorder. Sickle cell comes from a change in the gene for beta-globin, one of the protein chains in hemoglobin (the oxygen-carrying protein of red blood cells). At codon 6 of that gene, the DNA coding strand changes from GAG to GTG (a single A becomes a T). In the mRNA this reads GAG to GUG. Translate with the key: GAG = Glu (glutamic acid) and GUG = Val (valine). So the sixth amino acid changes from Glu to Val.

Only one base changed, one codon changed, and one amino acid changed. No base was added or removed, so this is a single-base substitution, a point mutation, and specifically a missense mutation because it swaps one amino acid for a different one. That single change distorts the hemoglobin protein enough to deform the red blood cells into a sickle shape. This is the recessive allele the Rivera family could carry (an autosomal recessive allele, so carriers are unaffected), which you will meet again in Chapter 13: Polygenic Traits and Pleiotropy and Chapter 23: Genetic Disorders and the Frontier.

Aha: sickle cell is the classic proof that "small" does not mean "harmless." A single-base missense mutation, one letter out of billions, is enough to cause a serious inherited disorder.

An inserted base garbles everything downstream

Every change so far swapped one base for another, leaving the total base count the same. Now do something different: add a base. Start again with 5'-AUG AAA UAU GGU-3' (Met-Lys-Tyr-Gly) and insert a single C right after the AUG start codon, giving 5'-AUG CAA AUA UGG U...-3'. The ribosome always reads in fixed, non-overlapping groups of three starting at the AUG, so re-group the new sequence from the start:

AUG  CAA  AUA  UGG
Met  Gln  Ile  Trp

The new protein is Met-Gln-Ile-Trp. Compare it to the original Met-Lys-Tyr-Gly. Only the first codon, which sits before the insertion point, is unchanged. Every codon after the inserted base has been re-grouped into a completely different triplet, so every downstream amino acid changed at once (Figure 3). This chain-reaction re-grouping is called a frameshift.

Aha: an indel does not change one spot and stop there. A frameshift is a chain reaction: because the ribosome always reads in fixed groups of three from the start, one extra base pushes every later base into the wrong triplet. That is why an added or removed base is usually far more damaging than a single substitution.

Find the pattern

Look back across the four cases. Two different things happened, and they split cleanly by what the change did to the base count.

  • In Cases 1, 2, 3 and in sickle cell, one base was swapped for another. The total number of bases stayed the same, so the reading frame stayed lined up and at most one codon changed. This is a substitution, and its effect (nothing, one amino acid, or an early stop) depends only on what that one codon now codes.
  • In the insertion case, a base was added. The base count changed, so every triplet downstream was re-grouped. This is an indel (insertion or deletion), and it can garble the entire rest of the protein.

So the single most useful question about any small mutation is: did it keep the base count the same (a substitution) or change it (an indel)? That one distinction sorts almost everything that follows. Now we name the types precisely and say why each behaves as it does.

The core idea: naming the mutation types

A mutation is any change in the sequence of bases in DNA. Some mutations come from an error made during copying that the cell did not repair (see Chapter 15: DNA Replication). Others come from outside agents. Whatever the cause, a mutation matters for one big reason: it is the ultimate source of new alleles. An allele is one version of a gene, and a brand new version can only arise when the DNA sequence itself changes. So mutation is the raw material of genetic variation and, over long times, of evolution.

A common misconception is that "mutation" means "damage." It does not. A mutation can be harmful, it can be neutral (harmless, with no real effect, as in Case 1), or it can occasionally be helpful in a particular environment. Harmful is only one of the three outcomes.

Mutations happen at two scales. Gene-level mutations change one or a few bases inside a single gene: these are point mutations and small insertions or deletions, the cases you just worked. Chromosome-level mutations move or lose whole segments of a chromosome and are large enough to see on a karyotype (a chart of all the chromosomes, covered in Chapter 21: Chromosomal Disorders and Karyotypes).

Point mutations: the three effects of a substitution

A point mutation is a substitution: one base is swapped for a different base, so the total number of bases does not change. Because the ribosome reads in codons, a single substitution changes at most one codon, giving one of three effects. Which one you get depends on which codon changes and which position inside it changes.

  • Silent mutation: the changed codon still codes for the same amino acid, so the protein is unchanged (Case 1, GGU to GGA). This is possible because the genetic code is degenerate, meaning most amino acids are spelled by more than one codon. A change that lands on a different codon for the same amino acid is silent.
  • Missense mutation: the changed codon now codes for a different amino acid, so the protein has one amino acid replaced (Case 2, AAA to AGA; and sickle cell, GAG to GUG). The effect ranges from tiny to serious depending on which amino acid changes and where.
  • Nonsense mutation: the changed codon becomes a premature stop codon (UAA, UAG, or UGA), so translation ends early and the protein is cut short (truncated) and usually nonfunctional (Case 3, UAU to UAA).

Insertions and deletions: the frameshift, and the multiple-of-3 rule

An insertion adds one or more bases, and a deletion removes one or more bases. Together these are called indels. Unlike a substitution, an indel changes the total number of bases, and that is what makes it dangerous. The ribosome reads the mRNA in a fixed reading frame: it starts at the AUG and takes the bases in non-overlapping groups of three. Add or remove bases and everything after that point gets re-grouped into new triplets, the frameshift you saw in Figure 3.

Here is the key rule the exam tests. An indel causes a frameshift only when the number of bases added or removed is not a multiple of 3. If the count is a multiple of 3 (that is 3, 6, 9, and so on), the reading frame is preserved. This is called an in-frame indel: it adds or removes whole codons, so the protein gains or loses whole amino acids, but every codon downstream stays lined up and unchanged.

Domain check, worth stating outright: a substitution is never a frameshift. Swapping one base keeps the base count the same, so the frame is preserved. A frameshift can only come from an insertion or deletion whose size is not a multiple of 3. This is why sickle cell (a substitution) is a missense point mutation, not a frameshift.

Chromosome-level mutations

Some mutations are far larger than a single base. They move, add, or lose entire segments of a chromosome, so they are visible on a karyotype and are usually much more serious. The Science Olympiad wiki names three main types.

  • Addition (insertion): extra genetic material is added, ranging from a single base pair up to a whole section of a chromosome placed in the wrong location.
  • Deletion: a segment of a chromosome is lost. Small deletions are often less harmful, but large deletions can be fatal. One real example is Williams syndrome, caused by a deletion of a segment on chromosome 7.
  • Translocation: a piece of one chromosome moves to a different chromosome. In a reciprocal translocation, the most common form, two chromosomes swap segments with each other. A translocation in a body cell can lead to some forms of cancer. A translocation involving chromosome 21 can even cause a specific inherited form of Down syndrome.

What causes mutations, and who inherits them

Causes and mutagens

Mutations arise in two broad ways. Many are spontaneous: they begin as a copying error during DNA replication that the cell's proofreading did not repair (Chapter 15: DNA Replication). Others are induced by outside agents called mutagens, which raise the mutation rate above its natural level. Common mutagens include ultraviolet (UV) light, ionizing radiation such as X-rays, and certain chemicals (for example, some found in tobacco smoke). A mutagen that leads to cancer is also called a carcinogen.

Germline versus somatic

Where a mutation happens decides whether it can be inherited. A germline mutation occurs in a cell that makes gametes (eggs or sperm), so it is passed on to offspring and to every cell of the next generation. These are the mutations that heredity tracks, and the sickle cell allele is one of them. A somatic mutation occurs in an ordinary body cell (a somatic cell). It affects only that person, is copied into that cell's descendants within the body, and is not passed to children. Many cancers begin as somatic mutations, which is exactly why a translocation in a body cell (Figure 4) can cause cancer without being inherited.

Common pitfalls:
  • Calling a substitution a frameshift. A substitution swaps one base for another, so the base count does not change and the reading frame is preserved. A substitution can never cause a frameshift. Only an insertion or a deletion can.
  • Calling every indel a frameshift. An indel shifts the frame only when the number of bases added or removed is not a multiple of 3. Adding or removing 3, 6, or 9 bases keeps the frame (an in-frame indel), which adds or removes whole amino acids but leaves the downstream codons lined up.
  • Thinking "silent" means "no mutation." A silent mutation is still a real, heritable change in the DNA. It is called silent only because the amino acid sequence of the protein does not change, thanks to the degeneracy of the code.
  • Misnaming sickle cell. Sickle cell anemia is a single-base missense point mutation (GAG to GTG in DNA, Glu to Val). It is not a deletion and not a frameshift.
  • Assuming a mutation is always harmful. Mutations can be harmful, neutral, or occasionally helpful. Harmful is only one of the three outcomes.
  • Confusing germline and somatic. Only germline mutations (in eggs or sperm) are inherited. A somatic mutation, such as many that cause cancer, affects only the individual and is not passed to offspring.

Connections

  • → Chapter 15: DNA Replication: many mutations begin as a copying error during replication that the cell's proofreading did not repair.
  • → Chapter 19: The Genetic Code: the codon chart, the reading frame, and the degeneracy of the code are exactly what make silent mutations possible and what a frameshift destroys.
  • → Chapter 16: RNA - Types and Structure and Chapter 18: Translation: because codons are read three bases at a time on the mRNA, the reading frame is the thing an indel can wreck.
  • → Chapter 21: Chromosomal Disorders and Karyotypes: the chromosome-scale mutations here (addition, deletion, translocation) are large enough to see on a karyotype.
  • → Chapter 23: Genetic Disorders and the Frontier: mutations are the cause of real disorders, including sickle cell anemia, cystic fibrosis, and many more.
  • → Chapter 13: Polygenic Traits and Pleiotropy: the one sickle cell base change has many downstream effects, an example of pleiotropy (one gene affecting many traits).
  • ↔ Evolution: mutation is the ultimate source of the new alleles that genetic variation and natural selection act on. Without mutation there would be nothing new for evolution to select.

★ Landmarks it points toward

  • CRISPR gene editing: a modern tool that lets scientists deliberately cut and change a chosen DNA sequence, turning mutation from an accident into something engineers can direct. (Name only.)

Practice

P1. Using the codon key, classify each single-base substitution as silent, missense, or nonsense: (a) AAA (Lys) changes to AAG; (b) UAU (Tyr) changes to UGU; (c) UGG (Trp) changes to UGA.

Answer

The calculation: translate each codon before and after, then compare. (a) AAA = Lys, AAG = Lys, same amino acid, so silent. (b) UAU = Tyr, UGU = Cys, different amino acid, so missense. (c) UGG = Trp, UGA = STOP, so nonsense (a premature stop).

Why it works: a substitution changes at most one codon, so the only question is what that one codon now codes. If it codes the same amino acid the protein is unchanged (silent, which the degenerate code allows). If it codes a different amino acid the protein carries one substitution (missense). If it becomes one of the three stop codons UAA, UAG, or UGA, translation halts and the rest of the protein is never made (nonsense). You cannot tell the type by looking at the DNA change alone. You must translate both versions.

Pattern (translate both, then compare): for any point mutation, write the amino acid before and the amino acid after. Same amino acid means silent, a different amino acid means missense, and landing on a stop codon means nonsense. The trigger to watch for is a new UAA, UAG, or UGA, which is always nonsense. This is the same procedure you used on the three opening cases; the only new twist is a change in the wobble (third) base staying silent, which follows from the degeneracy of the code in Chapter 19: The Genetic Code.

P2. For each change to a gene, decide whether it causes a frameshift, and say what happens to the protein downstream: (a) a deletion of 2 bases; (b) an insertion of 6 bases; (c) a deletion of 3 bases.

Answer

The calculation: test whether the number of bases is a multiple of 3. (a) 2 is not a multiple of 3, so yes, a frameshift: every codon after the deletion is re-grouped and garbled. (b) 6 is a multiple of 3 (\(6 = 3 \times 2\)), so no frameshift: this is an in-frame insertion that adds 2 whole amino acids, and the downstream codons stay lined up. (c) 3 is a multiple of 3, so no frameshift: an in-frame deletion that removes 1 whole amino acid, with the downstream codons preserved.

Why it works: the ribosome reads the mRNA in fixed, non-overlapping groups of three starting at AUG. Removing or adding a number of bases that is a multiple of 3 keeps every later base in its original triplet, so only whole codons are gained or lost. Removing or adding any other number pushes every later base into a new triplet, which changes every downstream codon at once. That is why the multiple-of-3 test alone decides frameshift or not.

Pattern (the mod-3 test): for any insertion or deletion, ask whether its size is a multiple of 3. Not a multiple of 3 means a frameshift, which garbles all downstream codons and is usually severe. A multiple of 3 means an in-frame indel, which still changes the protein by adding or removing whole amino acids but leaves the reading frame intact. Note the contrast with a substitution, which never changes the base count and so can never frameshift.

P3. Explain why sickle cell anemia is called a point mutation, and specifically a missense mutation, rather than a frameshift.

AnswerIt is a single-base substitution: the DNA coding strand changes GAG to GTG (in the mRNA, GAG to GUG). That change alters one codon, which changes one amino acid from glutamic acid (Glu) to valine (Val). Because one base is swapped for another and none is added or removed, the total base count is unchanged, so the reading frame stays lined up and the downstream codons are unaffected. A single-base substitution is a point mutation, and because it swaps one amino acid for a different one it is a missense mutation. A frameshift is impossible here because a frameshift requires an insertion or deletion whose size is not a multiple of 3.

Watch / read preview before showing students

↗ Khan Academy: The different kinds of mutations ↗ Learn.Genetics (University of Utah): What is a mutation? ↗ National Human Genome Research Institute: Mutation (Talking Glossary) ↗ National Human Genome Research Institute: Sickle Cell Disease

Teaching notes

Opener: this chapter is built to be taught examples-first. Write the mRNA 5'-AUG AAA UAU GGU-3' on the board and translate it with the class using the codon key (Met-Lys-Tyr-Gly). Then change one base at a time and ask "did the protein change, and how?" Let their answers surface the three point-mutation outcomes (nothing, one amino acid, cut short) BEFORE you name silent, missense, and nonsense. Show sickle cell as the real missense right after the toy one.

Board moves: keep the codon key visible the whole time. Box the reading frame in groups of three. For the frameshift, physically insert one letter after the AUG and re-box the frames so students see every downstream codon shift at once. Then insert three letters and show the frame survives, so the multiple-of-3 rule lands visually, not as a memorized fact.

What to hammer: three rules that the event tests directly. First, a substitution can never cause a frameshift. Second, an indel frameshifts only when its size is not a multiple of 3. Third, sickle cell is a single-base missense mutation, not a deletion or frameshift.

Common stumble: students call a silent mutation "no mutation," and they call every indel a frameshift. Correct both early: silent still changed the DNA, and an in-frame indel is a real indel that is simply not a frameshift. Also check that students keep germline (inherited) separate from somatic (not inherited, e.g. many cancers).

What to name: mutation, allele, point mutation, substitution, silent, missense, nonsense, degeneracy, indel, insertion, deletion, reading frame, frameshift, in-frame, addition, translocation, reciprocal translocation, mutagen, germline, somatic. Name CRISPR as a landmark only.

Pacing: about 14 minutes on the opening cases and Figures 1-3 (the three substitutions, sickle cell, and the frameshift), 8 minutes naming the taxonomy and the multiple-of-3 rule, 8 minutes on chromosome mutations and Figure 4, and 6 minutes on causes plus germline versus somatic. Save the frameshift case until the reading frame from Chapter 19: The Genetic Code is automatic, since that is the step students slip on.

Chromosomal Disorders and Karyotypes

A karyotype is an organized picture of a person's full set of 46 chromosomes. It lets you count the chromosomes and spot when a cell has the wrong number, which is how disorders like Down syndrome and Turner syndrome are diagnosed.

🧬 How to read this chapter: this is a chromosome-level chapter, so the examples use real karyotypes, chromosome counts, and named disorders, not the Rivera family. It takes the mistake called nondisjunction from Chapter 4: Meiosis and shows the whole-chromosome disorders it produces, seen through a karyotype.
Prerequisites: Chapter 4: Meiosis (how chromosomes separate, and nondisjunction when they fail) · Chapter 20: Mutations (mutation types, including whole-chromosome changes).
This chapter: what a karyotype is and how to read it, the notation for chromosome counts, and how nondisjunction produces trisomy and monosomy (aneuploidy), plus the structural changes a karyotype can reveal.
Unlocks: Chapter 22: Pedigrees (tracking inherited traits through a family tree) · Chapter 23: Genetic Disorders Casebook (the disorders list and their inheritance patterns).

Three karyotypes, counted first

Before any theory, look at three real chromosome pictures and just count. Each one is a karyotype: a photograph of a single cell's chromosomes, cut out and lined up in numbered pairs. A typical human cell holds 46 chromosomes. Watch what happens when the count comes out to something other than 46.

Case 1: a boy with Down syndrome (47,XY,+21)

Figure 1 is the karyotype of a boy with Down syndrome. Count down the numbered pairs. Every pair from 1 to 22 holds exactly two chromosomes, except pair 21, which holds three. The sex position holds one X and one Y. Add it up: the normal set is 46, and there is one extra chromosome at pair 21, so the total is \(46 + 1 = 47\).

So the count is 47, the odd pair is number 21, and the sex is male. Where did the extra chromosome 21 come from? It came from an error in meiosis, the cell division that makes eggs and sperm (see Chapter 4: Meiosis). One pair of chromosome 21 failed to pull apart, so a gamete carried two copies of chromosome 21 instead of one. When that gamete met a normal one at fertilization, the new cell had three copies. The disorder is Down syndrome, and the standard code for it is 47,XY,+21: 47 chromosomes total, sex chromosomes XY, one extra chromosome 21.

Case 2: two sex-chromosome cases (47,XXY and 45,X)

The same kind of miscount can hit the sex chromosomes instead of a numbered pair. Figure 2 shows the sex-chromosome position for three people. Ignore the 44 numbered chromosomes (which are normal in all three) and just count the sex chromosomes.

  • 47,XXY (Klinefelter syndrome): the sex position holds three chromosomes, X, X, and Y. Add them to the 44 normal autosomes and the total is \(44 + 3 = 47\). A Y is present, so this individual is assigned male. The extra X was carried in by a gamete that got both sex chromosomes because of nondisjunction.
  • 45,X (Turner syndrome): the sex position holds a single X with no partner. Add it to the 44 autosomes and the total is \(44 + 1 = 45\). There is no Y, so this individual is assigned female. Here nondisjunction produced a gamete that carried no sex chromosome at all.

The pattern: nondisjunction makes the wrong number

All three cases tell the same story. During meiosis, one chromosome pair failed to separate. That failure is called nondisjunction. It sends both members of a pair into one gamete and leaves the partner gamete short. When such a gamete joins a normal one at fertilization, the new cell ends up with 47 chromosomes (one too many) or 45 (one too few), instead of the normal 46. A karyotype makes the miscount visible by laying every chromosome out to be counted. The rest of the chapter names this pattern precisely, gives the notation, and shows the mechanism.

The concepts

A human body cell normally holds 46 chromosomes. These come as 23 pairs: 22 pairs of autosomes (the numbered chromosomes 1 through 22, which are the same in both sexes) and 1 pair of sex chromosomes (the pair that helps set biological sex, XX or XY). An autosome is any chromosome that is not a sex chromosome. You met chromosomes and this diploid count in Chapter 2: Chromosomes.

What a karyotype is, and how one is made

A karyotype is an organized picture of all of a cell's chromosomes, sorted by size and lined up in their numbered pairs. To make one, a technician stops many cells in the middle of cell division, the moment when chromosomes are condensed and visible, then stains them and photographs them under a light microscope. The chromosomes are then arranged into the standard chart: pair 1 (largest) through pair 22 (smallest), with the sex chromosomes placed last.

Because every chromosome is laid out and counted, a karyotype makes two things easy to see:

  • The wrong number of chromosomes. If a pair has three chromosomes instead of two, or one instead of two, the karyotype shows it directly.
  • Large rearrangements. A missing piece, an added piece, or a swap of segments between chromosomes (a translocation, from Chapter 20: Mutations) can be visible as an odd chromosome shape.

A karyotype does not show single-base mutations. A point mutation changes one letter of DNA, which is far too small to see under a microscope. So a karyotype catches whole-chromosome problems, not gene-sized ones.

Aneuploidy: trisomy and monosomy

Where does an extra or missing chromosome come from? From nondisjunction, the error you met in Chapter 4: Meiosis: chromosomes fail to separate during meiosis. Normally the two members of a pair (the homologous chromosomes) separate in anaphase I, and the sister chromatids separate in anaphase II, so each gamete (each sperm or egg) ends up with exactly one copy of every chromosome. When separation fails, one gamete receives an extra copy and the other receives none. Nondisjunction in meiosis I sends a whole homologous pair (two different chromosomes) into one gamete; nondisjunction in meiosis II sends two identical sister chromatids into one gamete.

Using \(n\) for the normal haploid gamete count (in humans \(n = 23\)), nondisjunction produces a gamete with \(n+1\) chromosomes (24, one too many) and a gamete with \(n-1\) chromosomes (22, one too few). When such a gamete joins a normal gamete at fertilization, the zygote has the wrong chromosome number. This condition is called aneuploidy, meaning a chromosome count that is not an exact multiple of the normal haploid set. The two kinds are:

  • Polysomy / trisomy: three copies of one chromosome instead of two (from an \(n+1\) gamete plus a normal one, giving \(2+1=3\)). Polysomy means more than two copies of a chromosome; the most common form is trisomy, meaning exactly three. Examples: Down syndrome, trisomy 21, written 47,XY,+21 or 47,XX,+21; Edwards syndrome, trisomy 18 (+18); Patau syndrome, trisomy 13 (+13).
  • Monosomy: only one copy of a chromosome instead of two (from an \(n-1\) gamete plus a normal one, giving \(0+1=1\)). The only monosomy a human survives to birth with is Turner syndrome, 45,X: a single X and no second sex chromosome. An individual with Turner syndrome is assigned female.

Karyotype notation

Chromosome makeup is written in a short standard code, in this order:

  1. The total number of chromosomes (normally 46).
  2. A comma, then the sex chromosomes (XX or XY).
  3. Any extra or missing chromosome, written as a plus or minus sign and the chromosome number. A plus sign means an extra copy. A minus sign means a missing copy.

So a typical female is 46,XX and a typical male is 46,XY. A male with an extra chromosome 21 is 47,XY,+21: 47 chromosomes total, sex chromosomes XY, one extra chromosome 21. Read the code left to right and the count, the sex, and the abnormality are all stated in order. A sex-chromosome aneuploidy is shown just by the sex-chromosome field: Klinefelter is 47,XXY and Turner is 45,X, with no separate plus or minus needed.

Structural changes: deletion, duplication, inversion, translocation

Aneuploidy is a change in chromosome number. A karyotype can also reveal a change in chromosome structure, where the count stays at 46 but a chromosome's content is rearranged. A large enough structural change shows up as an oddly shaped or oddly sized chromosome. There are four standard types (Figure 4):

  • Deletion: a segment of a chromosome breaks off and is lost, so the genes on that segment are missing. Large deletions can be serious. One example is Cri-du-chat syndrome, from a deletion on chromosome 5.
  • Duplication: a segment is copied so a stretch of genes appears twice on the same chromosome.
  • Inversion: a segment breaks out, flips end for end, and reattaches, so its genes now run in reverse order.
  • Translocation: a segment moves to a different, non-homologous chromosome. In a reciprocal translocation two chromosomes swap segments (from Chapter 20: Mutations).

Sex chromosomes and how sex is set

The sex chromosomes need their own rules, and the exam tests them directly.

  • Any Y means assigned male. The Y chromosome carries the switch that drives male development. So XY is male, and any karyotype that contains at least one Y is assigned male, no matter how many X chromosomes are also present.
  • XX with no Y means assigned female.
  • The father's sperm sets the sex. Every egg from the mother carries an X. The father's sperm carries either an X or a Y, about half and half. So it is which sperm fertilizes the egg that decides whether the child is XX (female) or XY (male).

Nondisjunction of the sex chromosomes gives sex-chromosome aneuploidies:

  • Klinefelter syndrome, 47,XXY: two X chromosomes and one Y. Because a Y is present, the individual is assigned male.
  • Turner syndrome, 45,X: a single X and no partner. Assigned female. This is the one viable human monosomy.
  • Triple X, 47,XXX: three X chromosomes and no Y. Assigned female.

Worked examples

Example 1 (read a karyotype and write its notation). A karyotype is prepared. Every numbered pair from 1 to 22 has exactly two chromosomes, except pair 21, which has three. The sex chromosomes are one X and one Y. Count the chromosomes, name the disorder, and write the notation.

Count: the 22 autosome pairs would normally give \(22\times 2 = 44\) chromosomes, and the sex pair adds 2, for the normal 46. Here pair 21 has one extra chromosome, so the total is \(46 + 1 = 47\). See Figure 1.

Name: three copies of chromosome 21 is trisomy 21, which is Down syndrome. One X and one Y means the individual is assigned male.

Notation: total first (47), then the sex chromosomes (XY), then the abnormality as a plus sign and the chromosome number (+21). The answer is 47,XY,+21.

Aha: read a karyotype in three moves. Count the total, check the sex chromosomes for a Y, then find the pair that is not a clean pair of two. Write those three facts in that order and the notation writes itself.

Example 2 (trace nondisjunction to a gamete, then to a zygote). During egg formation in the mother, chromosome 21 fails to separate in meiosis I. Follow one such egg through fertilization by a normal sperm. What are the possible zygotes, and which disorder can result?

Normally meiosis gives each egg exactly one copy of chromosome 21 (a haploid \(n = 23\) egg). When chromosome 21 fails to separate, both copies go into one egg and none into the other:

  • One egg gets two copies of chromosome 21, so it has \(n+1 = 24\) chromosomes.
  • The other egg gets zero copies of chromosome 21, so it has \(n-1 = 22\) chromosomes.

A normal sperm always brings one copy of chromosome 21. Fertilization adds the two counts (see Figure 3):

  • \(n+1\) egg (2 copies) plus normal sperm (1 copy) gives \(2+1 = 3\) copies: a trisomy 21 zygote with 47 chromosomes. This is Down syndrome, 47,XX,+21 or 47,XY,+21 depending on the sperm's sex chromosome.
  • \(n-1\) egg (0 copies) plus normal sperm (1 copy) gives \(0+1 = 1\) copy: a monosomy 21 zygote with 45 chromosomes, which is not viable and does not survive to birth.
Aha: nondisjunction always makes gametes in a matched pair, one with too many and one with too few. The extra chromosome is not created at fertilization. It is carried in by one aneuploid gamete, and the normal partner just supplies its usual single copy.

Example 3 (determine sex from a karyotype). Three karyotypes read 46,XX; 47,XXY; and 45,X. For each, state the assigned sex and, where there is one, name the disorder.

The rule is simple and absolute: any Y means assigned male; no Y (only X chromosomes) means assigned female. The number of X chromosomes does not override a Y. See Figure 2.

  • 46,XX: two X chromosomes, no Y, so assigned female. Normal count, no disorder.
  • 47,XXY: there is a Y, so assigned male, even though two X chromosomes are present. This is Klinefelter syndrome.
  • 45,X: a single X and no Y, so assigned female. This is Turner syndrome, the only viable human monosomy.
Aha: to read sex, do not count the X chromosomes. Just ask one question: is there a Y? If yes, male; if no, female. The father's sperm decided this by delivering an X or a Y.
Common pitfalls:
  • Reading sex by counting X chromosomes. Sex is set by the presence of a Y, not by how many X chromosomes there are. 47,XXY has two X chromosomes but is assigned male, because it has a Y.
  • Getting the notation order wrong. The order is total count, then sex chromosomes, then the extra or missing chromosome. Write 47,XY,+21, not 21+,XY,47 or XY,47,+21.
  • Confusing polysomy with polyploidy. Trisomy 21 is an extra copy of one chromosome (aneuploidy). It is not a full extra set of all chromosomes (that is polyploidy, for example triploidy 69,XXX, which is different and not covered here).
  • Confusing a number change with a structure change. Aneuploidy (trisomy, monosomy) is the wrong number of chromosomes. A deletion, duplication, inversion, or translocation is a rearrangement of a chromosome's content, so the count usually stays 46. Both can show on a karyotype, but they are different problems.
  • Thinking fertilization adds the extra chromosome. The extra or missing chromosome comes from nondisjunction during meiosis, before fertilization. The normal gamete just contributes its usual single copy.
  • Calling a karyotype a gene test. A karyotype shows whole chromosomes, so it catches trisomy, monosomy, and large rearrangements. It cannot show a single-base point mutation, which is far too small to see.
  • Mislabeling Turner or the viable monosomy. Turner syndrome is 45,X (one X, assigned female) and is the only monosomy a human survives to birth with. Do not write it as 45,Y or as a trisomy.

Connections

  • → Chapter 4: Meiosis: nondisjunction is the meiosis error where homologs fail to separate in anaphase I, or sister chromatids fail to separate in anaphase II. That failure is the direct cause of every trisomy and monosomy in this chapter.
  • → Chapter 2: Chromosomes: the normal count of 46 chromosomes, the 22 autosome pairs plus the sex pair, and what it means to be diploid. A karyotype is that set made visible.
  • → Chapter 20: Mutations: whole-chromosome changes (deletion, duplication, inversion, translocation) are chromosomal mutations. Aneuploidy is a change in chromosome number; the four structural changes are changes in chromosome structure. Both can appear on a karyotype, unlike point mutations.
  • → Chapter 22: Pedigrees: a karyotype describes one individual's chromosomes; a pedigree tracks a trait across a family. Together they diagnose and trace inherited conditions.
  • → Chapter 23: Genetic Disorders Casebook: the chromosomal disorders here (Down, Edwards, Patau, Turner, Klinefelter) sit alongside the gene-level disorders in the full casebook, each with its own inheritance pattern.
  • ↔ Cell biology: a karyotype must be made from cells caught in the middle of division, when chromosomes are condensed and countable, which ties directly back to the division stages in Chapters 3 and 4.

★ Landmark it points toward

Prenatal genetic testing (amniocentesis): a procedure that samples cells from the fluid around a developing fetus so a karyotype can be prepared before birth, which can detect trisomies such as Down syndrome. Newer methods read fetal DNA from a sample of the mother's blood. (Meet the names for now; the details are beyond this event.)

Practice

P1 (read a karyotype, give the notation). A karyotype shows two chromosomes in every numbered pair except pair 18, which has three. The sex chromosomes are two X chromosomes. Give the total count, the assigned sex, the disorder, and the notation.

Answer

The calculation: 22 autosome pairs give \(22\times 2 = 44\); the two X chromosomes add 2, for 46; the extra chromosome 18 makes \(46 + 1 = 47\). Two X chromosomes and no Y means assigned female. Three copies of chromosome 18 is trisomy 18, which is Edwards syndrome. Notation: 47,XX,+18.

Why it works: reading a karyotype is a fixed three-step routine. First total the chromosomes by counting each bar, which is fastest as 44 for the normal autosomes plus the sex chromosomes plus any extras. Second, look only at the sex pair and ask whether a Y is present; here it is not, so the individual is assigned female. Third, find the pair that is not a clean two and record it with a plus or minus sign. The three findings map straight onto the three parts of the notation, in that order.

Pattern (three-step karyotype read, written as count, sex, abnormality): use this same routine for every karyotype. Count the total, check for a Y to set the sex, then name the odd pair. Contrast the abnormalities you might find: a pair of three is a trisomy and gets a plus sign (for example +18 or +21), while a lone chromosome is a monosomy and gets a minus sign or is shown by a shortened sex code (for example 45,X). The number after the plus or minus sign is always the chromosome that is wrong, not the total.

P2 (trace nondisjunction to gamete and zygote). In sperm formation, the sex chromosomes fail to separate in meiosis I, so one sperm carries both an X and a Y. That sperm fertilizes a normal egg. What is the zygote's sex-chromosome makeup, what is its total chromosome count, and what disorder results?

Answer

The calculation: the abnormal sperm carries both sex chromosomes, X and Y, so it has \(n+1 = 24\) chromosomes. A normal egg always carries a single X, with \(n = 23\) chromosomes. Fertilization combines them: the sex chromosomes become X (from the egg) plus X and Y (from the sperm), which is XXY, and the total is \(24 + 23 = 47\). Because a Y is present, the individual is assigned male. The result is 47,XXY, Klinefelter syndrome.

Why it works: nondisjunction sends both members of a pair into one gamete. Here the sperm keeps both the X and the Y instead of one or the other, so it delivers two sex chromosomes rather than one. The egg still brings its normal single X. Adding the egg's X to the sperm's X and Y gives three sex chromosomes, XXY. The presence of even one Y drives male development, so the individual is assigned male despite having two X chromosomes. Trace the copies, do not guess the label.

Pattern (nondisjunction, add the gamete counts): to find any aneuploid zygote, write each gamete's contribution for the affected chromosome and add. An \(n+1\) gamete carrying an extra chromosome plus a normal \(n\) gamete gives a trisomy (three copies, 47 total); an \(n-1\) gamete missing a chromosome plus a normal one gives a monosomy (one copy, 45 total). This works the same whether the affected chromosome is an autosome (giving 47,+21 or 47,+18) or a sex chromosome (giving 47,XXY or 45,X). Contrast the meiosis I error here, where two different chromosomes (X and Y) end up together, with a meiosis II error, where two identical sister chromatids fail to separate.

P3 (determine sex, drill). State the assigned sex for each karyotype: (a) 47,XYY; (b) 47,XXX; (c) 45,X.

Answer(a) 47,XYY has a Y, so assigned male. (b) 47,XXX has no Y, so assigned female (Triple X). (c) 45,X has no Y, so assigned female (Turner syndrome). The rule: any Y means male; only X chromosomes means female.

Watch / read preview before showing students

↗ National Human Genome Research Institute: Karyotype (Talking Glossary) ↗ Khan Academy: Aneuploidy and nondisjunction ↗ Learn.Genetics (University of Utah): Tour of the Basics ↗ MedlinePlus Genetics: chromosomal conditions

Teaching notes

Opener: show Figure 1 and ask "how many chromosomes here, and is anything off?" Let students count and find pair 21. Then show Figure 2 and have them count just the sex chromosomes for Klinefelter and Turner.
