Only after they have counted three real cases do you name the general idea: this counting is exactly what a karyotype is for, and each odd count is a real, diagnosable disorder that traces back to nondisjunction.

Board moves: write the notation slots as three blanks: total, then sex chromosomes, then plus or minus and number. Fill them in live for 47,XY,+21. Then draw the nondisjunction split from Figure 3, pausing to write \(n+1 = 24\) and \(n-1 = 22\) on the two gametes, and add the sperm's single copy to each to get 47 and 45. Keep the meiosis picture from Chapter 4 on the board beside it so the link is visible. Finish with Figure 4 to contrast a number change (aneuploidy) with a structure change (deletion, duplication, inversion, translocation).

What to name: karyotype, autosome, sex chromosome, aneuploidy, nondisjunction, polysomy, trisomy, monosomy, the four structural changes (deletion, duplication, inversion, translocation), and the named disorders (Down 47,+21; Edwards +18; Patau +13; Turner 45,X; Klinefelter 47,XXY). Name amniocentesis and prenatal testing only as landmarks.

Check for understanding: give 47,XX,+18 and ask for the disorder and sex (Edwards, female). Give 45,X and ask the same (Turner, female). Ask "which sperm sets the sex of the child?" (the one that fertilizes the egg, since it carries either an X or a Y). Ask "can a karyotype show sickle cell?" (no, that is a point mutation, too small to see). Ask "does a translocation change the chromosome count?" (usually no; it rearranges content while the count stays 46).

Pacing: about 10 minutes counting the three opening karyotypes (Figures 1 and 2) and naming the pattern, 10 minutes on what a karyotype is and the notation, 15 minutes tracing nondisjunction to trisomy and monosomy with Figure 3, and 10 minutes on structural changes and the sex-chromosome rule. Save the sex-chromosome nondisjunction problem (P2) for after the autosome case in Example 2 is solid.

Pedigrees

A pedigree is a family tree for one trait, drawn with standard symbols. From the pattern of who is affected you deduce the mode of inheritance, work out each person's genotype, and compute the probability that the next child is affected or a carrier.

Prerequisites: Chapter 8: Probability in Genetics (the product and sum rules; each child is an independent event) · Chapter 11: Multiple Alleles and Blood Types (reading a genotype back from a phenotype; the exclusion rule) · Chapter 12: Sex-Linked Traits (X-linked recessive; no father-to-son transmission of an X allele).
This chapter: pedigrees and pedigree analysis (deduce the mode, assign genotypes, predict the next child).
Unlocks: Chapter 23: Genetic Disorders and the Frontier (reading real disorder families).
🧬 Running example: the Rivera family, tracked for red-green color blindness, a trait that makes certain reds and greens hard to tell apart. It is X-linked recessive, with the normal-vision allele written \(X^C\) and the color-blind allele \(X^c\). We keep the genotypes from Chapter 12: the grandfather Miguel is color-blind (\(X^c Y\)), his daughter Elena is an unaffected carrier (\(X^C X^c\)), her husband David has normal vision (\(X^C Y\)), and among their children Mateo is color-blind (\(X^c Y\)) while Lucia and Sofia are unaffected. We also study the real Queen Victoria royal hemophilia pedigree.

Start by reading two real family trees

Before any rules, look at two actual pedigrees and deduce everything from the picture. A pedigree is a diagram of one family across several generations that follows a single trait. The skill is reading it backward: you start from who is affected and work out the rule of inheritance and each person's hidden genotype. You only need four symbols to begin. A square is a male and a circle is a female. A filled (shaded) shape is affected, meaning that person shows the trait, and an open (empty) shape is unaffected. A shape with a small center dot (or half shading) is a carrier, an unaffected person who quietly carries one copy of the recessive allele. A horizontal line joins a couple, and a vertical line drops to their children.

Case 1 (deduce the mode and the genotypes). In the Rivera pedigree below, the grandfather Miguel (I-1) is color-blind and his grandson Mateo (III-1) is color-blind, but no one in between shows the trait. From the picture alone, what is the most likely inheritance mode, and what is each person's genotype?

Write the normal-vision allele as \(X^C\) and the color-blind allele as \(X^c\).

Step 1, find the mode from the picture. The two affected people, Miguel and Mateo, are both male, and the trait skips the middle generation, since Elena is unaffected. Look at Mateo's parents: Elena and David are both unaffected, yet their son Mateo is affected. That single fact is decisive. When two unaffected parents have an affected child, the allele for the trait was present but hidden in both parents, so the trait must be recessive. A dominant trait would have to show in at least one parent. Next, both affected people are male, and the allele reached Mateo through his mother Elena, not his father. The trait is also not carried on the Y chromosome: Mateo is an affected son whose father David is unaffected, and a Y-linked trait would pass from an affected father to every son. A trait that shows mostly in males, passes from a carrier mother to a son, and never from father to son is the fingerprint of an X-linked recessive trait, one carried on the X chromosome. Chapter 12 confirms the color-blindness gene is on the X.

Strictly, this small tree does not by itself completely exclude autosomal recessive: David could be an unseen carrier, which would let an autosomal recessive allele reach Mateo. The all-male affected pattern is what makes X-linked recessive the most likely reading, and Chapter 12 confirms the gene is on the X.

Step 2, deduce the genotypes. Once you know the mode, each genotype follows from who is affected and who their parents are.

  • Miguel is color-blind, so \(X^c Y\).
  • Elena is unaffected, so she carries at least one \(X^C\). Her father Miguel could give her only his single X, which is \(X^c\). Therefore Elena must be \(X^C X^c\). She is an obligate carrier, meaning her genotype is forced by the pedigree even though she does not show the trait.
  • David is an unaffected male, so \(X^C Y\).
  • Mateo is color-blind, so \(X^c Y\). His \(X^c\) came from Elena (his only X is from his mother) and his Y came from David.
  • Rosa is unaffected, so she has at least one \(X^C\). Nothing forces a second allele, so we record her as \(X^C X^{?}\), most simply \(X^C X^C\).
  • Lucia and Sofia each received an \(X^C\) from David for certain, so neither can be affected. From Elena each received \(X^C\) or \(X^c\) with equal chance, so each daughter is \(X^C X^C\) or \(X^C X^c\), a fifty-fifty split.
Aha: Elena is an obligate carrier because her father was affected. An affected father hands every daughter his one X, and here that X carries \(X^c\). So every daughter of a color-blind man is a carrier. That is why an X-linked recessive trait so often reappears in a grandson after skipping the mother in between.

Case 2 (a real pedigree). The most famous pedigree in history is the family of Queen Victoria and hemophilia, a real and well documented case. Hemophilia is a bleeding disorder in which the blood clots slowly, so minor injuries can be dangerous. From Victoria's family tree, how do we know hemophilia is X-linked recessive, and why did some royal branches stay free of it?

Write the normal allele as \(X^H\) and the hemophilia allele as \(X^h\). Historians call this "the royal disease" because it spread from Victoria into the German, Russian, and Spanish royal families through her daughters.

Reading the tree. Every affected person is male: Leopold and Beatrice's son. Victoria herself was unaffected, yet she had an affected son, so she must have been a carrier (\(X^H X^h\)). Leopold received his one X from Victoria, so he inherited her \(X^h\) and is \(X^h Y\). The same pattern repeats one generation down: Beatrice is a carrier, and her son received her \(X^h\) and has hemophilia. Mostly-male affected individuals, plus transmission from carrier mother to son, is the X-linked recessive fingerprint. Because hemophilia is a documented X-linked recessive disorder, this reading is confirmed by science and not just inferred from one small tree.

Why some branches stayed clear. Edward VII was unaffected, so he is \(X^H Y\). Since a father gives his sons only a Y, Edward could not pass the hemophilia allele to any son. His line, which includes today's British royal family, is free of the disease. Meanwhile Victoria's carrier daughters carried it onward: Alice's daughter Alexandra was a carrier, and her son Alexei, the Russian heir, had hemophilia, and Beatrice's line carried it into the Spanish royal family. The same allele vanished from one branch and reappeared in others, exactly as the no-father-to-son rule predicts.

Aha: the split between the clear British line and the affected Russian and Spanish lines is not luck. A father cannot pass an X-linked allele to his sons, so an unaffected king ends the trait in his own male line, while a carrier daughter can quietly carry it into the next family she marries into.

Find the pattern: three inheritance fingerprints

Both trees told the same story through the same clues: who is affected, whether the trait skips a generation, whether it favors one sex, and which parent passed it on. Step back and name those clues, because almost every trait tested is inherited in one of three modes, and each leaves a recognizable fingerprint. Autosomal means the gene sits on one of the 22 non-sex chromosome pairs, so its inheritance does not depend on sex.

  • Autosomal recessive. The trait skips generations. Two unaffected parents can have an affected child, because both parents are heterozygous carriers. It affects males and females about equally. Consanguinity (related parents) makes it more likely.
  • Autosomal dominant. The trait appears in every generation and does not skip. Every affected child has at least one affected parent (this is called vertical transmission). It affects both sexes about equally, and it can pass from father to son.
  • X-linked recessive. The trait shows up mostly in males, because a male is hemizygous (he has one X, so a single recessive allele is enough to affect him). It passes from a carrier mother to a son. There is no father-to-son transmission of the allele, because a father gives his sons a Y, not his X. An affected daughter is possible only when her father is affected AND her mother is a carrier or affected.
Two fast eliminators. You can often rule a mode out at a glance, exactly as we did in the two cases. (1) If two unaffected parents have an affected child, the trait cannot be dominant, so it is recessive (this is what settled the Rivera tree). (2) If an affected father has an affected son, the trait cannot be X-linked, because a father passes a Y (not his X) to a son. Apply these two tests first, then confirm with the full checklist.
Honest limit of a small tree. A pattern where every affected person is male strongly suggests X-linked recessive, but a small pedigree often cannot fully exclude autosomal recessive, because an unseen parent could be a hidden carrier. As the event wiki puts it for its worked example, the trait is most likely X-linked recessive, yet autosomal recessive is not ruled out without more data. State the most likely mode and name the ambiguity when it exists.

The systematic method (and why it works)

Reading a pedigree well is a fixed four-step routine. (1) Mark who is affected and note their sex. (2) Apply the two fast eliminators to narrow the mode, then confirm with the three-fingerprint checklist. (3) Assign genotypes, starting from the people the tree forces (every affected person, then every obligate carrier), and fill the rest as far as the tree allows. (4) For a future child, treat the one relevant couple as a single cross and read the odds off a Punnett square. The method works because a pedigree is just many small crosses stacked into one picture, and each affected or obligate individual pins down alleles that must be present, which then constrain their relatives.

The full set of symbols

You have already met the four core symbols. Here is the complete set. A few conventions are shared by essentially every pedigree, so learn them once. Some other details vary between sources, and the chapter notes where.

  • A square is a male. A circle is a female. (An individual of unknown or unspecified sex is drawn as a diamond.)
  • A shaded (fully filled) shape is affected: that person shows the trait. An open (empty) shape is unaffected.
  • A carrier is an unaffected heterozygote: one recessive allele, hidden behind a dominant one, so no trait shows. Carriers are often drawn half-shaded or with a small center dot. This convention is not universal. As the event wiki notes, some pedigrees mark carriers and some do not, so never assume an open shape is a non-carrier unless the tree forces it.
  • A horizontal line joining a square and a circle is a mating line (a couple who have children). A double horizontal line marks a consanguineous mating, meaning the partners are relatives, which raises the chance both carry the same rare recessive allele.
  • A vertical line drops from the couple to a sibship line, the horizontal bar that ties brothers and sisters together. Each child hangs from the sibship line.
  • Generations are labeled with Roman numerals (I is the oldest, then II, III), and individuals within a generation are numbered left to right (I-1, I-2, and so on), so any person can be named precisely, for example II-3.
  • The proband (or index case) is the individual, often marked with an arrow, who first brought the family to medical attention. Analysis usually starts there.

Once you know the mode and the parents' genotypes, the next-child step is Chapter 8 again. Draw the Punnett square for that one couple and read off the chance the next child is affected or a carrier. Each child is an independent event, so a family already having an affected child does not change the odds for the next one. The worked example below runs the full step 4 for the Rivera couple.

Worked example: the odds for the next child

Example (compute the next-child probability). Elena (\(X^C X^c\), a carrier) and David (\(X^C Y\), unaffected) are considering another child. What is the probability that this next child is color-blind? What is the probability the child is an unaffected carrier?

Step 1, list the gametes. Elena is \(X^C X^c\), so her eggs carry \(X^C\) or \(X^c\). David is \(X^C Y\), so his sperm carry \(X^C\) or \(Y\). The column that inherits David's X gives daughters; the column that inherits his Y gives sons.

Step 2, read the four cells. Each of the four cells is equally likely, so each has probability \(\tfrac{1}{4}\).

  • \(X^C X^C\): normal daughter, \(\tfrac{1}{4}\).
  • \(X^C Y\): normal son, \(\tfrac{1}{4}\).
  • \(X^C X^c\): carrier daughter, \(\tfrac{1}{4}\).
  • \(X^c Y\): color-blind son, \(\tfrac{1}{4}\).

So the probability the next child is color-blind is \(\tfrac{1}{4}\) (only the \(X^c Y\) son), and the probability the next child is an unaffected carrier is \(\tfrac{1}{4}\) (only the \(X^C X^c\) daughter). If instead you condition on sex first, as Chapter 8 suggests, the numbers sharpen: among sons, half are color-blind, so \(P(\text{color-blind}\mid\text{son})=\tfrac{1}{2}\); among daughters, none are affected but half are carriers, so \(P(\text{carrier}\mid\text{daughter})=\tfrac{1}{2}\). Multiplying by \(P(\text{son})=\tfrac{1}{2}\) recovers the \(\tfrac{1}{4}\) overall.

Aha: the fact that Mateo is already color-blind does not change these odds. Each child is an independent draw from the same four cells. Believing "we already have one affected child, so the next is safe" is the gambler's fallacy, and inheritance does not work that way.
Common pitfalls:
  • Drawing a carrier as affected. A carrier is an unaffected heterozygote. Use a center dot or half shading, never a fully shaded shape. Marking a carrier as affected breaks the whole analysis.
  • Reading an open shape as "no allele." An open symbol only means unaffected. That person may be a hidden carrier unless the pedigree forces the genotype (an obligate carrier). Remember that many pedigrees do not mark carriers at all.
  • Jumping to X-linked just because a male is affected. Autosomal traits affect males too. You need the male-heavy pattern PLUS carrier-mother-to-son transmission PLUS no father-to-son before you call it X-linked recessive.
  • Forgetting the father-to-son test. One clear father-to-son transmission of the trait rules out X-linked inheritance on the spot, because a father passes a Y to his sons.
  • Confusing "dominant" with "common." A trait appearing in every generation signals dominant inheritance, but that says nothing about how frequent the allele is in a population. A recessive allele can be far more common than a dominant one.
  • The gambler's fallacy. Prior affected children do not lower or raise the odds for the next child. Each birth is an independent event.

Connections

  • ← Chapter 8: Probability in Genetics: once you deduce the parents' genotypes from the tree, the next-child odds are a Punnett square plus the product and sum rules, with each child independent. Conditioning on sex first is the cleanest way to handle X-linked questions.
  • ← Chapter 11: Multiple Alleles and Blood Types: the exclusion rule (a type O child needs an \(i\) from each parent) is pedigree reasoning in miniature. Ruling a parent in or out from a child's phenotype is the same backward logic applied across a whole family tree.
  • ← Chapter 12: Sex-Linked Traits: this chapter applies the X-linked recessive rules directly, including no father-to-son transmission, to read both the Rivera color-blindness tree and the Queen Victoria hemophilia tree.
  • ↔ Chapter 7: Punnett Squares and Monohybrid Crosses: the square in the worked example is the same tool, now with an X and a Y on one edge. A test cross reveals a hidden genotype by breeding; a pedigree does the same job for humans, whom you cannot breed on purpose.
  • → Chapter 23: Genetic Disorders and the Frontier: pedigrees are how you read real disorder families. Chapter 23 uses them for cystic fibrosis and sickle cell (autosomal recessive), Huntington's disease (autosomal dominant), and hemophilia and color blindness (X-linked recessive).

★ Landmark it points toward

Genetic counseling. A genetic counselor takes a real family history, draws the pedigree, deduces the mode of inheritance, and gives a couple the probability that a future child is affected or a carrier, exactly the reasoning in this chapter done with clinical data and often refined by DNA testing and Bayesian probability. Named here, not taught.

Practice

P1 (deduce the mode). A family is tracked for cystic fibrosis. In Generation I, two unaffected parents have three children in Generation II: an affected daughter, an unaffected son, and an unaffected daughter. The affected daughter's own father (in Generation I) is unaffected. Both affected and unaffected members include males and females across the tree. What is the inheritance mode?

AnswerAutosomal recessive.

Why it works: two unaffected parents produced an affected child, so by fast eliminator 1 the trait cannot be dominant; it must be recessive (both parents are heterozygous carriers). Now decide autosomal versus X-linked recessive. The affected individual is a female whose father is unaffected. For an X-linked recessive trait, an affected daughter needs a recessive X from each parent, which requires an affected father. Since her father is unaffected, X-linked recessive is impossible, so the gene is autosomal. That the trait affects both sexes and skips a generation confirms autosomal recessive.

Pattern (the three-mode checklist plus the affected-daughter test): run three quick tests. Skips generations and comes from unaffected parents means recessive; appears every generation with an affected parent behind each affected child means dominant; mostly male with carrier-mother-to-son and no father-to-son means X-linked recessive. When a recessive trait shows an affected female with an unaffected father, that single fact forces autosomal recessive, because an X-linked recessive daughter always requires an affected father. Contrast this with Cases 1 and 2, where all affected individuals were male, which pointed toward X-linked recessive instead.

P2 (compute a next-child probability). A different couple is expecting a child. The mother is a color-blindness carrier (\(X^C X^c\)) and the father is color-blind (\(X^c Y\)). What is the probability their next child is color-blind? What is the probability the child is an unaffected carrier?

Answer\(P(\text{color-blind})=\tfrac{1}{2}\); \(P(\text{unaffected carrier})=\tfrac{1}{4}\).

The calculation: the mother's eggs carry \(X^C\) or \(X^c\); the father's sperm carry \(X^c\) or \(Y\). The four equally likely children are \(X^C X^c\) (carrier daughter), \(X^c X^c\) (affected daughter), \(X^C Y\) (normal son), and \(X^c Y\) (affected son), each with probability \(\tfrac{1}{4}\). Two of the four are affected (one daughter, one son), so \(P(\text{color-blind})=\tfrac{2}{4}=\tfrac{1}{2}\). Only the \(X^C X^c\) daughter is an unaffected carrier, so \(P(\text{carrier})=\tfrac{1}{4}\).

Why it works: the difference from the worked example is the father. There David was \(X^C Y\), so he gave every daughter a normal \(X^C\), which made an affected daughter impossible. Here the father is color-blind (\(X^c Y\)), so he hands every daughter his \(X^c\). A daughter is then affected whenever her mother also contributes an \(X^c\). That is the only route to an affected girl.

Pattern (an affected daughter needs both sides): for an X-linked recessive trait a daughter is affected only if her father is affected AND her mother is a carrier or affected, because a daughter must receive a recessive X from each parent. When the father is unaffected, daughters can be carriers but never affected. Always check the father's status before predicting affected daughters.

P3 (identify carriers). Look again at the Rivera color-blindness pedigree in Case 1. Who is the obligate carrier, what is her genotype, and how do you know for certain?

AnswerElena, genotype \(X^C X^c\). She is certain to be a carrier for two independent reasons: her father Miguel is color-blind (\(X^c Y\)), so she had to receive his \(X^c\); and her son Mateo is color-blind (\(X^c Y\)), so he had to receive an \(X^c\) from her. Either fact alone proves she carries the allele, even though she is unaffected. Lucia and Sofia are only possible carriers (each \(\tfrac{1}{2}\)), not obligate ones.

Watch / read preview before showing learners

↗ Khan Academy: Pedigrees review ▶ Amoeba Sisters: Pedigrees (YouTube) ↗ Learn.Genetics (Utah): reading pedigrees

Teaching notes

Opener: put up the Rivera color-blindness tree from Case 1 with the genotypes hidden and ask two questions: is this trait dominant or recessive, and is the gene on the X? Let the class argue for two minutes before naming any rule. The visible skip from grandfather to grandson is the hook, and the whole point is that they deduce the two answers from the picture before you ever state a rule.

Board move: after the two cases, write the three decision rules as a checklist down one side of the board (autosomal recessive, autosomal dominant, X-linked recessive), then write the two fast eliminators big underneath: an affected child from two unaffected parents means not dominant, and any father-to-son transmission means not X-linked. Students who memorize the two eliminators solve most pedigrees in seconds.

Deduce genotypes out loud: walk the Rivera tree person by person, saying each genotype as you write it, and name "obligate carrier" the moment you reach Elena. Make students explain why an affected father forces every daughter to be a carrier.

Use the real royal tree for the payoff: reveal the Queen Victoria pedigree as the famous case and spend time on Edward VII. Ask why his branch stayed clear while the Russian and Spanish branches did not. The answer, no father-to-son, is the rule they just learned doing real historical work.

Probability with a twist: do the worked example live, then ask "Mateo is already color-blind, so is the next child safer?" Let them fall into the gambler's fallacy, then correct it: each child is an independent draw. Finish with P2 so they see that affected daughters ARE possible once the father is affected. Also stress the honest limit: an all-male pattern points to X-linked recessive but a small tree may not fully exclude autosomal recessive, which is the P1 discrimination skill.

Genetic Disorders and the Frontier

The capstone: take the common human genetic disorders that show up on tests, sort each one by its inheritance pattern, and solve it with a tool you already have, a Punnett square, a pedigree, or a karyotype. Then a short look over the horizon at where genetics is heading.

Prerequisites: Chapter 20: Mutations (a single-base change can cause a disorder, such as sickle-cell anemia) · Chapter 21: Chromosomal Disorders and Karyotypes (an extra or missing chromosome, such as trisomy 21) · Chapter 22: Pedigrees (reading an inheritance pattern from a family tree).
This chapter: classify the common human genetic disorders by their inheritance pattern and work each one with the right tool.
Unlocks: the research frontier described in the ★ Landmark section below (CRISPR and genetic engineering, epigenetics, population genetics, genomics), and the Division C event Designer Genes, which extends this material.
🧬 How this chapter uses the family: most of this chapter is a molecular and clinical casebook of real disorders, so it does not force the Rivera family onto every example. But where a disorder is one the family could carry, we use them. The cystic fibrosis carrier cross below imagines Elena and David each carrying one copy of the recessive allele, and the sickle-cell allele is the exact single-base change from Chapter 20: Mutations.

Start with the casebook: five disorders, five patterns

Before any classification rules, meet five disorders that real families carry. For each one, look at two things: how it passes down (a small family tree or a molecular sketch) and what changed in the DNA. Do not try to memorize the rules yet. The pattern that sorts all five will appear on its own. Standard pedigree symbols are used throughout: a square is male, a circle is female, an open (unfilled) symbol is unaffected, a solid symbol is affected, and a symbol with a central dot is an unaffected carrier.

Case 1 — Cystic fibrosis. Two parents who do not have the disease have a child who does. Cystic fibrosis is the most common life-shortening inherited disease in people of Northern European descent.

What changed: the CFTR gene on chromosome 7, which builds a chloride channel in cell membranes. The most common cystic fibrosis mutation, called ΔF508, deletes three DNA bases and removes one amino acid (phenylalanine) from the CFTR protein, so the channel misfolds. What to notice: both parents are healthy, yet the child is affected. A hidden allele surfaced.

Case 2 — Huntington's disease. Here the disease appears in every generation of the family tree and never skips one. It is also late-onset, meaning symptoms usually begin only in middle age, often after the person has already had children.

What changed: the HTT gene on chromosome 4 carries a short DNA triplet, CAG, repeated in a row. In Huntington's the CAG triplet is repeated too many times (a trinucleotide repeat expansion), which builds an over-long, toxic huntingtin protein. What to notice: one copy of the allele is enough to cause the disease, so there are no hidden unaffected carriers.

Case 3 — Sickle-cell anemia. This is the exact single-base change from Chapter 20: Mutations, now seen as an inherited disorder. Like cystic fibrosis it is autosomal recessive, so two healthy carrier parents can have an affected child.

What changed: a single DNA base in the HBB beta-globin gene on chromosome 11. What to notice: the smallest possible genetic change, one base, is enough to cause a whole disease.

Case 4 — Hemophilia. This disorder appears almost only in males. A healthy carrier mother can have affected sons, but an affected father never passes it to his sons.

What changed: the gene for a blood-clotting protein (Factor VIII in the most common form) sits on the X chromosome, and the disease allele is recessive. A male has only one X, so a single copy affects him. A female has two X chromosomes, so she is usually an unaffected carrier. What to notice: the trait tracks the sex of the child and lands mostly on males.

Case 5 — Down syndrome. This one is not a single faulty allele at all. The affected person has an entire extra chromosome.

What changed: there are three copies of chromosome 21 instead of two, written 47,+21 and called trisomy 21. It usually comes from nondisjunction, a chromosome pair failing to separate during meiosis, so one gamete carries an extra chromosome 21. What to notice: the change is a whole chromosome, so you diagnose it by counting chromosomes on a karyotype, not with a cross.

Find the pattern: every one of these five is pinned down by two questions. First, its inheritance mode, meaning how it passes from parents to children. The five split into four modes: autosomal recessive (cystic fibrosis, sickle cell), autosomal dominant (Huntington's), X-linked recessive (hemophilia), and chromosomal (Down syndrome). Second, its molecular level, meaning how large the change is: from a single base (sickle cell), up through a whole gene (cystic fibrosis, Huntington's, hemophilia), to an entire extra chromosome (Down syndrome). Sort any disorder on those two axes and you know both how to predict it and what went wrong. The rest of the chapter makes that classification precise and fixes the right tool for each mode.

The core idea

The five cases above are the whole idea in miniature. A genetic disorder is a disease caused by a change in the genome, either a change in a single gene (a mutation in one gene, as in the first four cases) or a change in whole chromosomes (an extra or missing chromosome, as in Down syndrome). The Science Olympiad event does not usually ask you to memorize the biochemistry of each disease. It asks you to know two things about each one: its inheritance pattern (how it passes from parents to children) and its cause (the genetic change behind it). Once you know the pattern, every disorder becomes a problem you have already solved in an earlier chapter.

There are four patterns to sort them into, each defined plainly:

  • Autosomal recessive. The disease allele sits on an autosome (any chromosome that is not a sex chromosome) and is recessive (its trait appears only when both copies are the disease allele). A person with one disease allele and one working allele is a carrier: they are unaffected (they do not have the disease) but can pass the allele on. Two carriers have a \(\tfrac{1}{4}\) chance of an affected child. Solve it with a Punnett square.
  • Autosomal dominant. The disease allele is on an autosome and is dominant (one copy is enough to cause the disease). An affected parent who carries one copy passes it to about \(\tfrac{1}{2}\) of their children. There are no unaffected carriers of a fully dominant disorder, because one copy already shows.
  • X-linked recessive. The disease allele is a recessive allele on the X chromosome. Because males have only one X, a single disease allele affects a male (he is hemizygous, meaning he has just one copy of an X-linked gene). Females need two copies to be affected, so these disorders appear far more often in males. Solve it with the sex-linked square from Chapter 12: Sex-Linked Traits or a pedigree.
  • Chromosomal. The problem is not a single allele but a whole extra or missing chromosome, usually from nondisjunction (a chromosome pair fails to separate during meiosis). You read these from a karyotype (a picture of all the chromosomes), not from a Punnett square, because they are almost always a one-time meiotic accident rather than an inherited allele.

The table below is the whole tested list of common disorders, sorted into those four patterns. This is the single figure to memorize for the event.

Worked examples

Example 1 (the carrier cross, an autosomal recessive disorder). Cystic fibrosis is autosomal recessive. Imagine Elena and David each carry one copy of the cystic fibrosis allele, so both are carriers (\(Ff\), where \(F\) is the working allele and \(f\) is the recessive disease allele). What is the chance that a child of theirs has cystic fibrosis?

Both parents are heterozygous, so each makes two kinds of gamete in equal amounts: half carry \(F\) and half carry \(f\). This is the same \(Aa \times Aa\) monohybrid cross from Chapter 7. Put David's gametes across the top and Elena's down the side, then fill each cell.

Reading the cells: one \(FF\), two \(Ff\), one \(ff\). The affected genotype is \(ff\), which is one of the four cells, so the chance a child is affected is \(\tfrac{1}{4}\). The chance a child is an unaffected carrier is \(\tfrac{2}{4} = \tfrac{1}{2}\), and the chance of an unaffected non-carrier is \(\tfrac{1}{4}\). This same \(\tfrac{1}{4}\) affected result holds for every autosomal recessive disorder crossed carrier by carrier: cystic fibrosis, sickle-cell anemia, and Tay-Sachs disease all behave this way.

Aha: two unaffected parents produced an affected child. That is the fingerprint of a recessive disorder. Neither Elena nor David has cystic fibrosis, yet \(\tfrac{1}{4}\) of their children can. The recessive allele was hidden in each carrier and only shows when two copies meet.

Example 2 (an autosomal dominant disorder). Huntington's disease is autosomal dominant. A person who carries one copy of the disease allele (\(Hh\), where \(H\) is the dominant disease allele and \(h\) is the normal allele) has children with an unaffected partner (\(hh\)). What fraction of the children are expected to be affected?

This is a \(Hh \times hh\) cross, the same shape as the \(Aa \times aa\) cross from Chapter 7. The affected parent makes \(H\) and \(h\) gametes; the unaffected \(hh\) partner makes only \(h\). The cells are \(Hh, Hh, hh, hh\). Half the children are \(Hh\) and half are \(hh\).

Because the disease allele is dominant, a single copy causes the disease, so every \(Hh\) child is affected. The answer is \(\tfrac{1}{2}\) affected. Notice the difference from a recessive disorder: here an affected child must have an affected parent, because there is no way to hide a dominant allele in an unaffected carrier. Huntington's is also late-onset, meaning symptoms usually appear only in middle age, often after the person has already had children.

Aha: a dominant disorder cannot skip a generation. Every affected person has an affected parent (unless a brand new mutation just occurred). A recessive disorder can skip generations and appear from two unaffected parents, as in Example 1. That single contrast lets you tell the two patterns apart on a pedigree.

Example 3 (an X-linked recessive disorder). Hemophilia is X-linked recessive. A carrier mother (\(X^H X^h\), unaffected because she has one working \(X^H\)) has children with an unaffected father (\(X^H Y\)). What is the chance a son is affected, and a daughter?

This is the sex-linked square from Chapter 12. The mother's eggs are \(X^H\) or \(X^h\). The father's sperm are \(X^H\) or \(Y\). The four cells are \(X^H X^H\) (unaffected daughter), \(X^H X^h\) (unaffected carrier daughter), \(X^H Y\) (unaffected son), and \(X^h Y\) (affected son).

Among the sons, half are \(X^h Y\) and affected, because a son has only one X and it is the disease allele, so he is hemizygous and has no second X to mask it. Among the daughters, none are affected, because every daughter gets a working \(X^H\) from the father. So the disease shows up in about half the sons and no daughters. This is exactly why hemophilia, Duchenne muscular dystrophy, and red-green color blindness appear mostly in males. The real Queen Victoria hemophilia pedigree from Chapter 22 shows this pattern across four generations of European royalty.

Aha: a trait that lands almost entirely in males, passed from a carrier mother, is the signature of X-linked recessive inheritance. There is no father-to-son passing of the allele, because a father gives his sons a Y, not his X. A father passes his single X only to his daughters.
Common pitfalls:
  • Thinking a dominant disorder is common and a recessive one is rare. Dominant means one copy shows the trait, nothing more. It says nothing about frequency. Polydactyly is dominant yet rare, while the recessive cystic fibrosis allele is carried by roughly 1 in 25 people in some populations. Dominant is not the same as common.
  • Drawing a carrier as affected. A carrier of an autosomal recessive disorder is heterozygous and unaffected. Elena (\(Ff\)) does not have cystic fibrosis. On a pedigree a carrier is shown unfilled or half-filled, never fully filled. A fully filled symbol means the person has the disease.
  • Solving a chromosomal disorder with a Punnett square. Down, Edwards, Patau, Klinefelter, and Turner are whole-chromosome conditions, usually from a one-time nondisjunction in meiosis. They are not a dominant or recessive allele, so you diagnose them from a karyotype (counting chromosomes), not from a cross.
  • Forgetting that a female can have an X-linked recessive disorder. It is rare, not impossible. A daughter is affected only if she inherits the disease allele on both X chromosomes (for example, an affected father and a carrier or affected mother). The pattern is "mostly males," not "males only."
  • Mixing up the allele letter. Use a consistent letter for each disease. The letters \(F/f\), \(H/h\), \(X^H/X^h\) in these examples are stand-ins. On a test, define your own letters and keep dominant capital, recessive lowercase.

Connections

  • ← Chapter 20: Mutations: sickle-cell anemia is the worked example of a point mutation from that chapter. A single base change swaps one amino acid in the beta-globin protein (a missense mutation). The disorder in this chapter is the inherited consequence of that molecular change.
  • ← Chapter 21: Chromosomal Disorders and Karyotypes: Down syndrome is trisomy 21, the worked example of aneuploidy from that chapter. Edwards, Patau, Klinefelter, and Turner are read from a karyotype the same way, by counting chromosomes and checking the sex pair.
  • ← Chapter 22: Pedigrees: pedigrees are how you deduce an inheritance pattern for a real family, including the Queen Victoria hemophilia tree. The rules for reading dominant vs recessive and autosomal vs X-linked come straight from that chapter.
  • ← Chapter 7: Punnett Squares and Monohybrid Crosses: the carrier cross in Example 1 is the \(Aa \times Aa\) monohybrid cross, and Example 2 is \(Aa \times aa\). The disorders reuse the grid, they do not need a new tool.
  • ← Chapter 8: Probability in Genetics: once you know a child has a \(\tfrac{1}{4}\) chance of being affected, the product and sum rules answer questions like "chance that two of three children are affected."
  • ← Chapter 12: Sex-Linked Traits: Example 3 is the carrier-mother sex-linked cross. This is why hemophilia, Duchenne muscular dystrophy, and color blindness cluster in males.
  • ↔ Chemistry and biology beyond the event: the frontier topics below (CRISPR, epigenetics, population genetics, genomics) build directly on the molecular and population ideas from across this guide.

★ The frontier this chapter points toward (name only, not tested deeply at Division B)

These are landmarks. Learn the name and one line of what each is, enough to place it on the map. They are not drilled at Division B, and several are the core of the Division C event Designer Genes.

  • CRISPR and genetic engineering: tools that let scientists edit a specific DNA sequence on purpose, for example to correct a disease allele. Genetic engineering is the broader practice of deliberately changing an organism's genome.
  • Epigenetics: heritable changes in how genes are turned on or off without changing the DNA sequence itself, for example chemical tags (methylation) added to DNA.
  • Population genetics and the Hardy-Weinberg principle: the study of allele frequencies across a whole population rather than one family. Its landmark equation, shown once here as a preview and not something to drill in this chapter, is \[ p^2 + 2pq + q^2 = 1, \] where \(p\) and \(q\) are the frequencies of the two alleles, and the three terms are the fractions of the three genotypes. It explains how a recessive disease allele can stay common in a population even though affected people are rare, because most copies are hidden in unaffected carriers.
  • Genomics: the study of an organism's entire genome at once, including sequencing all of its DNA, rather than one gene at a time.
  • Designer Genes (Division C event): the high-school event that extends this material into molecular genetics, gene expression, and biotechnology, including many of the frontier topics above.

Practice

P1 (classify a disorder from a description). A family tree shows a disease that appears only in males. It is never passed from an affected father to his sons, but it can appear in the sons of an unaffected woman whose own father had the disease. Name the inheritance pattern and give one disorder that fits.

AnswerInheritance pattern: X-linked recessive. A disorder that fits: hemophilia, Duchenne muscular dystrophy, or red-green color blindness.

Why it works: read the clues one at a time. "Appears mostly in males" points to an X-linked recessive allele, because a male has one X and is affected by a single copy. "Never father to son" confirms it is on the X, since a father gives his sons a Y and his X only to daughters. "Appears in the sons of an unaffected woman whose father was affected" is the carrier-mother path: that woman inherited the disease allele on the X from her affected father, is an unaffected carrier, and passes it to half her sons. Each clue rules out other patterns: an autosomal disorder would not favor one sex, and a dominant one could not hide in an unaffected carrier.

Pattern (classify by clues, then name the tool): to classify any disorder from a description, run the same checklist. (1) Does it favor one sex? Strongly male means X-linked recessive. (2) Can two unaffected parents have an affected child? Yes means recessive; no, and every affected person has an affected parent, means dominant. (3) Is it a whole extra or missing chromosome? Then it is chromosomal, read from a karyotype, not a cross. Once you have the pattern, the earlier chapter that solves it is fixed: autosomal recessive and dominant use a Punnett square, X-linked uses the sex-linked square or a pedigree, and chromosomal uses a karyotype.

P2 (carrier-cross probability). Tay-Sachs disease is autosomal recessive. Two carriers have children. What fraction of their children are expected to be affected, and what fraction are expected to be unaffected carriers?

AnswerAffected: \(\tfrac{1}{4}\). Unaffected carriers: \(\tfrac{1}{2}\). (Unaffected non-carriers make up the last \(\tfrac{1}{4}\).) This is the same \(Aa \times Aa\) result as the cystic fibrosis cross in Example 1: genotype \(1 : 2 : 1\), and only the homozygous recessive class is affected.

P3 (misconception buster). A student says: "Polydactyly, having an extra finger or toe, is caused by a dominant allele. So most people must have extra fingers, because dominant traits are the common ones." What is wrong with this reasoning?

AnswerThe reasoning is wrong. Polydactyly is dominant, but it is rare, not common. Most people have the usual number of fingers.

Why it works: "dominant" describes only how the allele behaves in a heterozygote. A dominant allele shows its trait even when paired with a recessive allele, so one copy is enough. That is a rule about masking, not about counting. How common a trait is depends on the allele frequency, meaning how many copies of the allele exist in the population, which is a completely separate fact. The polydactyly allele is dominant yet very few people carry it, so the trait is rare. In the other direction, a recessive allele can be common: the cystic fibrosis allele is recessive yet roughly 1 in 25 people in some populations carry it. The Hardy-Weinberg principle in the frontier section is the tool that actually predicts trait frequency from allele frequency, and dominance is not one of its inputs.

Pattern (separate behavior from frequency): whenever a claim links "dominant" to "common" (or "recessive" to "rare"), stop and split the two ideas. Dominance answers "does one copy show?" Frequency answers "how many copies are out there?" They are independent. The clean test cases to remember are a rare dominant (polydactyly, Huntington's) and a common recessive (the cystic fibrosis carrier allele).

Watch / read preview before showing learners

↗ Khan Academy: Genetic disorders and inheritance patterns ▶ Amoeba Sisters: Genetics and pedigrees (YouTube) ↗ Science Olympiad Student Center Wiki: Heredity (the common disorders list)

Teaching notes

Opener: put the classification table on the board with the pattern column blank and ask the class to sort a shuffled list of disorders into the four patterns. This is exactly the tested skill, so start with it rather than lecture.

Board moves: work Example 1 (carrier cross) slowly and make the class name each of the four outcomes: affected, carrier, carrier, non-carrier. Then put Example 2 (dominant) next to it and draw out the one contrast that matters, that a recessive disorder can appear from two unaffected parents but a dominant one cannot skip a generation. Finish with Example 3 so they see the sex-linked square lands the trait in the sons.

What to hammer: the three-question classification checklist from P1 (favors a sex? can two unaffected parents have an affected child? whole chromosome?) and the split between dominance and frequency from P3. These two ideas answer most exam questions on this topic.

The common stumble: students equate dominant with common. Pre-empt it with the polydactyly and cystic fibrosis pair every time the word "dominant" comes up.

Pacing: spend most of the time on the table and Examples 1 and 3, which carry the tested skills. Keep the frontier section to a few minutes: name each landmark, show the Hardy-Weinberg equation once as a preview, and do not drill it. Point strong students toward the Division C Designer Genes event if they want more.

Glossary

Quick definitions, grouped by layer of the concept map.

Foundations & the cell

DNA
Deoxyribonucleic acid: the molecule that stores genetic information as a sequence of four bases (A, T, G, C).
Nucleotide
One building block of DNA or RNA: a phosphate group, a five-carbon sugar, and a nitrogenous base. (A nucleoside is just the sugar plus base, no phosphate.)
Purine / Pyrimidine
Purines (two rings) are adenine and guanine; pyrimidines (one ring) are cytosine, thymine, and (in RNA) uracil.
Base pairing
A pairs with T (2 hydrogen bonds), G pairs with C (3 bonds); in RNA, A pairs with U. Chargaff's rule: %A = %T and %G = %C.
Antiparallel
The two DNA strands run in opposite directions, one 5' to 3' and the other 3' to 5'.
Gene
A segment of DNA that carries the instructions for one protein or trait.
Chromosome
A long DNA molecule wound with proteins; humans have 23 pairs (46 total).
Chromatin
The complex of DNA and histone proteins; loosely packed euchromatin is actively read, tightly packed heterochromatin is not.
Chromatid / Centromere
After replication a chromosome is two identical sister chromatids joined at the centromere.
Diploid / Haploid
Diploid cells (2n) have two of each chromosome; haploid gametes (n) have one set.
Cell cycle
The life cycle of a cell: interphase (G1, S, G2) then mitosis and cytokinesis; DNA is copied in S phase.
Mitosis
Division of a body (somatic) cell into two genetically identical diploid cells (prophase, metaphase, anaphase, telophase).
Meiosis
Division that makes four genetically varied haploid gametes; crossing over and independent assortment create variation.
Crossing over
The swap of matching segments between homologous chromosomes during prophase I, mixing alleles.
Allele
One version of a gene (for example, the taster vs non-taster allele).
Genotype / Phenotype
Genotype is the pair of alleles carried (e.g. Tt); phenotype is the observable trait that results.
Homozygous / Heterozygous
Homozygous = two identical alleles (TT or tt); heterozygous = two different (Tt).
Dominant / Recessive
A dominant allele shows its effect with one copy and masks the recessive; a recessive shows only with two copies.

Classical genetics

Law of Segregation
The two alleles of a gene separate so each gamete carries only one.
Law of Independent Assortment
Alleles of genes on different chromosomes are sorted into gametes independently.
Punnett square
A grid that combines parental gametes to predict offspring genotypes and ratios.
Monohybrid / Dihybrid cross
A cross tracking one gene (phenotype 3:1) or two genes (phenotype 9:3:3:1).
Incomplete dominance
The heterozygote is an intermediate blend (red x white gives pink).
Codominance
Both alleles are fully and separately expressed in the heterozygote (type AB blood; roan coat).
Multiple alleles
A gene with more than two allele versions in the population (ABO: I^A, I^B, i).
Sex-linked (X-linked)
A gene on a sex chromosome; X-linked recessive traits appear more in males, who are hemizygous.
Polygenic inheritance
A trait set by many genes, giving a continuous range (height, skin color).
Pleiotropy
One gene affecting several different traits.
Epistasis
One gene masking or modifying another gene, producing modified ratios (12:3:1, 9:3:4, 15:1, or 9:7).
Lethal allele
An allele whose homozygous (or sometimes heterozygous) genotype causes death, removing a class and skewing ratios.
Carrier
An unaffected heterozygote who carries one recessive disease allele.
Pedigree
A family-tree diagram used to trace a trait and deduce its inheritance pattern.

Molecular genetics

DNA replication
Semi-conservative copying of DNA: each new double helix keeps one old strand and one new strand.
Helicase / Primase / DNA polymerase / Ligase
Replication enzymes: helicase unwinds, primase lays an RNA primer, DNA polymerase extends 5' to 3', ligase joins fragments.
Leading / Lagging strand
The leading strand is copied continuously; the lagging strand is copied in short Okazaki fragments.
RNA
Ribonucleic acid: usually single-stranded, uses ribose sugar and uracil instead of thymine.
mRNA / tRNA / rRNA
Messenger RNA carries the code; transfer RNA brings amino acids; ribosomal RNA builds the ribosome.
Transcription
Copying a gene's DNA into messenger RNA by RNA polymerase (reads template 3' to 5', builds mRNA 5' to 3').
Promoter
A DNA sequence just before a gene where transcription factors and RNA polymerase bind to start transcription.
Intron / Exon
Introns are non-coding sections spliced out of pre-mRNA; exons are kept and joined to make the mature mRNA.
5' cap / poly-A tail
Added to pre-mRNA in eukaryotes to protect it and help the ribosome attach.
Translation
Building a protein from mRNA at the ribosome, reading three-base codons into amino acids.
Codon / Anticodon
A codon is three mRNA bases coding one amino acid; the matching tRNA carries the complementary anticodon.
Genetic code
The mapping of 64 codons to 20 amino acids; it is degenerate (redundant) with wobble in the third base.
Start / Stop codon
AUG (methionine) starts translation; UAA, UAG, UGA stop it and code no amino acid.
Ribosome (A/P/E sites)
The machine that translates mRNA; tRNAs enter at the A site, form a peptide bond at the P site, and leave at the E site.

Mutations, chromosomes & disorders

Point mutation
A single-base change: silent (same amino acid), missense (different amino acid), or nonsense (early stop).
Frameshift
An insertion or deletion not a multiple of 3, which shifts the reading frame and garbles all downstream codons.
Translocation
A chromosomal mutation where segments move between chromosomes (reciprocal = two chromosomes swap).
Karyotype
An organized picture of all 46 chromosomes, used to spot extra, missing, or rearranged chromosomes.
Nondisjunction
Failure of chromosomes to separate in meiosis, producing gametes with too many or too few chromosomes.
Aneuploidy (trisomy / monosomy)
A wrong chromosome count: trisomy = 3 copies (Down 47,+21), monosomy = 1 copy (Turner 45,X).
Sex determination
XX = female, XY = male; the father's sperm (X or Y) sets the sex; any Y makes the individual male.
Autosomal recessive / dominant
Inheritance modes for non-sex-chromosome genes: recessive needs two copies (cystic fibrosis), dominant needs one (Huntington's).

★ Landmarks (name-only)

CRISPR / genetic engineering
Tools that precisely edit DNA sequences.
Epigenetics
Heritable changes in gene activity that do not change the DNA sequence.
Hardy-Weinberg equilibrium
A population-genetics model (p^2 + 2pq + q^2 = 1) for allele and genotype frequencies.
Genomics / Designer Genes
The study of whole genomes; the Division C event Designer Genes extends this Heredity material.
